E1.5.B.1.3. A QUESTION OF COURAGE

E1.5.B.1.3.a. DO YOU HAVE THE COURAGE TO BRING FORTH THE TREASURES THAT ARE HIDDEN WITHIN YOU? 

E1.5.B.1.3.a_REF1_PH DO YOU HAVE THE COURAGE TO BRING FORTH THE TREASURES THAT ARE HIDDEN WITHIN YOU? "This, I believe, is the central question upon which all creative living hinges: Do you have the courage to bring forth the treasures that are hidden within you? […]
Surely something wonderful is sheltered inside you. I say this with all confidence, because I happen to believe we are all walking repositories of buried treasure. I believe this is one of the oldest and most generous tricks the universe plays on us human beings, both for its own amusement and for ours: The universe buries strange jewels deep within us all, and then stands back to see if we can find them.
The hunt to uncover those jewels — that’s creative living.
The courage to go on that hunt in the first place — that’s what separates a mundane existence from a more enchanted one.
The often surprising results of that hunt — that’s what I call Big Magic. [...]
"“We must risk delight,” he [Jack Gilbert] wrote. “We must have the stubbornness to accept our gladness in the ruthless furnace of this world.” […]
He seemed to live in a state of uninterrupted marvel, and he encouraged [his students] to do the same. He didn’t so much teach them how to write poetry, they said, but why: because of delight. Because of stubborn gladness. He told them that they must live their most creative lives as a means of fighting back against the ruthless furnace of this world.
Most of all, though, he asked his students to be brave. Without bravery, he instructed, they would never be able to realize the vaulting scope of their own capacities. Without bravery, they would never know the world as richly as it longs to be known. Without bravery, their lives would remain small — far smaller than they probably wanted their lives to be. [...]
We all know that when courage dies, creativity dies with it. We all know that fear is a desolate boneyard where our dreams go to desiccate in the hot sun. This is common knowledge; sometimes we just don’t know what to do about it. […]
Creativity is a path for the brave, yes, but it is not a path for the fearless, and it’s important to recognize the distinction. […]
If your goal in life is to become fearless, then I believe you’re already on the wrong path, because the only truly fearless people I’ve ever met were straight-up sociopaths and a few exceptionally reckless three-year-olds — and those aren’t good role models for anyone."
(Elizabeth Gilbert, Big Magic: Creative Living Beyond Fear)

E1.5.B.1.3.a_REF1.1_MU BENJAMIN BRITTEN, SYMPHONIC SUITE, Op. 53a, "Gloriana": I. The tournament

Listen on YouTube :
https://youtu.be/5F34urh3lLA

E1.5.B.1.3.b. THE COURAGE TO LOVE LIFE, INCLUDING ITS LOSSES AND SHORTCOMINGS

E1.5.B.1.3.b_REF1_PH "FEARLESSNESS IS WHAT LOVE SEEKS. Love as craving is determined by its goal, and this goal is freedom from fear. [...]
A love that seeks anything safe and disposable on earth is constantly frustrated, because everything is doomed to die. In this frustration love turns about and its object becomes a negation, so that nothing is to be desired except freedom from fear. Such fearlessness exists only in the complete calm that can no longer be shaken by events expected of the future."
(Hannah Arendt,  Love and Saint Augustine)

E1.5.B.1.3.b_REF1.1_PH THE HUMAN CONDITION OF DESIRING TEMPORAL GOODS LEADS TO CONSTANT FEAR OF LOSS AND PREVENTS ENJOYMENT OF THE PRESENT. "So long as we desire temporal things, we are constantly under this threat, and our fear of losing always corresponds to our desire to have. Temporal goods originate and perish independently of man, who is tied to them by his desire. Constantly bound by craving and fear to a future full of uncertainties, we strip each present moment of its calm, its intrinsic import, which we are unable to enjoy. And so, the future destroys the present. [...]
The present is not determined by the future as such… but by certain events which we hope for or fear from the future, and which we accordingly crave and pursue, or shun and avoid. Happiness consists in possession, in having and holding our good, and even more in being sure of not losing it. Sorrow consists in having lost our good and in enduring this loss. However, for Augustine the happiness of having is not contrasted by sorrow but by fear of losing. The trouble with human happiness is that it is constantly beset by fear. It is not the lack of possessing but the safety of possession that is at stake."
(Hannah Arendt,  Love and Saint Augustine, 1929)

E1.5.B.1.3.b_REF2_PH DESIRE IS A COMBINATION OF AIMING AT SPECIFIC GOODS AND REFERRING BACK TO THE INDIVIDUAL'S UNDERSTANDING OF HAPPINESS, guiding the pursuit of what is perceived to make one happy.
"Every craving is tied to a definite object, and it takes this object to spark the craving itself, thus providing an aim for it. Craving is determined by the definitely given thing it seeks, just as a movement is set by the goal toward which it moves. For, as Augustine writes, love is “a kind of motion, and all motion is toward something.” What determines the motion of desire is always previously given. Our craving aims at a world we know; it does not discover anything new. The thing we know and desire is a “good,” otherwise we would not seek it for its own sake. All the goods we desire in our questing love are independent objects, unrelated to other objects. Each of them represents nothing but its isolated goodness. The distinctive trait of this good that we desire is that we do not have it. Once we have the object our desire ends, unless we are threatened with its loss. In that case the desire to have turns into a fear of losing. As a quest for the particular good rather than for things at random, desire is a combination of “aiming at” and “referring back to.” It refers back to the individual who knows the world’s good and evil and seeks to live happily. It is because we know happiness that we want to be happy, and since nothing is more certain than our wanting to be happy, our notion of happiness guides us in determining the respective goods that then became objects of our desires. Craving, or love, is a human being’s possibility of gaining possession of the good that will make him happy, that is, of gaining possession of what is most his own."
(Hannah Arendt,  Love and Saint Augustine)

E1.5.B.1.3.b_REF2.1_PH "    I COULD NOT LOVE EXCEPT WHERE DEATH
    WAS MINGLING HIS WITH BEAUTY’S BREATH...
We are here at the very heart of Edgar Poe's metaphysical drama. Here the motto of his work and life takes on its full meaning:
Strange motto of his twentieth year, which already speaks to the past after so short a life and yet already carries the deep meaning and fidelity of a whole existence.
Thus, to understand Edgar Poe, one must, at all the decisive moments of his poems and tales, make a synthesis of Beauty, Death and Water. This synthesis of Form, Event and Substance may seem artificial and impossible to the philosopher. And yet it is everywhere. If one loves, one immediately admires, fears and guards. In reveries, the three causes that define form, becoming, and matter, merge so well that they are inseparable. A profound dreamer, like Edgar Poe, has united them in one same symbolic force.
This is why water is the element of delicate and faithful death. Water alone can sleep, preserving its beauty; water alone can die, totally still, and yet keep reflecting, reproducing the dreamer's face, faithful to the Great Remembrance, to the One Shadow, water gives beauty to all shadows, it brings all memories to life. Thus is born a kind of delegated and recurrent narcissism that casts beauty on all those we have loved. Man reflects in his past, for him, all images are a memory."
(Gaston Bachelard, Water and Dreams, 1942)

E1.5.B.1.3.b_REF3_PH ““LOVE, BUT BE CAREFUL WHAT YOU LOVE,” the Roman African philosopher Saint Augustine wrote in the final years of the fourth century. We are, in some deep sense, what we love — we become it as much as it becomes us, beckoned from our myriad conscious and unconscious longings, despairs, and patterned desires. And yet there is something profoundly paradoxical about such an appeal to reason in the notion that we can exercise prudence in matters of love — to have loved is to have known the straitjacket of irrationality that slips over even the most willful mind when the heart takes over with its delicious carelessness.

How to heed Augustine’s caution, not by subjugating but by better understanding our experience of love, is what Hannah Arendt explores in her least known but in many ways most beautiful work, Love and Saint Augustine. […]

It was from Augustine that she borrowed the phrase amor mundi — “love of the world” — which would become a defining feature of her philosophy. Occupied by questions of why we succumb to and normalize evil, Arendt identified as the root of tyranny the act of making other human beings irrelevant. Again and again, she returned to Augustine for the antidote: love.”
(Maria Popova, The Marginalian)

“Every craving is tied to a definite object, and it takes this object to spark the craving itself, thus providing an aim for it. Craving is determined by the definitely given thing it seeks, just as a movement is set by the goal toward which it moves. For, as Augustine writes, love is “a kind of motion, and all motion is toward something.” What determines the motion of desire is always previously given. Our craving aims at a world we know; it does not discover anything new. The thing we know and desire is a “good,” otherwise we would not seek it for its own sake. All the goods we desire in our questing love are independent objects, unrelated to other objects. Each of them represents nothing but its isolated goodness. The distinctive trait of this good that we desire is that we do not have it. Once we have the object our desire ends, unless we are threatened with its loss. In that case the desire to have turns into a fear of losing. As a quest for the particular good rather than for things at random, desire is a combination of “aiming at” and “referring back to.” It refers back to the individual who knows the world’s good and evil and seeks to live happily. It is because we know happiness that we want to be happy, and since nothing is more certain than our wanting to be happy, our notion of happiness guides us in determining the respective goods that then became objects of our desires. Craving, or love, is a human being’s possibility of gaining possession of the good that will make him happy, that is, of gaining possession of what is most his own. […]

So long as we desire temporal things, we are constantly under this threat, and our fear of losing always corresponds to our desire to have. Temporal goods originate and perish independently of man, who is tied to them by his desire. Constantly bound by craving and fear to a future full of uncertainties, we strip each present moment of its calm, its intrinsic import, which we are unable to enjoy. And so, the future destroys the present. […]

The present is not determined by the future as such… but by certain events which we hope for or fear from the future, and which we accordingly crave and pursue, or shun and avoid. Happiness consists in possession, in having and holding our good, and even more in being sure of not losing it. Sorrow consists in having lost our good and in enduring this loss. However, for Augustine the happiness of having is not contrasted by sorrow but by fear of losing. The trouble with human happiness is that it is constantly beset by fear. It is not the lack of possessing but the safety of possession that is at stake. […]

In their fear of death, those living fear life itself, a life that is doomed to die… The mode in which life knows and perceives itself is worry. Thus the object of fear comes to be fear itself. Even if we should assume that there is nothing to fear, that death is no evil, the fact of fear (that all living things shun death) remains. […]

Fearlessness is what love seeks. Love as craving is determined by its goal, and this goal is freedom from fear. […]

A love that seeks anything safe and disposable on earth is constantly frustrated, because everything is doomed to die. In this frustration love turns about and its object becomes a negation, so that nothing is to be desired except freedom from fear. Such fearlessness exists only in the complete calm that can no longer be shaken by events expected of the future. […]

Even if things should last, human life does not. We lose it daily. As we live the years pass through us and they wear us out into nothingness. It seems that only the present is real, for “things past and things to come are not”; but how can the present (which I cannot measure) be real since it has no “space”? Life is always either no more or not yet. Like time, life “comes from what is not yet, passes through what is without space, and disappears into what is no longer.” Can life be said to exist at all? Still the fact is that man does measure time. Perhaps man possesses a “space” where time can be conserved long enough to be measured, and would not this “space,” which man carries with himself, transcend both life and time?

Time exists only insofar as it can be measured, and the yardstick by which we measure it is space. […]

Memory, the storehouse of time, is the presence of the “no more” (iam non) as expectation is the presence of the “not yet” (nondum). Therefore, I do not measure what is no more, but something in my memory that remains fixed in it. It is only by calling past and future into the present of remembrance and expectation that time exists at all. Hence the only valid tense is the present, the Now. […]

The Now is what measures time backwards and forwards, because the Now, strictly speaking, is not time but outside time. In the Now, past and future meet. For a fleeting moment they are simultaneous so that they can be stored up by memory, which remembers things past and holds the expectation of things to come. For a fleeting moment (the temporal Now) it is as though time stands still, and it is this Now that becomes Augustine’s model of eternity. […]

Who will hold [the heart], and fix it so that it may stand still for a little while and catch for a moment the splendor of eternity which stands still forever, and compare this with temporal moments that never stand still, and see that it is incomparable… but that all this while in the eternal, nothing passes but the whole is present. […]

What prevents man from “living” in the timeless present is life itself, which never “stands still.” The good for which love craves lies beyond all mere desires. If it were merely a question of desiring, all desires would end in fear. And since whatever confronts life from the outside as the object of its craving is sought for life’s sake (a life we are going to lose), the ultimate object of all desires is life itself. Life is the good we ought to seek, namely true life. […]

Desire mediates between subject and object, and it annihilates the distance between them by transforming the subject into a lover and the object into the beloved. For the lover is never isolated from what he loves; he belongs to it… Since man is not self-sufficient and therefore always desires something outside himself, the question of who he is can only be resolved by the object of his desire and not, as the Stoics thought, by the suppression of the impulse of desire itself: “Such is each as is his love” [Augustine wrote]. Strictly speaking, he who does not love and desire at all is a nobody. […]

Man as such, his essence, cannot be defined because he always desires to belong to something outside himself and changes accordingly… If he could be said to have an essential nature at all, it would be lack of self-sufficiency. Hence, he is driven to break out of his isolation by means of love… for happiness, which is the reversal of isolation, more is required than mere belonging. Happiness is achieved only when the beloved becomes a permanently inherent element of one’s own being. […]
(Hannah Arendt, Love and Saint Augustine, 1929)

E1.5.B.1.3.c. FEAR IS A FLIGHT FROM TIMELINESS. NOT KNOWING HOW TO SEIZE THE MOMENT, TO LIVE IN THE MOMENT, IS ANOTHER NAME FOR LACK OF COURAGE.

E1.5.B.1.3.c_REF1_PH "COURAGE, MASTERPIECE OF TIMING
Fear remains an escape from adequate timing. Not knowing how to seize the moment, how to live in the present, that's another name for lack of courage. To be constantly deported to a past or a future, to have no sense of time, only of time running out and of nothing ending or happening. Conversely, "living with a sense of time" becomes the art of continuous rebirth. "When I dance, I dance; when I sleep, I sleep; [...] it's an injustice to corrupt one's own rules. [Have you been able to meditate and manage your life? You've done the greatest work of all. It's a glorious masterpiece to put an end to fear, to come to terms with active inadequacy and the lack that comes. It's not about accepting inertia and its wanderings. "Our task is to compose our morality, not to write books and win, not to win battles and provinces, but to bring order and tranquility to our conduct. Our great and glorious masterpiece is to live properly (2). It's about active wandering, making it a humanizing initiation. 
To "behave well and properly as a man". To which Erasmus would reply that one is not born a human being, one becomes one, and the greatness of humanity comes from the courage of the right moment: "There is nothing so beautiful and legitimate as to behave well and properly as a man, nor science so laborious as to know how to live this life well and naturally; and our wildest disease is to despise our being (3)". The Renaissanceists have this sense of communal life and enjoyment, this sense from before the split. The twentieth century marked the end of convergences, the end of the apparent alliance between progress and reason, morality and science, ethics and technology. [...]
Notes:
(2) Montaigne, Les Essais, III, 13, "L'art de vivre". 
(3) Ibid.
(Cynthia Fleury, The End of Courage, 2010)

E1.5.B.1.3.c_REF2_LIT "I WANT TO WRITE DOWN EVERYTHING I KNOW ABOUT BEING AFRAID, but I’d probably never have enough time to write anything else. Afraid is a country where they issue us passports at birth and hope we never seek citizenship in any other country. The face of afraid keeps changing constantly, and I can count on that change. I need to travel light and fast, and there’s a lot of baggage I’m going to have to leave behind me. Jettison cargo."
(Audre Lorde, A Burst of Light and Other Essays)

E1.5.B.1.3.c_REF3_PH MISTAKES AND PAINFUL EXPERIENCES IN OUR YOUTH ULTIMATELY GIVE LIFE MEANING AND OFTEN LEAD TO A HAPPY LIFE IN LATER YEARS
"I'm sixteen and have just recently gotten really into your work. I was wondering: what advice would you give to your sixteen year old self and why?
TAYA, LOWER SACKVILLE, CANADA

Dear Taya,

This is an excellent question and I am often asked it, but I find it a very difficult question to answer. 

The difficulty is that I am, in fact, my sixteen-year-old self, just half a century older. Not only that but, sitting here at the kitchen table, with the sun streaming through the window, answering your excellent question, my older self, the current destination of my younger self, is mostly happy.

However, my older self is protective and instinctively wants to warn my sixteen-year-old self not to make the mistakes that I know from experience are going to cause him pain. But, you see, this older and more experienced self also knows that the painful things are often the things that ultimately give substance and meaning to life. My older self knows that life’s mistakes are destiny’s way of laying the tracks that will bring my younger self to the place where I am at this very moment — the mostly happy place, where I sit, with the sun coming through the window, writing an answer to your excellent question. 

Have a great life, Taya, it’s all ahead of you.

Love, Nick"
(Nick Cave, The Red Hand Files, ISSUE #176 / DECEMBER 2021)

Read it on The Red Hand Files: https://www.theredhandfiles.com/do-you-pray-with-your-family-and-maybe-friends-hopeing-that-sometimes-someone-leesen-your-despered-litle-iner-voice-to-get-answer-or-gidence-ephifany-in-this-cruel-world-which-most-of/

E1.5.B.1.3.d. HOW TO BREAK FREE FROM YOUR OWN PRISONS?

E1.5.B.1.3.d_REF1_CUR "MY WORK « I WALK DOWN ANOTHER STREET » IS ABOUT THE COURAGE and « the night vision » that has been required to get out of a deep hole and the insights that actually make you take a different path, inspired by Portia Nelson's poem « There's a Hole in My Sidewalk »."
(Louise Swardshammar)

"Chapter I
I walk down the street.
There is a deep hole in the sidewalk.
I fall in.
I am lost… I am helpless.
It isn’t my fault.
It takes forever to find a way out.

Chapter II
I walk down the same street.
There is a deep hole in the sidewalk.
I pretend I don’t see it.
I fall in again.
I can’t believe I am in the same place.
But it isn’t my fault.
It still takes a long time to get out.

Chapter III
I walk down the same street.
There is a deep hole in the sidewalk.
I see it is there.
I still fall in… it’s a habit.
My eyes are open.
I know where I am.
It is my fault… I get out immediately.

Chapter IV
I walk down the same street.
There is a deep hole in the sidewalk.
I walk around it.

Chapter V
I walk down another street."
(Portia Nelson, There's a Hole in My Sidewalk)

E1.5.B.1.3.d_REF2_PO "NO ONE STRIPS NAKED BEFORE THEY GET DRESSED.
How many feathers do I possess and which of those feathers do I assume?
Become who you are - said the philosopher
How can I become who I am if I do not assume my own shadows?
Look at me, I admit, I am also narcissic! My greatest fear is also my greatest desire.
I want to be seen, I want to be loved, contemplated, praised!
Here I am! Look at me!
It's in the act of assuming myself that I recognize myself
It's in the act of assuming myself that
I see myself
It's in the act of assuming myself that I become familiar with myself and my feathers
When I take on my own desires I can realise that I no longer need them 
No one can take off the mask they don't assume to be wearing
It is when I assume my own feathers that I can finally pluck myself.
I transit between repression and acceptance
To finally be able to be naked, to finally be authentic, to finally be free, including from my own prisons".
(Jéssica Miranda)

E1.5.B.1.3.d_REF3_CUR "FIST WAS ONE OF DONATI'S WORKS INCLUDED IN THE POSTWAR EXHIBITION DEDICATED TO MYTH AND MAGIC - Le Surréalisme en 1947 at the Galerie Maeght in Paris. Fist consists of a hand clenching two fishy eyes which together create an uncanny facial expression. The work alludes to the evil eye. In « Le Surréalisme » it was in a gallery dedicated to superstition, where the viewers were meant to conceptually confront and ward off their fears."
(Curatorship: Peggy Guggenheim, Venice)

E1.5.B.1.3.d_REF4_CUR Dans "MY EPIDEMIC" LILI REYNAUD DEWAR RÉFLÉCHIT À CE QUI S'EST JOUÉ PENDANT LA CRISE DU SIDA, même si son pic des années 80 est passé. Une épidémie est forcément collective mais avec le sida les rapports intimes des malades sont exposés. Dans le chapitre "Small blood Opera" présenté à la Biénnale de Venise de 2015, Lili Reynaud Dewar réfléchit sur la peur et le rejet dont sont l'objet les personnes atteintes du sida. 
Dans cette installation, Lili Reynaud Dewar nous présente des vidéos dans lesquelles elle danse nue, reproduisant des chorégraphies de Joséphine Baker dans un lieu publique où l'on comprend qu'elle ne devrait pas se livrer à cette activité. L'artiste a fait le choix de Josephine Baker en partie pour se libérer du traumatisme de la danse classique qu'elle avait vécu enfant, mais surtout parce que Baker a démontré un engagement politique puisqu'elle a quitté les US pour venir en France afin de ne plus être une danseuse dans des spectacle réservés à un public spécifique à cause de la ségrégation. On a pu voir Joséphine Baker comme une femme objectivée, mais on peut aussi penser qu'elle retournait vers le spectateur le regard raciste dont elle faisait l'objet - par l'intermédiaire de la danse. Par son travail riche en couches de significations superposée, Lili Reynaud Dewar nous invite à nous libérer de nos peurs par l'action et la réflexion.

Écouter l'entretien avec Lili Reynaud Dewar: https://youtu.be/IY1-LITK2mk

E1.5.B.1.3.e. HOW TO OVERCOME EXCESSIVE SHYNESS?

E1.5.B.1.3.e_REF1_LIT “I HAVE, HEAVEN FORGIVE ME, A PASSION FOR BEING LIKED — I will not say for being admired, for I am not exacting about that — by every child of Adam I meet! The bottom of it is, I suppose, simply Fear. I must resemble a cowardly dog in my conviction that the most negligible person, any sort of a poor devil, could be terribly dangerous. What I expect them to do to me I am sure I don’t know, but when I meet their eyes—the eye of a peddler, of an organ-grinder, of a grocer’s assistant, of a waiter, of a cab-driver, of the forlornest beggar—I feel as if I could not flatter this ferocious and terrifying being humbly enough, or get away quickly enough! Yes that is what it is. It is just fear. My life would have been different at every point, not only at school but long afterwards, now in fact, if I only had the power to stand up to people and speak out, or strike out, boldly. [...] If only I could have got angry! But I couldn’t. I have a long memory for insults, but I cannot flare up and hit back! It is this combination of a cold and even deadly analytical judgment with a perfect terror of scenes, contradictions, clashes, man-to-man challenges, that does the harm."
(John Cowper Powys, Autobiography, 1934, Faber and Faber, p.142-144)

E1.5.B.1.3.g. WAR: AN "OPPORTUNITY" TO EXERCISE THE VIRTUE OF COURAGE

E1.5.B.1.3.g_REF1_PH COURAGEOUS INDIVIDUALS FACE DEATH AND WOUNDS RELUCTANTLY BUT ENDURE THEM FOR A NOBLE CAUSE, despite the pain and the significant loss of life's greatest goods.
"Courage is in itself a painful thing, and it is rightly the object of our praise, because it is more difficult to endure sorrows than to abstain from pleasures. Not that we should think that the end which courage proposes to itself is not a pleasant thing; only, it is obscured by the circumstances which accompany it, as it also occurs in the gymnasium's competitions: the end for which the pugilists are fighting is pleasant, they fight for the crown and honors, whereas the blows which they receive are for beings of flesh, a dire and painful thing, as is the whole of their harsh training. And all these efforts - because of their extent - make the final object appear as insignificant and unpleasant. If then the end concerning courage is of the same order, death and wounds will be painful to the courageous man, who will suffer them reluctantly; he will nevertheless endure them, because it is noble to do so, or it is shameful to shirk them. And the more complete the virtue he possesses and the greater his happiness, the more painful will be the thought of death: for it is for such a man that life is especially worth living, it is he whom death will deprive of the greatest goods, and he is fully aware of this: all this is not without affliction. But he is no less brave, perhaps even more so, because he prefers the noble labors of war to these great goods of which we speak."
(Aristotle, The Nicomachean Ethics)

E1.5.B.1.4_REF1_LIT "IT IS A MELANCHOLY CONSIDERATION, THAT SO MUCH OF OUR TIME IS NECESSARILY TO BE SPENT UPON THE CARE OF LIVING, and that we can seldom obtain ease in one respect but by resigning it in another ; yet I suppose we are by this dispensation not less happy in the whole, than if the spontaneous bounty of Nature poured all that we want into our hands. "Marc Weis and Martin di Mabbia, known as M+M, have created a sinister 3D horror movie in miniature format. The plot in short goes as follows: a tenant feels threatened by the outside world. When an uninvited guest is trying to come close to her, the film ends in a shocking manner. The piece is reminiscent of Roman Polanski's Apartment trilogy and conveys several amazing references and actions. This is surprising in the sense that the protagonists of the vastly symbolic frames are praying mantises. The work's setting - made without any computer animations - is a doll's house. However, the movie is far from cute.
The surrealists had also been fascinated by these anthropomorphic mantises, predominantly remaining in a somewhat statuesque rigidity as well as the myth of the love act, lethal for the males. Shot in 3D - typical for M+M - and presented as a space filling video installation, the instinct driven protagonists appear to be moving closely to the viewer in their physical presence [augmented by the 3D glasses]."
(Cora Waschke)

E1.5.B.1.5. RELIGIONS THAT DO MORE HARM THAN GOOD

E1.5.B.1.5_REF1_PH HANNAH ARENDT, UNDERLINES THAT THE DEVELOPMENT OF CHRISTIANISM HAS DEFORMED THE DOCTRINE OF JESUS WITH FUTURE CHASTIMATES AND FEAR, making the "good news" of the Gospels a source of fear rather than joy.
"Perhaps nothing in the whole development of Christianity through the centuries is more contrary or more alien to the letter and spirit of the teaching of Jesus of Nazareth than this detailed catalogue of future punishments and this enormous power of coercion through fear, which only in the last stages of the modern age has lost its political and public importance. From the point of view of religious thought, it is certainly a terrible irony that the "good news" of the Gospels, that "life is eternal," could subsequently lead not to an increase in joy but to an increase in fear on earth, and could make man's death not easier but more painful. "
(Hannah Arendt, The Cultural Crisis, 1954)

1.5.B.1.6. TO WHAT EXTENT IS THE SENSATION OF FREEDOM NECESSARY TO HAPPINESS?

E1.5.B.1.6_REF1_LIT "WHAT I CALL HAPPINESS IS, IN THE END, NOTHING MORE THAN THE INTENSE FEELING OF MY FREEDOM. 
I can go, I'm happy."

E1.5.B.1.6_REF2_SOC THE RELATIONSHIP BETWEEN SENSE OF FREEDOM AND LIFE SATISFACTION
A particular channel through which social environment may affect happiness is freedom: the society we live in may crucially affect the availability of options that we have to shape our own life.
The visualization below shows the relationship between self-reported sense of freedom and self-reported life satisfaction using data from the Gallup World Poll. The variable measuring life satisfaction corresponds to country-level averages of survey responses to the Cantril Ladder question (a 0-10 scale, where 10 is the highest level of life satisfaction); while the variable measuring freedom corresponds to the share of people who agree with the statement “In this country, I am satisfied with my freedom to choose what I do with my life”.
As we can see, there is a clear positive relationship: countries where people feel free to choose and control their lives tend to be countries where people are happier. As Inglehart et al. (2008) show, this positive relationship holds even after we control for other factors, such as incomes and strength of religiosity.
Interestingly, this chart also shows that while there are some countries where the perceived sense of freedom is high but average life satisfaction is low (e.g. Rwanda); there are no countries where the perceived sense of freedom is low but average life satisfaction is high (i.e. there are no countries in the top left area of the chart).
To our knowledge there are no rigorous studies exploring the causal mechanisms linking freedom and happiness. However, it seems natural to expect that self-determination and absence of coercion are important components of what people consider a happy and meaningful life."
(Updated happiness and life satisfaction world data)

Read the full article:
https://ourworldindata.org/happiness-and-life-satisfaction#the-relationship-between-sense-of-freedom-and-life-satisfaction

(Friedrich Nietzsche, Human All Too Human II, Aphorism 311, 1879)

E1.5.B.3. THE FATIGUE of being what you want to be is a "good" fatigue. The other fatigue would be the petty demon of everyday life [...] - the hassles that prevent us from being what we would like to be. It is also the fatigue that prevents us from being present to the world - this inability to embrace every moment weighs on us.

E1.5.B.3.1. ODE TO FATIGUE
The fatigue experienced by someone who is as they desired to be is considered "good" fatigue. Alternatively, the other type of fatigue acts as a petty demon of everyday life. This fatigue involves the hassles that prevent us from becoming what we aspire to be. It also prevents us from being present in the world, and this inability to fully engage in every moment weighs heavily on us.

E1.5.B.3.1_REF1_PH HE WHO IS AS HE WISHED TO BE ENJOYS 'GOOD' FATIGUE 
"Ode to fatigue
Parodying La Fontaine's Les animaux malades de la peste (The Sick Animals of the Plague), and substituting fatigue for the plague, we could say of fatigue that it is now present among men as "an evil that spreads terror, [fatigue], if it must be called by its name, makes war on men, not all of whom die, but all of whom are afflicted. [...] no food aroused their envy, neither wolf nor fox spied on the sweet and innocent prey, the turtle doves fled from each other, no more love given, no more joy".
But the tiredness of the one who is as he wanted to be is a 'good' tiredness. The other kind of tiredness would be the petty demon of everyday life (the phrase comes from Jean Giono) - the annoyances that prevent us from being what we would like to be - but doesn't it also reveal our human condition? Existential fatigue? This tiredness is like a spider's web that catches us without ever facing us: it is treacherous. Like the Lilliputians who immobilise Gulliver by appearing everywhere. Like quicksand, in which the more you stir, the deeper you sink - and from which you can't get out on your own".
(Eric Fiat in Les Chemins de la Philosophie , broadcast on France Culture on 20/12/2017)

Listen to the podcast :
https://www.franceculture.fr/emissions/les-chemins-de-la-philosophie/la-fatigue-24-ode-a-la-fatigue

E1.5.B.3.2. FATIGUE MAY BE THE REMORSE OF HOURS BADLY SPENT
Could existential fatigue be the memory of hours that are dead because they are empty, those hours of life that we haven't used as an hour of thought or an hour of sympathy? The greater our degree of presence in the world, the less tired we are?

E1.5.B.3.2_REF1_PH “THE AFFECTIVE CHARACTER OF DURATION-THE JOY OR PAIN OF BEING - derives from the proportion or disproportion of living hours used as time for thought, or time for feeling.  Matter neglects being, life neglects living, and the heart neglects loving. It  is  in slumber that we lose paradise. But let us delve more deeply into the scenario of our idleness: an atom radiates with frequency, and hence exists by using a great number,  though never all,  of its instants. A living  cell is already  more sparing in its efforts, using a  mere fraction of the temporal possibilities furnished by the ensemble of atoms that constitute it. As for thought, it is by irregular flashes that it utilizes life. Three types of filters through which all too few instants can emerge  into con-sciousness!  And so the shadow of mute suffering stalks us whenever we go in search of lost instants. Yes, we recall those rich hours marked by the endless pealing of Easter bells,  those bells of resurrection whose strokes we never count because they all count, because they each give rise to an echo in our awakened soul.  But this joyful remembrance readily turns into remorse as soon as we compare those hours of full life to the slow intellectual hours, relatively sparse as they are;  to the dead empty hours, so empty of purpose as Carlyle would lament; to the unending hostile hours, as they yield nothing. 
And so we dream of  a divine hour that would bestow everything. Not the replete hour, but the complete hour. The  hour where all instants of time would be utilized by matter, the  hour where all instants realized by matter would be utilized by life, the hour where all living instants would be felt, loved, thought-hence, the hour where the relativity of consciousness would fade away, for consciousness would now be commensurate with the fullness of time. 
Ultimately, objective time is maximal time-that time which, contain¬ing each  and every instant, is composed of the richly dense ensemble of the Creator's acts.”
(Gaston Bachelard, Intuition of the instant, 1931)

E1.5.B.3.2_REF2_PO "I AM GRATEFUL TO THE POET IN US FASCINATED BY A SIMPLE WAVE,
to this residual part which still resembles us at the end of our fatigues and our lost days,
to this part that we would like to believe to be as irreducible as it is rebellious to the injunction of fashion,
rebel to the dreams that are chartered in order to lose us 
and which makes us look for words to try among the crowd 
to wake up in each of us the poet who was silenced.
(Michel Baglin, Alcohol of the Winds, 2010)

E1.5.B.3.3. THE EXHAUSTION OF BALANCING COMPETING PARTS OF OURSELVES
Can we escape the work-life, self, relationship balance cultural trap? Do we have to make such a dreadfull choice - between working OR being alive? Isn't it rather that "we are collectively exhausted because of our inability to hold competing parts of ourselves together in a more integrated way [...] To neglect any one of the three marriages is to impoverish them all, because they are not actually separate commitments but different expressions of the way each individual belongs to the world."
When work is seen as the ransom of existence, rather than one's "due" (Rodin's expression), one experiences existential fatigue.

E1.5.B.3.3_REF1_PH "THE EQUILIBRIUM BETWEEN PRODUCTIVITY AND PRESENCE IS ONE OF THE HARDEST THINGS TO MASTER IN LIFE, and one of the most important. We, both as a culture and as individuals, often conflate it with the deceptively similar-sounding yet profoundly different notion of “work/life balance” — a concept rather disheartening upon closer inspection. It implies, after all, that we must counter the downside — that which we must endure in order to make a living — with the upside — that which we long to do in order to feel alive. It implies allocating half of our waking hours to something we begrudge while anxiously awaiting the other half to arrive so we can live already. What a woefully shortchanging way to exist — lest we forget, so speaks Annie Dillard: “How we spend our days is, of course, how we spend our lives.”
In The Three Marriages: Reimagining Work, Self and Relationship (public library) — a book reminiscent of Parker Palmer’s beautiful Thoreau-esque writings on the art of inner wholeness yet wholly revelatory in its own right — English poet and philosopher David Whyte aptly calls “work/life balance” a “phrase that often becomes a lash with which we punish ourselves” and offers an emboldening way out of this cultural trap. In an immensely insightful inquiry into these three primary commitments we all make in life, consciously or not, and the dynamics common to them all — the sequence of recognition, pursuit, disappointment, and recommitment — he explores “all those strange and inexplicable inner ways we belong to ourselves.”
In a sentiment reminiscent of Emerson — who observed in his reflections on how we grow: “People wish to be settled; only as far as they are unsettled is there any hope for them.” — Whyte turns a skeptical eye toward the concept of “balance” between work and life. [...]
And yet the most challenging marriage of all is the invisible, private one behind the two public-facing commitments of work and relationship — “the internal and often secret marriage to that tricky movable frontier called ourselves.” While poets and psychologists agree that the self is a fluid phenomenon and there is no such thing as fixed personality, we still cling to the comforting falsehood that the self is a stable foundation. And yet in its very instability, Whyte suggests, lies its promise of satisfaction — the self “moves and changes and surprises us as much as anything in the outer world to which it wants to commit,” and that outer world invariably shapes our inner experience. "
(Maria Popova, Brain Pickings)

E1.5.B.3.3_REF1.1_PH "POETS HAVE NEVER USED THE WORD BALANCE, FOR GOOD REASON. First of all, it is too obvious and therefore untrustworthy; it is also a deadly boring concept and seems to speak as much to being stuck and immovable, as much as to harmony. There is also the sense of unbalancing that must take place in order to push a person into a new and larger set of circumstances. […]
The current understanding of work-life balance is too simplistic. People find it hard to balance work with family, family with self, because it might not be a question of balance. Some other dynamic is in play, something to do with a very human attempt at happiness that does not quantify different parts of life and then set them against one another. We are collectively exhausted because of our inability to hold competing parts of ourselves together in a more integrated way." 
(David Whyte, The Three Marriages: Reimagining Work, Self and Relationship)

E1.5.B.3.3_REF1.1.a_PH “LIFE IS SIMPLE BUT NOT EASY. The problem is we want life to be easy, which makes it complicated.”
(FS Street, Brain food)

E1.5.B.3.3_REF1.2_PH "HUMAN BEINGS ARE CREATURES OF BELONGING, though they may come to that sense of belonging only through long periods of exile and loneliness. Interestingly, we belong to life as much through our sense that it is all impossible, as we do through the sense that we will accomplish everything we have set out to do. This sense of belonging and not belonging is lived out by most people through three principal dynamics: first, through relationship to other people and other living things (particularly and very personally, to one other living, breathing person in relationship or marriage); second, through work; and third, through an understanding of what it means to be themselves, discrete individuals alive and seemingly separate from everyone and everything else."
(David Whyte, The Three Marriages: Reimagining Work, Self and Relationship)

E1.5.B.3.3_REF1.2.a_PH "THESE ARE THE THREE MARRIAGES: WORK, SELF AND OTHER. […]
We can call these three separate commitments marriages because at their core they are usually lifelong commitments and … they involve vows made either consciously or unconsciously… To neglect any one of the three marriages is to impoverish them all, because they are not actually separate commitments but different expressions of the way each individual belongs to the world. [...]
We should stop thinking in terms of work-life balance. Work-life balance is a concept that has us simply lashing ourselves on the back and working too hard in each of the three commitments. In the ensuing exhaustion we ultimately give up on one or more of them to gain an easier life. [...]
We can still make a real life even when crowded by other identities, or even when unbalanced and intoxicated with desire, or even when we are disappointed in work or love, and perhaps the way, at the center of all this deep love of belonging and this deep exhaustion of belonging, we may have waiting for us, at the end of the tunnel, a marriage of marriages, a life worth living, and one we can call, despite all the difficulties and imperfections, our very own. [...]
We are each a river with a particular abiding character, but we show radically different aspects of our self according to the territory through which we travel. […]
Neglecting this internal marriage, we can easily make ourselves a hostage to the externals of work and the demands of relationship. We find ourselves unable to move in these outer marriages because we have no inner foundation from which to step out with a firm persuasion. It is as if, absent a loving relationship with this inner representation of our self, we fling ourselves in all directions in our outer lives, looking for love in all the wrong places."
(David Whyte, The Three Marriages: Reimagining Work, Self and Relationship)

E1.5.B.3.3_REF2_PH "IN SHORT, I AM CONVINCED, BOTH BY FAITH AND EXPERIENCE, THAT TO MAINTAIN ONE’S SELF ON THIS EARTH IS NOT A HARDSHIP BUT A PASTIME, if we will live simply and wisely; as the pursuits of the simpler nations are still the sports of the more artificial. It is not necessary that a man should earn his living by the sweat of his brow, unless he sweats easier than I do."
(Henry David Thoreau, Walden, 1854)

E1.5.B.3.1_REF1_PH.wE1.5.B.3.3

E1.5.B.3.4. ACEDIA: WHEN WE LOSE OUR TASTE FOR EXERCISE AND FATIGUE TAKES OVER.
"Burn-out" is not a new concept; it was known as acedia in the Middle Ages, especially among monks. Acedia translates into weariness, slackness, and sometimes hyperactivity without being present in one's actions - this is professional burn-out. Acedia differs from boredom in that boredom is a state of waiting without knowing what for, and it discredits everything that arises. In contrast, acedia no longer holds any expectations.

E1.5.B.3.4_REF1_PH "AKÈDIA
Acedia

Feeling, state of a monk who disinvests from asceticism, who can no longer invest in it (different from losing faith). It's not a loss of faith, but a loss of involvement. State of depression: moodiness, weariness, sadness, boredom, discouragement. (Spiritual) life seems monotonous, aimless, tedious and meaningless: the ascetic ideal is obscured, without attraction. Cassian (Institutions): [...] what the Greeks called akedia, and which we may call boredom or anxiety of the heart (taedium sive anxietas cordis). This phenomenon often appears in the stories of Eastern eremitism. [...]

It's a state (of degradation through blockage) closer to aphanisis (Jones' term: state of non-desire, fear of non-desire) than to castration (fear of castration). [...]

A process well described by Robinson, or rather the sailor Selkirk: "But these appetites [needs] were satisfied, the desire for companionship gnawed at him just as much, and it seemed to him that he was the least needy when everything was lacking; for what was necessary for the support of the body he could easily acquire, while the ardent desire to see a human face again, which arose in the interval between the devouring bodily appetites, was scarcely bearable. He became dull, languid, sad, and could hardly restrain himself from doing violence to himself [...]".

I give these references [...] to Robinson to suggest that acedia is not exclusively associated with the monastic state. We are not monks, but we are interested in acedia. This is precisely because it is typically associated with asceticism, i.e. the practice (in the etymological sense) of a way of life. Acedia is not a belief, an idea, an option of belief (acedia is not a doubt), but the renunciation of a way of life. Acedia: a repeated, widespread, insistent moment when we've had enough of our way of life, our relationship with the world (the mundane). I can wake up one morning and see my week's programme unfolding before me without hope. It repeats itself, it goes round and round: the same tasks, the same appointments, and yet there's no involvement, even if each part of the programme is bearable, sometimes even pleasant. [...]

What is the relationship between acedia and the concept of living together? Historically, a concept linked above all to eremitic asceticism: painful withdrawal from the asceticism of solitude → return of the hermit to the world. Cenobitism: probably partly conceived as a means of combating acedia by integrating the monk into a strong community structure. Acedia (modern): when one can no longer invest in others, in living with others, without being able to invest in solitude. → The waste of everything, without even a place for this waste: waste without a dustbin".
(Roland Barthes, How to Live Together, course at the Collège de France, 1976-1977)

E1.6.B.3.7_REF2_PH.wE1.5.B.3.4_REF1_PH

E1.5.B.3.4_REF2_PO "BREVITY

Why are you so withdrawn? don't you love, as before,
 don't you now no longer love the song? you were fond of it though as a youth,
 In the days of hope,
When you sang without end!"
As is my happiness, so is my song. - Will you bathe in the sunset
It is over, and the earth is cold,
And the night bird flutters
 Uncomfortably before your eyes."
(Friedrich Hölderlin)

E1.5.B.3.4_REF3_PH “BURNOUT FEELS LIKE A LIVING CENTRAL ABSENCE, NOT ONLY OF A CENTRE, BUT THE SOURCES THAT USED TO RISE FROM THAT CENTRE.
The exhaustion of burnout always recalls a previously felt internal fire, one from which our unquenchable energies once emerged. Burnout denotes a kind of amnesia: not only in the forgetting of our very personal priorities but the inability to locate a source inside us that previously seemed to run through all the seasons of our life. This loss of a fiery essential centre is also experienced as a loss of faith: a form of forgetting, not only that the source actually existed inside me in the first place but that I might not now ever remember how to drink from it again. […]
Burnout always involves a loss of the timeless and therefore of the ability to rest. Burnout, in a very profound way, is a loss of friendship with time itself… the experience of feeling continually out of season… In the loss of faith in existence itself, we refuse, in a kind of symmetrical sympathy, to fully exist ourselves. Being out of season with the outside world means we also miss our own inner, creative, tidal comings and goings. […]
Burnout calls for creative breakdown, either in submitting to unconscious self-sabotage, the way that disasters large and small seem to track our exhausted burned-out self on a daily basis, the way we actually create those disasters unknowingly ourselves, trying to make a break for freedom or to create a conscious creative breakdown. Burnout is often as much the resistance to making these changes as being worn down by what we cannot seem to change: all the ways I find it impossible to leave the job, or leave the relationship; all the ways I find it impossible to change my approach to work, or all the ways I need to simply learn to love again must be looked at and allowed to break down and fall away. […]
Burnout fully realised is also the decisive, exhausted moment in which we realise we cannot go on in the same way.
Not being able to go on, is always in the end, a creative act, the threshold moment of our transformation away from physical exhaustion. Not being able to go on is the beginning of a proper relationship with the timeless and the healing possibilities of timelessness: healing ourselves from burnout always involves a reacquaintance with the eternal: my ability to experience the timeless is a parallel to my ability to rest. […]
The foundation from which we transform the experience of burnout is always the realisation that we have been measuring all the wrong things in all the wrong ways and that we have for too long, mis-measured our sense of self in the same way; that we have allowed the shallow rewards of false goals or false people to mesmerise, bedazzle and entrain us: to hide from us an ancient and abiding human dynamic — that we belong to something greater and even better for us than the realm of the measured.”
(David Whyte, Consolations II)

E1.5.B.3.5. FATIGUE: A MISSED OPPORTUNITY FOR WONDER AND ECSTASY IN THE FACE OF REALITY'S STRANGENESS.
For some, fatigue signifies a surrender to the body, a testament to their encounter with reality - they allow their bodies to fully absorb their existence. For others, like Jean-Paul Sartre's character Roquentin, who is repulsed by reality, fatigue is a sign of their rejection of reality. They view such bodily surrender as complacency - they resist the inherent state of existence (what is) to recreate themselves in an existentialist manner.

E1.5.B.3.5_REF1_PH "INTELLIGENCE SHOWS ITS COURAGE BY GRASPING ALTERNATIVES. There are other ways to be right. A thousand other ways that lead elsewhere. And this claim to the elsewhere and the alternative remains, in the final analysis, the prerogative of gifted rationalities. 
Moreover, behind the melancholy of the self lies not so much intelligence as the fear of a self that achieves nothing. From then on, fear prevails over intelligence, splitting the original unity. The self cannot resist, and beyond the melancholy - which knew how to remain a little literary - comes nausea. On the other side of our being is a vindictive Roquentin. "I don't know how to take advantage of the opportunity: I walk at random, empty and quiet, under this unused sky. (1)" 
Notes:
(1) Jean-Paul Sartre, Nausea, 1938".
(Cynthia Fleury, The End of Courage, 2010)

E1.5.B.3.5_REF2_PH "ASTONISHMENT IS BORN OF THE ENCOUNTER WITH THE STRANGE, THE UNUSUAL. This strangeness can be unsettling because it shatters tour habits and places us in the presence of a stranger, a host (hospes, the one who gives or receives hospitality), perhaps even hostile (hostis, the enemy, same root); but this strangeness can still be delightful, it can take us, like a kidnapping, to a higher world that transports us with enthusiasm, in an "explosion of joy". And we can still experience the disconcerting strangeness of this world in the indifference of boredom, when everything is external and indifferent to us, when we suddenly see the absurdity of a daily life that we had come to regard as normal out of habit. Three modes, then, of experiencing the feeling that the world exists: the zero degree of boredom, the minor mode of anguish, the major mode of ecstasy. If we now turn our attention to each of these, we'll become familiar with strangeness and, short of knowing the unknowable, or of making sense of nonsense, we'll be better able to draw its boundaries".
(Jacques Darriulat, Being and Existence, 3-Experiencing Existence, 2020)

E1.5.B.3.5_REF3_PH "WHEN ANXIOUS, WE SUFFER THE OPPRESSION OF MEER PRESENCE, the world which, in the eyes of anxiety, becomes strangely unsettling, exerting against us what we call 'the power of things'.
Under the weight of anxiety, we intimately experience the monstrosity of the phenomenal and the incomprehensible permanence of the Presence, which opens us to the question of Being. This is why the object of anxiety is radically unspecified, it is not about what exists but about existence itself, not about what is but about being itself. And this oppression is fundamental, because it reminds us of the absence and nothingness of another life, which the limited circle of habit has repressed. It is through the ordeal of anguish that man comes to know himself as a being destined to die, and discovers within himself an 'impulse to live' that had hitherto been suppressed by the overwhelming rituals of daily life."
(Jacques Darriulat, Being and Existence, 3-Experiencing Existence, 2020)

E1.5.B.3.5_REF3.1_PH ANGUISH, ACCORDING TO HEIDEGGER, IS A FUNDAMENTAL EXPERIENCE THAT LIBERATES US FROM ROUTINE AND OPENS US UP TO THE QUESTION OF BEING. This experience is essential for escaping the inauthentic life and awakening the intellect to its true power.
“If boredom suffers from the monotony of Being, like the ever-recurring sea, anguish emerges from that state of imbecilic stupor, almost hypnosis, which meditates depression. Boredom is prodigiously indifferent to the platitude, to the overwhelming universality of Existence, everywhere present. But this indifference turns into distress, and even terror, when it is experienced in the feeling of anguish. In the first section of the first part of Sein und Zeit (there is no second part, the work remains unfinished), starting with chapter VI, § 40: “The fundamental affection of anguish as the privileged opening of Dasein” (14), Heidegger develops an “analytic of Dasein”, a reasoned description of the mode of existence that is specifically human, in an astonishing language that is difficult to translate and that belongs to him alone, as if kindled by some fire. Unlike the animal, which inhabits a habitat whose horizon is limited by nature - every animal has its own environment, whose frontier it does not transgress of its own accord, but only when forced to do so by danger, and then at the risk of its own life - man is the living being who has no environment of his own, whose world is not bounded, but rather open to the indefiniteness of transgression. This is why the beast never willingly leaves its niche, whereas man - that Being who only makes sense by asking the question of Being - launches his probes “beyond the sun, beyond the ethers, / Beyond the confines of the starry spheres” (Baudelaire). Dasein, then, is never housed in a specific world, but always stretched towards a beyond whose absence, or nothingness, it suffers. It is only in this tension towards, in the existence of a pro-ject, a project beyond itself, that Dasein becomes worthy of its destination, its historical destiny, in the sense that its destiny does not depend on the historical sequence of circumstances, but on Dasein's very being. If Dasein is thus like a being beyond itself, in debt to itself, to what it is not yet, if no world, however perfect, can calm the movement that carries it beyond, it is because it belongs to the vocation, the "ad-vocation" whose address it perceives, to always question, beyond any manifested world, the Being from which the world comes. Man exists only to provoke being, to ask that question of being which, according to Wittgenstein, is nonsense, but which, according to Heidegger, is the only way to unlock the circle of routine and thus to awaken thought to the power that belongs to it. For the task of thought is not to know or master being, much less to immerse itself in thinking about itself, but to maintain itself in questioning, never forgetting the enigma of being - at the risk of becoming stupid, trapped in a habitat made up of our habits, like a chatter that goes round and round in the circle of the norm, the discourse of the anonymous "someone", the discourse of everyone and no one, and not the authentic declaration of the one who dares to speak. In a style that owes its tension to the dark, nihilistic Romanticism that inspired it, Heidegger destines Dasein to the thought of being - to think is not to know, not to understand, but to weigh the weight of the incomprehensible - an anxiety from which we must not turn away, which we must not forget, for fear of sinking into the inauthentic, worthless life that no longer responds to the call of thought and therefore dies before its death. Anguish, then, is the primordial experience that opens the limited horizon of routine to the insurmountable dimension of the enigma, to the thought of Being. [...]
In anguish (die Angst, from angustia, “narrow”, “constricted”: we are anguished when we find ourselves trapped, like the speleologist in a bottleneck that slowly narrows around him and closes the way back), we suffer the oppression of meer presence, the world which, in the eyes of anxiety, becomes strangely unsettling, exerting against us what we call 'the power of things'. [...]
Under the weight of anxiety, we intimately experience the monstrosity of the phenomenal and the incomprehensible permanence of the Presence, which opens us to the question of Being. This is why the object of anxiety is radically unspecified, it is not about what exists but about existence itself, not about what is but about being itself. And this oppression is fundamental, because it reminds us of the absence and nothingness of another life, which the limited circle of habit has repressed. It is through the ordeal of anguish that man comes to know himself as a being destined to die, and discovers within himself an 'impulse to live' that had hitherto been suppressed by the overwhelming rituals of daily life."
(Jacques Darriulat, Being and Existence, 3-Experiencing Existence, 2020)

E1.5.B.3.5_REF3.1.a_PH "HEIDEGGER'S PENETRATING ANALYSIS FINDS ITS TRANSLATION IN THE STYLE OF THE POPULAR NOVEL NAUSEA, published by Jean-Paul Sartre in 1938. Antoine Roquentin, a drifter who has come back from everything, is engaged in vague research into the life of an 18th-century marquis and ends up in Bouville, a place reminiscent of Le Havre, where the author, an exiled Parisian, bitterly spent his early years as a teacher. Roquentin gets bogged down in Bouville as one gets bogged down in mud, sinking little by little as if absorbed by the inertia of things, by their overwhelming presence. One depressing day, Roquentin comes to sit in Bouville's public garden, a common place for Sunday boredom. Sartre devotes an impressive ten pages to the brutal, shattering revelation of the monstrosity of existence: 'Never, before these last days, had I felt what it meant to exist [...] Suddenly it was there, as clear as day: existence had suddenly revealed itself. It had lost its harmless appeal as an abstract category: it was the very dough of things. "
(Jacques Darriulat, Being and Existence, 3-Experiencing Existence, 2020)

E1.5.B.3.5_REF4_PH “ANXIETY [...]
Contingency is like a possibility that has come to pass. Contingent things are those that are and could not have been otherwise. Every individual being is therefore contingent. This is what anxiety obscurely perceives or manifests, like the shadow cast by nothingness on the suddenly fragile evidence of being. "In anxiety," writes Heidegger, "being as a whole becomes unstable." This is because being loses its necessity, fullness, and justification. Why is there something rather than nothing? There is no answer. All being is contingent and superfluous, as Sartre would say—hence the nausea, which is his vertigo—and absurd, as Camus would say. Being only appears by detaching itself—but why and how?—from the imperceptible background of what we cannot otherwise conceive. "In the clear night of the nothingness of anxiety," writes Heidegger, "the original manifestation of being as such finally shows itself: namely, that there is something rather than nothing." However, as Valéry said, nothingness breaks through, or at least anxiety makes us feel that way. »
(André Comte-Sponville, Philosophical Dictionary, 2013)

E1.5.B.3.5_REF5_PH LISA FELDMAN BARRETT SPECULATES THAT ANXIETY IS A DISORDER OF PREDICTION THAT IS THE OPPOSITE OF DEPRESSION: INSTEAD OF BEING LOCKED INTO THE PAST, AN ANXIOUS BRAIN FAILS TO MAKE SUCCESSFUL PREDICTIONS, LEAVING IT OVERWHELMED BY UNRESOLVED PREDICTION ERROR FROM THE WORLD AND A CHRONIC STATE OF UNCERTAINTY.
“Anxiety is a condition that seems very different from chronic pain and depression. When you’re anxious, you feel worried or worked up, like you don’t know what to do with yourself, and generally miserable. This is a stark contrast with depression, in which you feel sluggish, like you can’t go on with life, and also generally miserable, and with chronic pain, which is, well, painful.
So far, we’ve learned that emotion, chronic pain, chronic stress, and de-pression all involve the interoceptive and control networks. Those same networks are critical to anxiety as well. Anxiety is still a puzzle being unraveled, but one thing seems certain: it is yet another disorder of prediction and prediction error across these two networks. The neural pathways studied in anxiety for prediction and prediction error are also the same ones as for emotion, pain, stress, and depression.
Traditional research on anxiety disorders is founded on the old “triune brain” model, that cognition controls emotion. Your allegedly emotional amygdala is overactive, they say, and your so-called rational prefrontal cortex is failing to regulate it. This approach is still influential, even though the amygdala is not the home of any emotion, the prefrontal cortex does not house cognition, and emotion and cognition are whole-brain constructions that cannot regulate each other. So, how is anxiety made? We don’t know all the details yet, but we have some tantalizing clues.
I speculate that an anxious brain, in a sense, is the opposite of a de-pressed brain. In depression, prediction is dialed way up and prediction er-ror way down, so you’re locked into the past. In anxiety, the metaphorical dial is stuck on allowing too much prediction error from the world, and too many predictions are unsuccessful. With insufficient prediction, you don’t know what’s coming around the next corner, and life contains a lot of cor-ners. That’s classic anxiety.

Anxiety sufferers, for whatever reason, have weakened connections between several key hubs in the interoceptive network, including the amygdala. Some of these hubs also happen to sit in the control network. These weakened connections likely translate into an anxious brain that is clumsy at crafting predictions to match the immediate circumstances, and that fails to learn effectively from experience. You might predict threats needlessly, or create uncertainty by predicting imprecisely or not at all. In addition, your interoceptive inputs become even more noisy than usual when your body budget has been in the red for a while; as a consequence, your brain ignores them. These situations leave you open to a lot of uncertainty and a lot of prediction error that you can’t resolve. And uncertainty is more unpleasant and arousing than assured harm, because if the future is a mystery, you can’t prepare for it. For example, when people are seriously ill but have an excellent chance of recovery, they are less satisfied with life than people who know their disease is permanent.
Based on the evidence, it appears that anxiety, like depression, is a con-structed category in the same fashion as emotion, pain, and stress. The misery you feel in anxiety and depression tells you that something is seriously wrong with your body budget. Either your brain is trying to secure a de-posit, ramping up unpleasant affect, or it’s attempting to reduce your need for the deposit by remaining still, resulting in fatigue. Your brain may cat-egorize these sensations as anxiety, depression, or, for that matter, pain or stress or emotion.”
(Lisa Feldman Barrett, How Emotions Are Made - The Secret Life of the Brain, 2017)

E1.5.B.3.6. A SIMPLE REMEDY FOR FATIGUE AND ANXIETY: TAKE A NAP
It's important to understand how to rest, as a tired body can easily fall into despair, further worsening its condition.

E1.5.B.3.6_REF1_PH "THE ELOQUENCE OF PASSION ALMOST ALWAYS TRICKS US; by this I mean the sad or cheerful, brilliant or gloomy phantasmagoria that the imagination unfolds for us, depending on whether our body is rested or tired, excited or depressed. Naturally, we then blame things and our fellow men, instead of finding and changing the real cause, which is often small and insignificant. [...] Always defiant before the proofs of the imagination, he [the tired man] should, by reflection, suspect the eloquence of the passions at play and refuse to believe in them, which would suddenly destroy most of his ailment; for a little headache and tiredness of the eyes are bearable and hardly last; but despair is terrible and of itself aggravates its causes".
(Alain, On Happiness, 14 May 1913)

E1.5.B.3.7. TRANSFORMING THE FAILURE OF FATIGUE INTO VALUE
Flaubert seeks a form of engagement with reality that conceals none of its inherent emptiness. Life is "only this", yet we can create a realm of life sensation that embodies hope.

E1.5.B.3.7_REF1_PH FLAUBERT'S CONCEPT OF STYLE IS INTEGRAL TO HIS LITERARY METHOD, EMPHASIZING IMPERSONALITY, THE RELATIVITY OF VIEWPOINTS, AND NON-CONCLUSIVE NARRATIVES. His style aims to create an existential experience for the reader,  reveal the void within reality and transform failure into value, establishing a sphere of life sensation that offers hope.
"La notion de style telle que la pense Flaubert est totalement intégrante de tout: du concept qu'il invente d'impersonalité qui est le noeud de sa méthode littéraire, de la relativité des points de vues, de la forme non conclusive de son récit, du fait qu'un récit a pour but de faire rêver. Ce style pour lui c'est même un style de vie - du tout pour l'art pour que la lecture devienne une expérience existentielle pour le lecteur. Il ne faut donc pas distinguer le style du sujet chez Flaubert. Ça devient même une affaire de "moi". Il faut s'affranchir du moi pour que le lecteur puisse y trouver toute sa place. L'impersonalité cela consiste à refuser tous ces mensonges dont la littérature de son époque est faite: "le pot de chambre romantique". Il essaye de trouver une forme de rapport au réel qui ne cache rien de ce néant qui traverse le réel; de tous ces phénomènes de répulsion qui traversent notre expérience de la vie, parce que c'est la seule façon de ne pas y ajouter quelque chose de négatif, en donnant au contraire au temps la saveur proustienne de ce qui peut néanmoins être une réussite dans l'échec. C'est le rejet d'une partie du réel qui le fait écrire. Son oeuvre est la dialectique d'une transformation de l'échec en valeur. D'accord la vie ce n'est que cela, mais on peut y établir une sphère de sensation de vie qui constitue un espoir."
(Pierre-Marc de Biasi au micro de l'émission les Chemins de la Philosophie, diffusée sur France Culture le 03/05/2021)

E1.5.B.3.7_REF2_PH "ALL SPIRITUAL LIFE COMES DOWN TO A TANGO WITH THE TRAGEDY OF EXISTENCE AND NOT BEING EMBITTERED BY WHAT OVERWHELMS US. I came across a phrase from Etty Hillesum which offered me a magnificent spiritual exercise: « I am not the only one to be tired, sick, sad or anxious, I am in unison with millions of other people through the centuries, all this is life ».
Therefore, when you get up in the morning, you can choose to consider other people as teammates on the same boat, or even on the same galley. [...] Reflecting on the towering suffering that weighs down on the world every day invites us to venture to be a bit more generous on a daily basis, although this may be easier said than done. Etty Hillesum also urges me not to tell myself « this is my fatigue » but to simply welcome the fatigue that is passing through me today."
(Alexandre Jollien, The Pharmacopoeia n° 56)

E1.5.B.3.8. FATIGUE = A CLAIM BY THE EXHAUSTED INDIVIDUAL BODY FOR THE RIGHT TO SOCIAL REST 
In this sense, it is a form of intensity, not negativity. 

E1.5.B.3.8_REF1_PH FATIGUE, SO OFTEN PERCEIVED AS AN UNACCEPTABLE EXCUSE, IS IN REALITY A LEGITIMATE DEMAND OF THE BODY FOR A SOCIAL REST, a need that society does not recognise.
“Make an experiment: draw up a table of (credible) excuses received: you want to cancel a conference, an intellectual task: what will be the excuses that won't raise suspicion, or retaliation? Fatigue? Certainly not. [...]
So fatigue is not coded, is not acceptable = still functions in language as a mere metaphor, a sign without a referent, which falls into the realm of the artist (of the intellectual as artist) unclassified, therefore unclassifiable: placeless, without a place to rest itself, socially untenable → hence Blanchot's (tired!) cry: “I'm not asking for fatigue to be suppressed. I'm asking to be transported to a region where it is possible to be tired." → Fatigue = the exhausting claim of the individual body that demands the right to social rest (that sociality in me might be allowed to rest for a while = current theme of the Neutral). In fact fatigue = an intensity: society doesn't recognize it.”
(Roland Barthes, The Neutral, course at the Collège de France, 1977-1978)

E1.5.B.3.8_REF2_PH “EXHAUSTION OF THE INTELLECT.—Our occasional coldness and indifference towards people, which is imputed to us as hardness and defect of character, is often only an exhaustion of the intellect. In this state other men are to us, as we are to ourselves, tedious or immaterial.”
(Friedrich Nietzsche, Human, All Too Human III, Aphorism 299, 1878)

E1.5.B.3.9. FATIGUE AND ETHICS
What is the impact of fatigue on our ethical behavior? Can a fatigued person behave in the same way as someone who isn't tired? Do philosophical or moral texts consider the constant state of fatigue that we often experience?

E1.5.B.3.10. FATIGUE BORN OUT OF SUFFERING

E1.5.B.3.10_REF1_PO "THERE IS A LANGUOR OF THE LIFE 
More imminent than Pain -

Envelopes Consciousness -
