E1.5.B.8.3. IT'S NOT VERTICAL WALKING THAT MAKES A HUMAN A HUMAN, IT'S THE GERM OF AWARENESS OF MENTAL UNEVENNESS THAT THE STANDING UPRIGHT POSITION PROVOKES IN THE HUMAN BEING.
The topic of differently-abled individuals, those with varied complexions, leads us to an expression that reflects the inherent nature of beings under vertical tension [vertically challanged people]. As stated in Rilke's poem on the torso, "You have to change your life" implies maintaining awareness of the internal vertical and observing the influence of the higher pole's attraction.

E1.5.B.8.3_REF1_PH PETER SLOTERDIJK EXPLORES THE IDEA THAT HUMAN BEINGS ARE INHERENTLY FLAWED AND NEED PROPER TRAINING TO COMPENSATE FOR THEIR VARIOUS DISABILITIES. Human existence is characterized by a constant need to learn and adapt, underscoring the importance of self-awareness and effort to change one's life.
"Wherever humans appear, their crippledom has preceded them: this insight was the chorus of philosophical discussions on the human being in the previous century, regardless of whether, as in psychoanalysis, one speaks of humans as cripples of helplessness who can only hobble towards their goals; or, like Bolk and Gehlen, views them as neotenic cripples whose chronic immaturity can only be balanced out by rigid cultural capsules; or, like Plessner, as eccentric cripples chronically standing beside themselves and observing their lives; or, like Sartre and Blumenberg, as visibility cripples who must spend their lives coming to terms with the disadvantage of being seen. [...]
The implications of these observations are as diverse as the diagnoses themselves. They have one thing in common, however: if humans are cripples, without exception and in different ways, then each one of them, in their own particular way, has good reason to understand their existence as an incentive for corrective exercises. 
We recall that in Würtz’s schema of crippledoms, short persons were classified as ‘growth cripples’. In later times, the same people were termed ‘disabled in terms of growth’. When it even became offensive to speak of ‘disability’, the small became those who have different abilities in terms of their format. In the 1980s, politically correct Americans found the most up-to-date name for people who often have to look upwards: ‘vertically challenged people’. This turn of phrase cannot be admired enough. It constitutes a terminological creation that outgrew its inventors without their even noticing what they had achieved. We can laugh at this formulation twice: once at the correct preciousness of its authors and once at ourselves. We have every right and reason to laugh, for we have an absolute majority in the assembly of those who are challenged by verticality. The formula has been valid since we began to practise learning to live – and, as I am seeking to show, one can neither not practise nor not learn to live. Even being a poor student must first be learned.
In short, people had to speak about the handicapped, the differently constituted, to stumble on a phrase that expresses the general constitution of beings under vertical tension. ‘You must change your life!’ means, as we saw in Rilke’s torso poem: you must pay attention to your inner vertical axis and judge how the pull from its upper pole affects you! It is not walking upright that makes humans human; it is rather the incipient awareness of the inner gradient that causes humans to do so."
(Peter Sloterdijk, You must change your life, 2009)

E1.5.B.9. MADNESS is part of every human being's potential

E1.5.B.9.1. THERE IS A LINK BETWEEN MADNESS AND CREATIVITY, a connection that lies at the heart of what makes us human. This link should be recognized as the potential within every individual. Compared to other primates, our brains are significantly more complex, but also more fragile - more susceptible to instability. With its formidable complexity, our brain can sometimes struggle to support itself.

E1.5.B.9.1_REF1_TH THERE SEEMS TO BE A SUBTLE LINK BETWEEN MADNESS AND CREATIVITY, NOT A DIRECT OVERLAP BUT A KINSHIP. Studies show that people who are related to people with mental disorders are more likely to work in creative professions.
"This connection, this hypothesis of a connection between madness and creativity, I have the impression that everyone has the intuition that there is something that connects madness and creativity. In the history of thought, this intuition takes different forms: the melancholy of the ancient Greeks, the intensification of passions in the Romantics, the demand for absolute freedom in the Surrealists. I was very surprised to discover - and this is where science comes in - that this connection is not what we thought it was. It's not an overlap, where madness = creativity; it's not the cliché "you have to be mad to be creative"; it's actually a link of kinship. Epidemiology shows, for example, that the probability of working in a creative profession is higher among relatives of people with a mental disorder (their parents, siblings, children). There's a family resemblance between madness and creativity, but it's not romantic madness. I think that's largely untrue, it's much more subtle than that. 
We could also talk about genetics. It's quite amazing when you go back in history to prehistoric times to find that the genes that make you susceptible to mental disorders, that increase your risk of having a mental disorder (there is no schizophrenia or bipolar gene) are in the part of the genome that's different from that of the Neanderthal man with whom we lived until minus 30,000 years ago - as if these genes had accompanied us at the root of what makes us humans of knowledge, Homo Sapiens. Evolution would have selected for something that both exposes us to psychic risk and makes us the human beings we are. Our psychic apparatus allows us to create infinitely, because we can manipulate representations of the world and enrich them infinitely, but it's also what exposes us to losing ourselves. Perhaps we could say that this is really our fall from paradise, this access to representations of the world - especially language, of course - but also what makes us strong. 
There's a connection between madness and creativity; it's a connection that's at the root of what makes us human. I think we need to be able to see this as the potential of every human being. Compared to other primates, our brains are much more complex, but also more fragile - more susceptible to the madness bug. Our brain, in all its formidable complexity, can no longer support itself. Following the same line of reasoning, it is highly likely that the proportion of people who will experience moments of suffering that fall within the scope of psychiatric care will increase as our brains become even more powerful in the future - perhaps even more so in the hybridisation it will undergo with machines. We will probably pay an even higher price for the increased complexity and reality of mental disorders."
(Raphaël Gaillard, psychiatrist, interviewed in the programme Les chemins de la philosophie, broadcast on France Culture on 1 April 2022)

E1.5.B.9.2. LET'S BE CRAZY, LET'S BE OURSELVES!
To what extent are individuals deemed 'mad' compelled to seek treatment to conform to society's conventional model? What is the genuine need for a person to change for their own betterment? A person considered 'mad,' who poses no harm to themselves or others, is likely happier living freely in their eccentricity than being forced into conformity. This reflection applies to all levels of perceived insanity - extending to the eccentricity inherent in all of us: Let's be crazy, let's be ourselves!

E1.5.B.10. MONEY AND HAPPINESS

E1.5.B.10.1. HOW MUCH DO WE NEED?

E1.5.B.10.1_REF1_PSI "THE SATIATION LEVEL BEYOND WHICH EXPERIENCED WELL-BEING NO LONGER INCREASES was a household income of about [US$109,000 in 2022] in high-cost areas (it could be less in areas where the cost of living is lower). The average increase of experienced well-being associated with incomes beyond that level was precisely zero. This is surprising because higher income undoubtedly permits the purchase of many pleasures, including vacations in interesting places and opera tickets, as well as an improved living environment. Why do these added pleasures not show up in reports of emotional experience? A plausible interpretation is that higher income is associated with a reduced ability to enjoy the small pleasures of life. [...] “Beyond the satiation level of income, you can buy more pleasurable experiences, but you will lose some of your ability to enjoy the less expensive ones.” 
(Daniel Kahneman, Thinking Fast and Slow, 2011)

E1.5.B.10.1_REF1.1_CUR NEGUIN, 22, PHOTOGRAPHED BY HIS SWIMMING POOL in a working-class neighborhood of Jaboatão dos Guararapes, PE, Brazil, has fought hard to build this bubble in his image. He earns €7,650/year (discounted to 31/12/2023) as a dancer with the group Brega Funk MC Tróia (@mctroiaoficial). 
At what income level does money stop increasing happiness? The most recent studies conducted on the model proposed by Nobel Prize winner Daniel Kahneman in 2011 and updated in 2022 on the level of global satisfaction situate at €100,000/year the household income beyond which there is no longer any marginal increase in satisfaction from earning more. Neguin still has room to catch up...

E1.5.B.10.1_REF1.1.a_CUR “REGINALDO FRANCELINO DA SILVA, 22, STARTED DANCING AT THE AGE OF 10 at swingueira contests in the Totó neighborhood, in the far west of Recife, where he rose to prominence leading the Movimento Dance Group. About five years ago, in possession of a cell phone with a camera and internet access, he began recording home videos in which he appears dancing his own Brega Funk choreographies, which made him known on social media as Neguin do Charme (@neguinhobdtc10). Today, hired exclusively as a dancer for MC Tróia (@mctroiaoficial), he performs more than four shows a week, which guarantees him around three minimum salaries per month. In the future, he says, he plans to open a dance school in the Sucupira region of Jaboatão dos Guararapes, where he lives with his mother. In the photo, Neguin do Charme during the recording of the clip “Tô nem aí”, a song released by MCs Tróia, Lipinho Dantas, Elvis and PP in January 2016.
Until the end of January, photographer Bárbara Wagner will be on ZUM magazine's Instagram with images of the process of her #bolsadefotografia ZUM/IMS winning work, “Mestres de cerimônias”, which documents the relationship between reality and fantasy in the lives of young MCs from the Brega Funk movement in Recife and Funk Ostentação in São Paulo. When MCs, DJs and dancers leave the dances of the periphery and enter the mainstream through music videos made exclusively for social networks, a strong and lucrative culture of online fans and followers consolidates a dynamic of appropriation of status symbols historically belonging to the dominant classes. Following the production of the genre's main music video labels in order to document the gestures and scenes that build MC culture in the two Brazilian capitals, Bárbara observes this economy of desires for visibility, consumption and celebrity never before experienced in the country.”
(Zum Magazine)

E1.5.B.10.1_REF3_PSI RESEARCH INDICATES THAT FINANCIAL SUPPORT, SUCH AS CASH TRANSFERS, CAN ENHANCE SUBJECTIVE WELL-BEING (SWB). A meta-analysis shows a small but significant increase in SWB from cash transfers. Larger cash influxes lead to lasting improvements in SWB, and cash transfers are as effective or better than other cost-comparable interventions like psychotherapy and job training.
"Providing Financial Support
Upon his death in 44 BC, Julius Caesar left 300 Roman coins for each citizen (Goldsworthy 2006). More recently, governments and nonprofit organizations have frequently utilized cash transfers in an attempt to raise the well-being of disadvantaged populations. According to a recent meta-analysis of 45 studies conducted in low- and middle-income countries, cash transfers lead to a small but significant increase in SWB (d = 0.13) (McGuire et al. 2022). Although most of these studies were not preregistered, a growing number of rigorous preregistered experiments suggest that such cash transfers and other forms of financial support can provide an efficient mechanism for enhancing happiness. [...]

Taken together, the collection of studies above provide compelling evidence that increases in wealth cause increases in SWB. While small cash transfers—equivalent to 1 week or month of income—do not necessarily produce benefits, people who receive a large influx of cash exhibit lasting improvements in SWB. Moreover, cash seems to be as good or better than other interventions that carry similar costs, including psychotherapy and job training."
(Dunigan Folk and Elizabeth Dunn, How Can People Become Happier? A Systematic Review of Preregistered Experiments, 2023)

Read the full article: https://www.annualreviews.org/doi/10.1146/annurev-psych-022423-030818#abstractSection

(Cameron Crowe, Jerry Maguire, 1996)

E1.5.B.10.2. WISDOM, IT SEEMS TO ME, IS ALWAYS POISED UPON THE KNOWLEDGE OF MINIMUMS; IT MIGHT BE THOUGHT TO BE THE ART OF MINIMUMS
As Thoreau so well knew, and so painstakingly tried to show us, what a man most needs is not a knowledge of how to get more, but a knowledge of the most he can do without, and of how to get along without it. The essential cultural discrimination is not between having and not having or haves and have-nots, but between the superfluous and the indispensable.

E1.5.B.10.2_REF1_LIT BERRY CONTRASTS THE STRENGTH DERIVED FROM SIMPLE, WORLD-DEPENDENT PLEASURES WITH THE WEAKNESS TIED TO MATERIAL WEALTH AND SOCIETAL STANDARDS. Berry recounts growing up around a black man named Nick, who worked for Berry’s grandfather. Nick, to whom he dedicates the book, was a benediction of presence during Berry’s most formative years — a hard-working man with a buoyant imagination and an uncommonly cheerful mindset [...]:
"This much is clear to me: insofar as I am capable of feeling such pleasures as I believe Nick felt, I am strong; insofar as I am dependent on the pleasures made available by my salary and the things I own, I am weak. I feel much more secure in those pleasures for which I am dependent on the world, as Nick was for most of his, than in those for which I am dependent on the government or on a power company or on the manufacturers of appliances. And I am far from conceding anything to those who assume that the poor or anyone else can be improved by recourse to that carnival of waste and ostentation and greed known as “our high standard of living.” As Thoreau so well knew, and so painstakingly tried to show us, what a man most needs is not a knowledge of how to get more, but a knowledge of the most he can do without, and of how to get along without it. The essential cultural discrimination is not between having and not having or haves and have-nots, but between the superfluous and the indispensable. Wisdom, it seems to me, is always poised upon the knowledge of minimums; it might be thought to be the art of minimums."
(Maria Popova, Brain Pickings, Review on Wendell Berry's The Hidden Wound)

E1.5.B.10.3. CONSUMER HAPPINESS
Consumption permits us to obtain a "well-being that is measurable by objects and signs of comfort," according to Jean Baudrillard in The Consumption Society.
Thus, the quest for happiness is merely the accessible promise of equal well-being through consumption. Equality then loses its authentic political significance and transforms into "equality in front of the Object and other clear signs of social success and happiness." Consequently, the consumer ideal tends to dismiss "that happiness that does not need signs to prove its existence to others or ourselves".

E1.5.B.10.3_REF1_PH "TO THE DELIGHT OF CONSUMERS
The 2019 edition of China's "Singles' Day" was marked by an unprecedented world record, set by commercial giant Alibaba. In 24 hours, consumers spent 268.4 billion yuan, or nearly 35 billion euros, an increase of 26%. This year, it took barely an hour to reach 100 billion. This begs the question: is economic success an indicator of consumer happiness?

According to Jean Baudrillard, theorist of the "consumer society", we need to distinguish between economic well-being, which is socially conditioned, and the private search for authentic happiness. Through the simple act of consumption, it is indeed happiness that we claim to seek, the "absolute reference of the consumer society". But this "myth of happiness" is in fact the one that "collects and embodies in modern societies the myth of equality". And yet, in order to satisfy this desire for equality, it was necessary to provide criteria for measuring happiness; consumption thus allows us to grasp a "well-being measurable in objects and signs, in comfort", he writes in The Consumer Society.

The pursuit of happiness, then, means nothing more than the promise of a well-being that can be achieved through consumption. Equality then loses its real political meaning and becomes "equality before the object and other obvious signs of social success and happiness". In this way, the consumer ideal tends to exclude "that happiness which is independent of signs that could manifest it in the eyes of others and ourselves, that happiness which needs no proof". True happiness lies elsewhere, always outside the realm of consumption.

But can we completely separate the question of happiness from our standard of living and purchasing power? In The Economy of Happiness, Claudia Senik defends the idea that there is a positive link between happiness and economic interaction. Certainly, as the American researcher Richard Easterlin showed in the 1970s, post-war growth in the US did not lead to an increase in reported happiness. Happiness is essentially felt in relation to others, to a reference group, rather than to an objective situation.

To limit ourselves to this observation, however, would be to overlook the "remarkable result" achieved by developed societies. As Claudia Senik puts it: "if the average level of happiness does not increase, the inequality of happiness decreases". In other words, consumption is not a sign of complete happiness, but rather a more modest way out of a real malaise. This explains our attachment to economic development and growth, and the hopes of developing countries.

So what should we think about the concept of degrowth? Considering the hypothesis of a renunciation of growth, Claudia Senik concludes that "the economics of happiness does not lead us to believe that it could make us happier".
(Jean-Baptiste Juillard, Time To Philo, 14/11/2019)

E1.5.B.10.3_REF2_PH “THE LONGING FOR GROWTH IS NOT WRONG. The nub of the problem now is how to flip over, as in jujitsu, the magnificent growth-energy of modern civilization into a nonacquisitive search for deeper knowledge of self and nature… If people come to realize that there are many nonmaterial, nondestructive paths of growth — of the highest and most fascinating order — it would help dampen the common fear that a steady state economy would mean deadly stagnation.”

(Gary Snyder, Turtle Island, 1974)

“The danger and hope politically is that Western civilization has reached the end of its ecological rope. Right now there is the potential for the growth of a real people’s consciousness. […]

All of industrial/technological civilization is really on the wrong track, because its drive and energy are purely mechanical and self-serving — real values are someplace else. The real values are within nature, family, mind, and into liberation… And how do we make the choices in our national economic policy that take into account that kind of cost accounting — that ask, “What is the natural-spiritual price we pay for this particular piece of affluence, comfort, pleasure, or labor saving? […]

The only hope for a society ultimately hell-bent on self-destructive growth is not to deny growth as a mode of being, but to translate it to another level, another dimension… The change can be hastened, but there are preconditions to doing that… Nobody can move from [one] to [the other] in a vacuum as a solitary individual…. What have to be built are community networks… When people, in a very modest way, are able to define a certain unity of being together, a commitment to staying together for a while, they can begin to correct their use of energy and find a way to be mutually employed. And this, of course, brings a commitment to the place, which means right relation to nature.”
(Gary Snyder, The Real Work, 1980)

”True teachers are called into being by the contradictions generated by civilization… We need them.”
(Gary Snyder)

Who are the true teachers of our time?

E1.5.B.11. WAR

E1.5.B.11.1. AND WHEN IT COMES TO FIGHTING A "JUST WAR"?

E1.5.B.11.1.a. WHAT IS THE DIFFICULTY FOR THE MAN WHO HAS TO TAKE THE WORLD AS IT IS AND ACT IN IT, AND YET WOULD PREFER TO LIVE A SPIRITUAL LIFE INSTEAD?

E1.5.B.11.1.a_REF1_PH "THE BHAGAVAD GîTÂ TAKES AS ITS SITE ONE OF THOSE PERIODS OF TRANSITION AND CRISIS THROUGH WHICH HUMANITY PERIODICALLY GOES THROUGH IN ITS HISTORY, when great forces collide, resulting in gigantic destructions and reconstructions, intellectual, social, moral, religious and political; and these crises, at the present stage - psychological and social - of human evolution, generally culminate in a violent physical convulsion: struggle, war or revolution. The Gîtâ accepts the inherent necessity of these violent crises; it accepts not only the moral aspect, the struggle between the just and the unjust, between the asserting law of good and the forces that oppose its progress, but also the material aspect, the actual war of arms or any other violent physical struggle between the human beings who represent the conflicting forces.

Arjuna and Krishna, one human and the other divine, are reunited, not as prophets in a peaceful hermitage dedicated to meditation, but one fighting and the other holding the reins, on a battlefield filled with clamor, in a chariot and amidst a cloud of arrows. The Teacher of the Gîtâ is therefore not only the God in man who reveals himself in the Word of Knowledge, but also the God in man who moves our whole world of action, through whom and for whom all humanity exists, struggles and toils, towards whom all our human life travels and progresses.

It's all a terrible sin - for here the moral sense awakens to justify the revolt of sensations and emotions. It's a sin; there is no right or justice in killing one another, much less in killing those who are the natural objects of our worship and love, those whose death would deprive our life of all its charm. To violate these sacred sentiments cannot be a virtue, but only a heinous crime. It is understood that the offense, the aggression, the first sin, the crimes of lust and selfish passion, which caused such a crisis, came from the other side; nevertheless, armed resistance to evil under such conditions would itself be an even worse sin and crime, for the other side is blinded by passion and unconscious of its fault, whereas on this side one would sin with a clear consciousness of evil.

What then is the difficulty for the man who has to take the world as it is and act in it, and yet wants to live the spiritual life within himself?
(The Bhagavad Gita, commentary by Aurobindo)

E1.5.B.11.1.b. THE TEACHING OF THE GITA
One can draw inspiration from the main teachings of the Gita without necessarily believing in the reincarnation of souls. Interpret the cycle of reincarnations as an allegory for self-reinvention within this life, leading to new states of maturity and consciousness. Additionally, take joy in our contributions to the human condition's adventure, which continues after our death on a grand scale of time.

E1.5.B.11.1.b_REF1_PH "THE ÂRYAN WARRIOR'S CREED
16 - Being is not found in that which does not exist. Non-being is not found in that which exists. The limit of both being and non-being is perceived by those who see the truth.

The soul is and cannot cease to be, although it may change the forms through which it appears. This opposition of being and non-being, this equilibrium of being and becoming, which is the mental conception of existence, ends when we realize that the soul is the unique, imperishable self through which this entire universe has been 'expanded'.

17 - Know this: that with which all this world is woven is not to be destroyed. No one is able to effect the destruction of the imperishable.
18 - These bodies have an end; but they are said to belong to the eternal embodied self – that which is never lost and cannot be measured. So fight, Son of Bharata! 
19 - The one who perceives the self as a killer, and the one who perceives the self as killed: neither of them know that this self does not kill, nor is it killed. 
20 - The self is not born nor does it ever die. Once it has been, this self will never cease to be again. Unborn,eternal, continuing from the old, the self is not killed when the body is killed.
21 - The one who knows the eternal and the indestructible, that which is unborn, and imperishable, how does he cause to die, Son of Pritha, and whom? How does he kill, and whom?
22 - Just as one throws out old clothes and then takes on other, new ones; so the embodied self casts out old bodies as it gets other, new ones.
23 - Weapons do not cut the self, nor does fire burn it, nor do waters drench it, nor does wind dry it. 
24 - The self is not to be pierced, nor burned, nor drenched, nor dried; it is eternal, all-pervading and fixed – unmoving from the beginning.
25 - The self is not readily seen; by sight or mind; it is said to be formless and unchanging; so, when you have known this, you should not mourn.

Our grief at the death of men is an ignorant affliction about those for whom there is no reason to grieve, since they have not gone out of existence, since they have not undergone a painful or terrible change of state, but are, beyond death, neither less existing nor more unhappy than in life.

30 - Son of Bharata, the embodied self which exists in the body of everyone is eternally free from harm; so you should not grieve for any living beings.”
(The Bhagavad Gitâ, commentary by Aurobindo)

E1.5.B.11.1.c. PARTICIPATING IN A JUST CONFLICT IS ALSO PART OF THE COURAGEOUS ETHIC, WHICH FOCUSES NOT ON THE OUTCOME OF AN ACTION, BUT ON ITS JUSTNESS.

E1.5.B.11.1.c_REF1_PH THE BHAGAVAD GÎTÂ EMPHASIZES THE IMPORTANCE OF A JUST BATTLE. The struggle between good and evil is constant, and refusal to fight would be considered a sin. 
"31 - And as you discern your own dharma, you should not waver. For the warrior, there can be found nothing greater than battle for the sake of dharma.

This world, this manifestation of the Self in the material universe, is not merely a cycle of inner development, but a realm in which the outer circumstances of life must be accepted as the milieu and occasion for that development. It's a world of mutual aid and struggle; it doesn't allow us to glide along in peace and serenity among easy joys; every step forward must be conquered by heroic effort, by a violent conflict of opposing forces. Those who carry the inner and outer struggle through to the most physical conflict of all, war, are the kshatriyas [members of the second caste, that of princes and warriors], the powerful men; battle, strength, nobility, courage, that is their nature; to protect the right and to accept the stakes of battle without fail, that is their virtue and their duty.
There is a constant struggle between good and evil, just and unjust, the power that protects and the power that violates and oppresses. When this struggle is carried to the point of physical conflict, the champion, the standard-bearer of Right, must neither tremble nor shudder at the terrible and violent nature of the task he must accomplish. His virtue and duty are in the fight, not in abstention; it's not the slaughter, but the refusal to kill that would be a sin here.

38 - When you have made pleasure and pain the same – also gain and loss, and victory and defeat, then join yourself to battle; and in this way, you will not cause harm.”
(The Bhagavad Gitâ, commentary by Aurobindo)

E1.5.B.11.2. WAR CRIMES

E1.5.B.11.2_REF1_CUR "WITH ITS BLACK-AND-WHITE TONES, THE CHARNEL HOUSE WAS INSPIRED BY NEWSPAPER PHOTOGRAPHS SHOWING PILES OF CORPSES in liberated Nazi concentration camps. Picasso depicted a murdered family unceremoniously tangled together under a dining table. With its varying degrees of finish-you can see traces of charcoal and sections of blank canvas-and vast network of undulating lines and geometric shapes, Picasso seems to have obscured legibility in favor of generating a rhythmic abstraction. As if questioning the ability of painting to represent war, he created a shifting, spectral vision."
(Curatorship, MomA, NYC)

E1.5.B.11.2_REF2_CUR "THIS WORK IS OFTEN INTERPRETED AS A SELF-PORTRAIT THAT CAPTURES THE FEAR HESSE FELT AS A CHILD fleeing her home in Hamburg, Germany, to escape the oppressive Nazi regime. She was one of the last children saved by Kindertransport, a rescue program that transported Jewish children to unoccupied areas of Europe. At age two, she was relocated to Amsterdam with her sister; they rejoined their family in New York a year later. Hesse spoke about the lasting trauma of this experience: "The world thought I was a cute, smart kid and I kidded them. But at home I was called a terror. I was miserable, I had trouble-tremendous fear-incredible fear"
(Curatorship, MomA, NYC)

E1.5.B.11.2_REF3_PO “WAR, WHICH INFICTS SUFFERING ON THE WORLD with its
squadrons,
Is the perfect demonstration of philosophy’s error.
War, like everything human, wants to change things.
But nothing wants to change things more than war, and to
change them so much
And to change them so quickly.
But war causes death.
And to cause death is to disdain the universe.
Since it results in death, war proves itself wrong.
Since it’s proven wrong, all wanting-to-change-things is
proven wrong.
Let’s leave the outer universe and other people where Nature
put them.
So much pride and lack of awareness!
So much bustling, having to do things, wanting to leave a
mark!
When his heart stops beating, the commander of the
squadrons
Slowly returns to the outer universe.
In Nature’s direct chemistry
There’s no room for thought.
Humanity is an uprising of slaves.
Humanity is a government usurped by the people,

Existing because usurped, but erring, since to usurp is to
have no right.

E1.5.B.12. LET'S NOT ADD TO THE WOES THAT COME FROM OUTSIDE!

E1.5.B.12.1. THERE IS THE FUTURE THAT IS MADE AND THE FUTURE THAT WE MAKE FOR OURSELVES. THE REAL FUTURE IS MADE UP OF BOTH

E1.5.B.12.1_REF1_PH ""THERE IS THE FUTURE THAT IS MADE AND THE FUTURE THAT WE MAKE FOR OURSELVES. THE REAL FUTURE CONSISTS OF BOTH. As for the future that is made, like a storm or an eclipse, there's no point in hoping; you have to know and observe with dry eyes. As we wipe the lenses of the telescope, so we must wipe the fog of passions from our eyes. I hear well. The things of heaven, which we never change, have taught us resignation and the geometric mind, which are a good part of wisdom. But in earthly things, what changes has the industrious man made! [...]
Especially in the human order itself, where confidence is a fact, I count very badly if I don't count my own confidence. If I believe that I will fall, I will fall; if I believe that I can do nothing, I can do nothing. If I believe that my hope deceives me, it deceives me. Be careful there. I make good weather and storms; first in myself, then around me, in the world of men. For despair and hope move from one to the other, faster than the clouds change. If I trust him, he's honest; if I accuse him beforehand, he steals from me. They all give me my change, according to the coin.
(Alain, On Happiness, 1913)

E1.5.B.12.1_REF2_PH IF FAIRIES HAVE ADORNED YOUR CRADLE, BEWARE. "We would like to see a fatality in these choices which we inherit and which we have not made; but these choices do not commit us, for there is no bad lot; every lot is good if we want to make it good. Nothing marks weakness better than to argue about one's nature; no one has a choice; but a nature is rich enough to satisfy the most ambitious. To make virtue necessary is the great and beautiful work. 
"It's a pity I didn't study." That's the lazy man's excuse. Just study. I don't think it's so great to have studied in the past if you don't study anymore. Relying on the past is just as crazy as complaining about the past. Nothing that has been done is so beautiful that we can rest on it, nothing so ugly that we can't save it. I'd even go so far as to say that good luck is harder to track than bad. If fairies have adorned your cradle, beware.
(Alain, On Happiness, 1922)

E1.5.B.12.1_REF3_PH "HE WHO FIGHTS WITH MONSTERS SHOULD BE CAREFUL LEST HE THEREBY BECOME A MONSTER. And if thou gaze long into an abyss, the abyss will also gaze into thee."
(Friedrich Nietzsche, Beyond Good and Evil, 1886)

E1.5.B.12.1_REF4_LIT "ARE WE THEN CONDEMNED TO CONTORT OURSELVES LIKE A BUTTERFLY ON A PIN? Not if we do not entertain sorrow or give it a chance to remain with us throughout our life."
(Hans Christian Andersen)

E1.5.B.12.2. LET'S STOP RUINING OUR LIVES!

E1.5.B.12.2_REF1_TH PAUL WATZLAWICK LISTS THE RECIPES FOR MAKING US UNHAPPY IN SPITE OF OURSELVES:
“1. Above all, be loyal to yourself and never give up;
2. Play with the past: The fatal glass of beer; 
3. Just insist; 
4. Perpetuate the situation or difficulty you fear through avoidance; 
5. The powerlessness of the desire for revenge;
6. Communication about the object and the relationship;
7. The illusory alternative;
8. “Do whatever you like, as long as you don't get any pleasure out of it” or ‘Your duty is not to enjoy yourself’ and also ‘Be spontaneous!’ or ‘You should enjoy your duty’;
9. “You have no right, and no reason, to be sad”, ”Be happy!
10. Why should anyone love me?
11. Collusion;
12. Zero-sum games”
(Paul Watzlawick, Instructions to Make Ourselves Unhappy, 1983)

E1.5.B.12.2_REF1.1_TH BE LOYAL TO YOURSELF WITHOUT GIVING UP: "1. Simply choose one and only one [of the various guideposts of the so-called wisdom of the nations] and stick to it, make it your rule of life, the expression of yourself, so that you become absolutely convinced that there is only one valid point of view: your own. From such a point of view, it will soon become clear that the world has gone crazy.
(Paul Watzlawick, Instructions to Make Ourselves Unhappy, 1983)

E1.5.B.12.2_REF1.2_TH PLAYING WITH THE PAST: THE FATAL GLASS OF BEER "2. I never should have done it, but now it's too late. Remorse is choking me, but there's nothing I can do. It was a sin, the first time, that fatal time I'll regret all my life, but I've become a victim of my own sin."
(Paul Watzlawick, Instructions to Make Ourselves Unhappy, 1983)

E1.5.B.12.2_REF1.3_TH ALL YOU HAVE TO DO IS INSIST. "3 This seemingly simple formula, 'just insist,' is one of the most disastrous recipes developed on our planet over hundreds of millions of years. It has driven entire species to extinction. [...] This [...] game is based entirely on the relentless preservation of adaptations and solutions that may once have been the best, or even the only, possible. Difficulties are inevitable because all situations change over time.
(Paul Watzlawick, Instructions to Make Ourselves Unhappy, 1983)

E1.5.B.12.2_REF1.4_TH PERPETUATE THE SITUATION OR DIFFICULTY THROUGH AN AVOIDANCE CONDUCT. "4 Suppose a horse receives an electric shock to one of its hooves through a metal plate concealed in the stable floor. If a bell is rung before each shock, the animal seems to quickly establish a causal relationship between the bell and the unpleasant sensation. Now, every time the bell rings, he lifts his hoof - obviously to avoid the electric shock. Once this causal relationship is established between the two events, the electric shock is no longer useful. The ringing of the bell alone is enough to trigger the movement of the leg to lift the hoof. What's more, each of these avoidance behaviors seems to reinforce the animal's 'belief' that by lifting its hoof, it is avoiding an unpleasant shock. What the horse doesn't know, and what its avoidance behavior will forever prevent it from knowing, is that the danger has ceased to exist.
But this is not just superstition. Superstitious behavior has a well-known irregularity that renders it practically worthless, whereas avoidance behavior deserves the full confidence of all those who seek misfortune."
(Paul Watzlawick, Instructions to Make Ourselves Unhappy, 1983)

E1.5.B.12.2_REF1.5_TH THE POWERLESSNESS OF THE DESIRE FOR REVENGE. "5 Strictly speaking, there is no such thing as revenge. Revenge is an act that we burn to commit when we are powerless and because we are powerless: as soon as the feeling of powerlessness disappears, the desire for revenge disappears with it. 
'Who wouldn't have jumped for joy in 1940 at the mere thought of seeing SS officers humiliated and kicked? But when it becomes possible, it's nothing but ridiculous, pitiful and disgusting.'"
(Paul Watzlawick, Instructions to Make Ourselves Unhappy, 1983)

E1.5.B.12.2_REF1.6_TH COMMUNICATION ABOUT THE OBJECT AND ABOUT THE RELATION. "6. Seventy years ago, Bertrand Russell insisted on the need for a strict separation between statements about things and statements about relations. 'This apple is red' is a statement about the properties of this particular apple. But 'This apple is bigger than that one' is a statement about the relation between these apples. It would make no sense to apply it to either of the two apples separately, because the bigger-than statement focuses not on one of the two apples, but on the relationship that binds them together. 
Anthropologist and communication researcher Gregory Bateson took this important distinction one step further. He pointed out that both forms of statement are always present in all human communication, and he called them the object level and the relational level, respectively. This distinction allows us to understand more clearly that we can quickly come into conflict with any partner - any partner, but the closer the better. Suppose a woman asks her husband: 'Do you like this soup I made according to a new recipe?' If it is, he'll have no trouble answering 'yes,' to her great satisfaction. But if he hates the soup and isn't too worried about disappointing his partner, he can simply answer 'no.' But in the (statistically more likely) case where he both hates the soup and doesn't want to upset his wife, a difficulty arises from the start. At the object level (i.e., as far as the soup object is concerned), his answer should be 'no'. But at the relationship level, his answer should be 'yes' so as not to hurt her feelings. But since we have only one language for both levels, what can he say? His answer can't be yes or no. So he'll probably try to get out of this predicament with an ambiguous statement, such as 'Yes, the taste is fun,' hoping that she'll understand what he really means."
(Paul Watzlawick, Instructions to Make Ourselves Unhappy, 1983)

E1.5.B.12.2_REF1.7_TH THE ILLUSORY ALTERNATIVE"7 There is a useful and effective way to complicate your relationship with others. It consists in offering your interlocutor a choice between two possibilities. As soon as he chooses one, he can be blamed for not choosing the other. Communication experts call this trick the 'illusory alternative'. The structure is very simple. If the partner does A, he should have done B; but if he chooses B, he should have done A. I borrow a particularly telling example from Dan Greenburg's instructions to Jewish mothers - already quoted in the introduction: 
Offer your son two different ties. The first time he wears one, look at him with bitterness and say in the 'basic tone of voice: 'I knew you wouldn't like the other one.'"
(Paul Watzlawick, Instructions to Make Ourselves Unhappy, 1983)

E1.5.B.12.2_REF1.8_TH DO WHATEVER YOU WISH, AS LONG AS YOU DON'T GET ANY PLEASURE OUT OF IT "8. Spontaneously doing what is ordered is as impossible as consciously forgetting or deciding to sleep more soundly. Either we act spontaneously, that is, at our own discretion, or we obey an order and therefore don't act spontaneously. From a purely logical point of view, it is impossible to do both at the same time. [...]
This is the hackneyed and wretched example of the mother who demands that her son do his homework - not because it's the rule at school, but because he should like it. Here we come to the reversal of the definition of Puritanism. It's no longer your duty not to enjoy yourself, but that you should enjoy your duty. [...]
[If we are incapable of it, then] we are forced to assume that we are not normal, or that we live in an abnormal world. Since the 'world' is almost completely beyond individual action, we're practically forced to blame ourselves."
(Paul Watzlawick, Instructions to Make Ourselves Unhappy, 1983)

E1.5.B.12.2_REF1.19_TH "BE HAPPY!" "9 It seems to make little difference whether the 'be happy!' paradox is imposed on us by ourselves or by some external authority. In fact, it's just one of many variations on the 'be spontaneous' paradox. Virtually all spontaneous behavior lends itself to the construction of these inescapable traps: we can demand that someone spontaneously remember or forget; we can express the wish to receive such and such a gift, and then be disappointed to have received it 'only' for having expressed the wish; we can try to induce an erection or an orgasm by the mere play of the will, which makes impossible precisely what it is trying to accomplish; we can try to fall asleep because we want to; we can try to love when love is presented as a moral obligation. "
(Paul Watzlawick, Instructions for Making Ourselves Unhappy, 1983)

E1.5.B.12.2_REF1.10_TH WHY SHOULD ANYONE LOVE ME? "10. I must first refer to Dostoyevsky's interesting suggestion that the biblical commandment 'Thou shalt love thy neighbor as thyself' would make more sense if understood in reverse - i.e., that one can only love one's neighbor if one begins by loving oneself. 
With less elegance but all the more precision, Marx (no, not that one, Groucho) later wrote: 'I don't want to belong to any club that will accept me as a member' [...] For there is much to be done when one starts from a simple disbelief in one's own ability to arouse the love of others. From the strength of this fundamental conviction, we can discredit, without seeming to touch, all those who would dare to love us. For there is obviously something wrong with people who are capable of loving someone who doesn't deserve to be loved."
(Paul Watzlawick, Instructions to Make Ourselves Unhappy, 1983)

E1.5.B.12.2_REF1.11_TH COLLUSION "11. We need to find a partner who, by being what he or she is, enables us to be what we want to be - but, again, let's be careful not to succeed! 
In communication theory this model is called collusion. It describes a rather subtle arrangement, a form of (often unconscious) give and take, in which I allow myself to be confirmed and sanctioned by my partner in the way I see myself and want to show myself. [...] Only a partner who plays the required role in his relationship with me can make us 'real'. In his absence, we must rely on our dreams alone, the unreality of which is a recognised characteristic. But why should someone be willing to play this particular role for me? There are two possible reasons. 

1. The role he has to play to make me feel 'real' is the same role he wants to play to produce his own 'reality'. You'd think they would complement each other perfectly. And so we might be tempted to question the usefulness of this mechanism for our purposes. But please note that in order to remain perfect, this relationship must never change in the slightest. As time passes, children tend to grow up, patients tend to heal, and initial joy is soon followed by disillusionment. For the desperate attempts to prevent the other from escaping this bond become more unbearable every day. Allow me to quote Sartre again:
'While I am trying to free myself from the grip of others, others are trying to free themselves from mine; while I am trying to enslave others, others are trying to enslave me. We're not talking here about unilateral relations with an object in itself, but about reciprocal, shifting relations.'
Insofar as any collusion presupposes that the other is, of his own accord, exactly as I need him to be, it inevitably leads to a 'be spontaneous' paradox.

2. The inevitability of this result becomes even more obvious when we consider the second reason why a partner might want to play the complementary role from which I will derive a sense of my own 'reality'. This reason is, quite simply, fair payment for the service rendered. The example of prostitution immediately comes to mind.
(Paul Watzlawick, Instructions to Make Ourselves Unhappy, 1983)

E1.5.B.12.2_REF1.12_TH ZERO-SUM GAMES "12. Game theory makes a fundamental distinction between two categories of games, those in which the sum of gains and losses is zero and the others. [You only have to start a zero-sum game at the level of relationships to be sure of the hellish turn things will sooner or later take. Desperately obsessed by the idea of winning in order not to lose, players of this kind of game risk forgetting one thing: the main adversary, life, and all that it has to offer apart from victory and defeat. In the face of this adversary, both partners lose".
(Paul Watzlawick, Instructions to Make Ourselves Unhappy, 1983)

E1.5.B.12.2_REF2_CUR LIKE NICOLÁS ROBBIO, WE CAN CHOOSE TO PLAY THINGS DOWN. This bouquet has clearly undergone unexpected treatment. “It has had better days”, Robbio tells us. By choosing to play down a situation that could hardly have a tragic consequence for anyone, Robbio invites us to adopt this same cheerful outlook on situations that affect us more deeply, and whose impact on our mood we can work on through the use of reason. Let's learn to put things into perspective!

E1.5.B.12.2_REF3_PH "THE REMEDY IS THE SAME, AGAINST ALL FEARS AND TYRANIC FEELINGS; you must go straight to the thing and see what it is.
Epictetus says to the passenger: "You are as afraid of this storm as if you had to swallow this whole great sea; but, my dear, it only takes two pints of water for you to drown". It's safe to say that this tremendous movement of the waves misrepresents the real danger. We say and think: "raging sea: voice of the abyss; angry waves; threat; attack". It's not true; they are rocking according to gravity, the tide and the wind; no bad fate; it's not all this noise or all this movement that will kill you; no fatality; you can survive a shipwreck; you can drown in calm waters; the real problem is this: will you keep your head above water?"
(Alain, On Happiness, December 10, 1910)

E1.5.B.12.3. "THE BAD IS STRONGER THAN THE GOOD".

E1.5.B.12.3_REF1_PSI NEGATIVITY DOMINANCE, supported by research, indicates that bad emotions, feedback, and information have a greater effect on us and are processed more thoroughly than positive counterparts. 
"The psychologist Paul Rozin, an expert on disgust, observed that a single cockroach will completely wreck the appeal of a bowl of cherries, but a cherry will do nothing at all for a bowl of cockroaches. As he points out, the negative trumps the positive in many ways, and loss aversion is one of many manifestations of a broad negativity dominance. Other scholars, in a paper titled « Bad Is Stronger Than Good », summarized the evidence as follows: « Bad emotions, bad parents, and bad feedback have more impact than good ones, and bad information is processed more thoroughly than good. The self is more motivated to avoid bad self-definitions than to pursue good ones. Bad impressions and bad stereotypes are quicker to form and more resistant to disconfirmation than good ones ».”
(Daniel Kahneman, Thinking Fast and Slow, 2011)

E1.5.B.12.3_REF2_PH PSYCHODRAMA "There is the tragedy of existence, loneliness, suffering, disease, wickedness and violence. Let's not make things worse by colapsing into psychodrama, this totally useless mental storm that cuts us off from goodness in the world. Life is like a train, step by step, it moves forward and again and again, suffering appears as soon as you want to settle somewhere."
(Alexandre Jollien, The Pharmacopoeia n ° 60)

E1.5.B.12.3_REF3_CUR MIRROR OF LIFE, like many of Henry Koerner’s paintings, reveals the artist’s preocupation with his experiences during the second World War. Born in Vienna to Jewish parents, Koerner escaped Austria following Hitler's 1938 invasion, fleeing first to Italy and subsequently to America. Soon after, he was drafted by the United States military and stationed in Europe, where he was assigned to sketch the proceedings of the Nuremberg trials. Koerner returns to Vienna only to learn that his parents, who had stayed in Austria, had died in concentration camps during the war. Mirror of Life emerges from this context of conflict and loss. Disorienting juxtapositions - night and day, biblical events and present-day life, ordinary pastimes and bizarre phenomena -  present a chaotic and disjunctive reflection of reality. The shirtless man leaning out of his window seems to be a stand-in for the artist. Home for him, is not only the place where one resides but also a vantage point from which to witness all that has been lost. 
(Whitney Museum, NY curator´s review, 2019)

E1.5.B.12.4. CONSIDER THINGS IN THE GRAND SCALE OF THINGS AND OUR RIGHTFUL PLACE IN THE WORLD

E1.5.B.12.4.a. WHEN WE THINK IN TERMS OF TIME IN THE GRAND SCALE OF THINGS, WE HAVE 3 OPTIONS OPEN TO US: 
i - PROTECT WHAT IS PRECIOUS,
ii - CULTIVATE FOR THE FUTURE, 
iii - ELIMINATE WHAT IS TOXIC.

"The same three tasks recur across cultures and epochs: to shelter what is precious, to yield what is valuable, and to dispose of what is harmful.

Shelter (memories, precious matter, messages, fragile lives).
Yield (information, wealth, metaphors, minerals, visions).
Dispose (waste, trauma, poison, secrets).

Into the underland we have long placed that which we fear and wish to lose, and that which we love and wish to save."
(Robert Macfarlane's Underland: A Deep Time Journey)

E1.5.B.12.4.a.i. PROTECT WHAT IS PRECIOUS

E1.5.B.12.4.a.i_REF1_PH "THE JOY OF MEETING AND THE SORROW OF SEPARATION … WE SHOULD WELCOME THESE GIFTS … with our whole soul, and experience to the full, and with the same gratitude, all the sweetness or bitterness as the case may be. Meeting and separation are two forms of friendship that contain the same good, in the one case through pleasure and in the other through sorrow… Soon there will be distance between us. Let us love this distance which is wholly woven of friendship, for those who do not love each other are not separated."
(Simone Weil, Gravity and Grace)

E1.5.B.12.4.a.i_REF2_CUR “A SCULPTURAL PORTRAIT OF THE ARTIST'S FAMILY, this work features seventeen human skulls, consisting of 3D prints from CT scans of all the biological members, as well as that of the artist's partner. The creation of the installation included the exhumation and scanning of skulls of his younger sister who died in childbirth and his deceased father. This unusual family portrait brings together the 15 alive and 2 deceased members of the family, who get to be together for the first time even when some never met.”
(Curatorship: Jumex, Mexico City)

E1.5.B.12.4.a.ii. CULTIVATE FOR THE FUTURE

E1.5.B.12.4.a.ii_REF1_LIT "IS ART RESISTANCE? CAN YOU PLANT A GARDEN TO STOP A WAR? It depends how you think about time. It depends what you think a seed does, if it’s tossed into fertile soil. But it seems to me that whatever else you do, it’s worth tending to paradise, however you define it and wherever it arises."
(Olivia Laing, Drôle de Temps: L'Art en cas d'Urgence, 2020)

E1.5.B.12.4.a.ii_REF2_LIT ROBERT MACFARLANE EXPLORES THE CONCEPT OF "DEEP TIME," THE VAST CHRONOLOGICAL EXPANSE OF EARTH'S HISTORY MEASURED IN EPOCHS AND AEONS. By adopting this long-term view, we can re-imagine our present, recognize our impact, and consider the legacy we leave for future epochs.
"Enshrined in the layers of the underland, in the layered dust of cultures and epochs, are traces of our abiding need for shelter and sacrament, our age-old hunger for knowledge encoded in the stone tablets of dead languages and the rusted instruments of annealed curiosity, radiating a reminder that we are creatures not only of place but of time. Plunging into the time-warping wonderland beneath the surface through the riven trunk of an old ash tree, Macfarlane writes:
"Down between roots to a passage of stone that deepens steeply into the earth. Colour depletes to greys, browns, black. Cold air pushes past. Above is solid rock, utter matter. The surface is scarcely thinkable… Direction is difficult to keep. Space is behaving strangely — and so too is time. Time moves differently here in the underland. It thickens, pools, flows, rushes, slows."
“Deep time” is the chronology of the underland. Deep time is the dizzying expanses of Earth history that stretch away from the present moment. Deep time is measured in units that humble the human instant: epochs and aeons, instead of minutes and years. Deep time is kept by stone, ice, stalactites, seabed sediments and the drift of tectonic plates. Deep time opens into the future as well as the past. The Earth will fall dark when the sun exhausts its fuel in around 5 billion years. We stand with our toes, as well as our heels, on a brink."

But for all its consolations, such a dilation of the telescopic perspective can be deeply disquieting in alerting us to our own helpless insignificance — motes of matter in a blink of time, adrift amid the unfeeling emptiness of pure spacetime. It takes especial existential courage to inhabit this physical fact with unflinching psychic agency, with the insistence that however brief our earthly time may be, however small our impact relative to the vast scales of time and civilization, we can still leave a worthy mark on an ancient world. Macfarlane cautions against the defeatist cowardice of taking the scale of deep time for permission to squander our precious allotment:

"We should resist such inertial thinking; indeed, we should urge its opposite – deep time as a radical perspective, provoking us to action not apathy. For to think in deep time can be a means not of escaping our troubled present, but rather of re-imagining it; countermanding its quick greeds and furies with older, slower stories of making and unmaking. At its best, a deep time awareness might help us see ourselves as part of a web of gift, inheritance and legacy stretching over millions of years past and millions to come, bringing us to consider what we are leaving behind for the epochs and beings that will follow us. [...]
When viewed in deep time, things come alive that seemed inert. New responsibilities declare themselves. A conviviality of being leaps to mind and eye. The world becomes eerily various and vibrant again. Ice breathes. Rock has tides. Mountains ebb and flow. Stone pulses. We live on a restless Earth."
(Maria Popova, Brain Pickings, review on Robert Macfarlane's Underland: A Deep Time Journey)

E1.5.B.12.4.a.ii_REF2.1_PO “THE WHOLE OF IT

If you step back, you can see it all
on the horizon: your mother’s death, the children
grown, their smooth eyelids crossed with veins
like saffron filaments. Further still, and see
your smiling grandmother treading the cold ocean,
tiny lakes in her collarbones, your great-
great grandchildren drawing their names
in the sand with sticks. The seas
rising and falling, ice scraping the earth,
and pockets of life surviving — lee sides, hot springs,
protected places. First light on the first day
of your life, and first light of first stars.
And in this way, every death, each apparent ending,
might, in the mind of spacetime, be woven
into one memory, so that always is
this tree, and the long days of falling in love
over the intricate pattern of bark and leaf,
and the first green cell learning to swallow sun.”
(Hannah Fries)

The whole of it by hannah Fries, read by Rose Hanzlik:
https://www.themarginalian.org/2025/06/17/the-whole-of-it-hannah-fries/?mc_cid=c3e50bcd3f&mc_eid=0b6483decb

E1.5.B.12.4.a.ii_REF3_CUR “PALE BLUE DOT”, originally released in 1990, shows Earth as a faint speck in a streak of sunlight. The image was taken by Voyager 1 when it had completed its planetary flybys and was 3.7 billion miles away from the sun.

E1.5.B.12.4.a.iii. ELIMINATE WHAT IS TOXIC

E1.5.B.12.4.a.iii_REF1_LIT “CONSIDER, SIR, HOW INSIGNIFICANT THIS WILL APPEAR A TWELVEMONTH HENCE.” "I was exceedingly uneasy at the aukward appearance I supposed I should make to Johnson and the other gentlemen whom I had invited, not being able to receive them at home, and being obliged to order supper at the Mitre. I went to Johnson in the morning and talked of it as a serious distress. He laughed and said, ‘ Consider, Sir, how insignificant this will appear a twelvemonth hence.’— Were this consideration to be applied to most of the little vexations incidents of life, by which our quiet is too often disturbed, it would prevent many painful sensations. I have tried it frequently, with good effect. ‘ There is nothing (continued he) in this mighty misfortune ; nay, we shall be better at the Mitre.’ "
(Boswell’s Life of Johnson, revised third edition 1799, first published in 1791)

E1.5.B.12.4.b. THE WONDER AND PEACE THAT COME FROM THE AWARENESS OF BEING PART OF A WHOLE FAR MORE MAJESTIC AND ETERNAL THAN ONESELF

E1.5.B.12.4.b_REF1_PO "ACHIEVING PERSPECTIVE

Straight up away from this road,
Away from the fitted particles of frost
Coating the hull of each chick pea,
And the stiff archer bug making its way
In the morning dark, toe hair by toe hair,
Up the stem of the trillium,
Straight up through the sky above this road right now,
The galaxies of the Cygnus A cluster
Are colliding with each other in a massive swarm
Of interpenetrating and exploding catastrophes.
I try to remember that.

And even in the gold and purple pretense
Of evening, I make myself remember
That it would take 40,000 years full of gathering
Into leaf and dropping, full of pulp splitting
And the hard wrinkling of seed, of the rising up
Of wood fibers and the disintegration of forests,
Of this lake disappearing completely in the bodies
Of toad slush and duckweed rock,
40,000 years and the fastest thing we own,
To reach the one star nearest to us.

And when you speak to me like this,
I try to remember that the wood and cement walls
Of this room are being swept away now,
Molecule by molecule, in a slow and steady wind,
And nothing at all separates our bodies
From the vast emptiness expanding, and I know
We are sitting in our chairs
Discoursing in the middle of the blackness of space.
And when you look at me
I try to recall that at this moment
Somewhere millions of miles beyond the dimness
Of the sun, the comet Biela, speeding
In its rocks and ices, is just beginning to enter
The widest arc of its elliptical turn."
(Pattiann Rogers)

E1.5.B.12.4.b_REF2_LIT LOREN EISELEY CONTEMPLATES THE DEEP CONNECTION BETWEEN PAST AND PRESENT LIFE FORMS AND THE JOURNEY OF LIFE ITSELF, EMPHASIZING THE PROFOUND AWARENESS AND GRATITUDE that arise from recognizing our shared biological heritage. He suggests that while humans are bound by time, there is a potential for achieving new dimensions of wisdom and understanding through persistent exploration and curiosity.
"Descending into an enormous slit in Earth’s crust — “a perfect cross section through perhaps ten million years of time” — in search of fossils, Eiseley describes the skull he discovers entombed in stone several million years down this chute of time:

      It was not, of course, human. I was deep, deep below the time of man* in a remote age near the beginning of the reign of mammals. I squatted on my heels in the narrow ravine, and we stared a little blankly at each other, the skull and I. There were marks of generalized primitiveness in that low, pinched brain case and grinning jaw that marked it as lying far back along those converging roads where… cat and man and weasel must leap into a single shape.

Eiseley meets the bygone creature with a jolt of perspective:

      The skull lay tilted in such a manner that it stared, sightless, up at me as though I, too, were already caught a few feet above him in the strata and, in my turn, were staring upward at that strip of sky which the ages were carrying farther away from me beneath the tumbling debris of falling mountains. The creature had never lived to see a man, and I, what was it I was never going to see? … I thought, as I patiently began the task of chiseling into the stone around the skull, I would never again excavate a fossil under conditions which led to so vivid an impression that I was already one myself. The truth is that we are all potential fossils still carrying within our bodies the crudities of former existences, the marks of a world in which living creatures flow with little more consistency than clouds from age to age.

[...] To remember this, just like remembering that we only exist because of flowers, is to allow an awareness that reaches beyond cerebral knowledge and into some deep creaturely gladness that, in an instant, makes you feel connected to everything else alive, grateful to be part of this immense living symphony of time and chance.

      Perhaps there is no meaning in it at all, the thought went on inside me, save that of journey itself, so far as men can see. It has altered with the chances of life, and the chances brought us here; but it was a good journey — long, perhaps — but a good journey under a pleasant sun. Do not look for the purpose. Think of the way we came and be a little proud. Think of this hand — the utter pain of its first venture on the pebbly shore. [...]

Perhaps the Slit, with its exposed bones and its far-off vanishing sky, has come to stand symbolically in my mind for a dimension denied to man, the dimension of time. Like the wistaria on the garden wall he is rooted in his particular century. Out of it — forward or backward — he cannot run. As he stands on his circumscribed pinpoint of time, his sight for the past is growing longer, and even the shadowy outlines of the galactic future are growing clearer, though his own fate he cannot yet see. Along the dimension of time, man, like the rooted vine in space, may never pass in person. Considering the innumerable devices by which the mindless root has evaded the limitations of its own stability, however, it may well be that man himself is slowly achieving powers over a new dimension — a dimension capable of presenting him with a wisdom he has barely begun to discern. Through how many dimensions and how many media will life have to pass? Down how many roads among the stars must man propel himself in search of the final secret? The journey is difficult, immense, at times impossible, yet that will not deter some of us from attempting it… We have joined the caravan, you might say, at a certain point; we will travel as far as we can, but we cannot in one lifetime see all that we would like to see or learn all that we hunger to know."
(Maria Popova's review of Loren Eiseley's The Immense Journey, 1957)

E1.5.B.12.4.b_REF4_PO "KOSMOS
Who includes diversity and is Nature,
Who is the amplitude of the earth, and the coarseness and sexuality of the earth, and the great charity of the earth and the equilibrium also,

E1.5.B.12.4.b_REF4.1_PO "CROSSING BROOKLYN FERRY

 1
Flood-tide below me! I see you face to face!
Clouds of the west—sun there half an hour high—I see you also face to face.

Crowds of men and women attired in the usual costumes, how curious you are to me!
On the ferry-boats the hundreds and hundreds that cross, returning 
    home, are more curious to me than you suppose,
And you that shall cross from shore to shore years hence are more to
me, and more in my meditations, than you might suppose.

 2
The impalpable sustenance of me from all things at all hours of the day,
The simple, compact, well-join’d scheme, myself disintegrated, every one disintegrated yet part of the scheme,
The similitudes of the past and those of the future,
The glories strung like beads on my smallest sights and hearings, on the 
    walk in the street and the passage over the river,
The current rushing so swiftly and swimming with me far away,
The others that are to follow me, the ties between me and them,
The certainty of others, the life, love, sight, hearing of others.

Others will enter the gates of the ferry and cross from shore to shore,
Others will watch the run of the flood-tide,
Others will see the shipping of Manhattan north and west, and the heights of Brooklyn to the south and east,
Others will see the islands large and small;
Fifty years hence, others will see them as they cross, the sun half an hour high,
A hundred years hence, or ever so many hundred years hence, others will see them,
Will enjoy the sunset, the pouring-in of the flood-tide, the falling-back to the sea of the ebb-tide.

3
It avails not, time nor place—distance avails not,
I am with you, you men and women of a generation, or ever so many generations hence,
Just as you feel when you look on the river and sky, so I felt,
Just as any of you is one of a living crowd, I was one of a crowd,
Just as you are refresh’d by the gladness of the river and the bright flow,
I was refresh’d,
Just as you stand and lean on the rail, yet hurry with the swift current, I stood yet was hurried,
Just as you look on the numberless masts of ships and the thickstemm’d pipes of steamboats, I look’d. [...]

5
What is it then between us?
What is the count of the scores or hundreds of years between us?
Whatever it is, it avails not—distance avails not, and place avails not,
I too lived, Brooklyn of ample hills was mine,
I too walk’d the streets of Manhattan island, and bathed in the waters around it,
I too felt the curious abrupt questionings stir within me,
In the day among crowds of people sometimes they came upon me,
In my walks home late at night or as I lay in my bed they came upon me,
I too had been struck from the float forever held in solution,
I too had receiv’d identity by my body,
That I was I knew was of my body, and what I should be I knew I should be of my body."
(Walt Whitman, Leaves of grass, 1855)

[...]
Of vista—suppose some sight in arriere through the formative chaos, 
     presuming the growth, fulness, life, now attain’d on the journey,
(But I see the road continued, and the journey ever continued;)
Of what was once lacking on earth, and in due time has become supplied—and of what will yet be supplied,
Because all I see and know I believe to have its main purport in what will yet be supplied."
(Walt Whitman, Leaves of grass, 1855)

E1.5.B.12.4.b_REF5_PO "DEAD STARS

Out here, there’s a bowing even the trees are doing.
            Winter’s icy hand at the back of all of us.
Black bark, slick yellow leaves, a kind of stillness that feels
so mute it’s almost in another year.

I am a hearth of spiders these days: a nest of trying.

We point out the stars that make Orion as we take out
      the trash, the rolling containers a song of suburban thunder.

It’s almost romantic as we adjust the waxy blue
      recycling bin until you say, Man, we should really learn
some new constellations.

And it’s true. We keep forgetting about Antlia, Centaurus,
      Draco, Lacerta, Hydra, Lyra, Lynx.

But mostly we’re forgetting we’re dead stars too, my mouth is full
      of dust and I wish to reclaim the rising —

to lean in the spotlight of streetlight with you, toward
      what’s larger within us, toward how we were born.

Look, we are not unspectacular things.
      We’ve come this far, survived this much. What

would happen if we decided to survive more? To love harder?

What if we stood up with our synapses and flesh and said, No.
      No, to the rising tides.

Stood for the many mute mouths of the sea, of the land?

What would happen if we used our bodies to bargain

for the safety of others, for earth,
      if we declared a clean night, if we stopped being terrified,

if we launched our demands into the sky, made ourselves so big
people could point to us with the arrows they make in their minds,

rolling their trash bins out, after all of this is over?"
(Ada Limón)

E1.5.B.12.4.b_REF6_PH "GANDHI SAID: « AS LONG AS A MAN DOES NOT PUT HIMSELF IN THE LAST PLACE OF HIS OWN ACCORD, THERE IS NO SALVATION FOR HIM. »
Letting go of one's advantage, daring to put oneself in the last place is perhaps freeing oneself from the stupidest tyranny, that of the ego. Shouldn't we learn to disobey this capricious ego which always wants to put itself at the center of the world, which judges it, comments on it and which draws everything towards itself? Putting oneself in the last place is perhaps letting the others exist as they are, seeing that the world is bigger than our opinions, seeing that the universe is bigger than our desires. Asceticism here, as always, can be joyful. Play with the self. When it's pretentious, that is almost always, resist it kindly. What if putting ourselves in the last place, far from making us suffer and humiliating us, was giving us access to a larger mindset? A state of mind that welcomes everything, without focusing on the little me, without focusing on the mood of the moment."
(Alexandre Jollien, The Pharmacopoeia n ° 22)

Through existential engineering. “Give me a place to stand,” Archimedes proclaimed in the third century B.C., “and I will move the earth.” Levered by ecstasy, one springs out of one’s mind. Thrown free of one’s normal self, a person stands in another place, on the limits of body, society, and reason, watching the known world dwindle in the distance (a spot standing far away)."

E1.5.B.12.4.b_REF8_PO "WHELKS

Here are the perfect 
fans of the scallops, 
quahogs, and weedy mussels 
still holding their orange fruit - 
and here are the whelks -
whirlwinds, 
each the size of a fist, 
but always cracked and broken - 
clearly they have been traveling 
under the sky-blue waves 
for a long time.
All my life
I have been restless -
 I have felt there is something 
more wonderful than gloss -  
than wholeness- 
than staying at home. 
I have not been sure what it is.
But every morning on the wide shore 
I pass what is perfect and shining 
to look for the whelks, whose edges 
have rubbed so long against the world 
they have snapped and crumbled - 
they have almost vanished, 
with the last relinquishing 
of their unrepeatable energy, 
back into everything else.
When I find one 
I hold it in my hand,
I look out over that shanking fire, 
I shut my eyes. Not often, 
but now and again there's a moment 
when the heart cries aloud: 
yes, I am willing to be 
that wild darkness, 
that long, blue body of light."
(Mary Oliver, New and selected poems: Volume one, 1992)

E1.5.B.12.4.b_REF9_LIT "IN THE BREAKING WAVES, WOOLF FINDS A STAGGERING EMBLEM OF OUR STRUGGLE TO HOLD THE LARGER WHOLENESS IN VIEW, in faith, when our worlds come momentarily disworlded:

Radiating from Woolf’s gorgeous words is the reminder that all states of mind, all territories of feeling, even those that feel most unsurvivable — perhaps especially those that feel most unsurvivable — are merely moments in time, and yet they are not islanded in the river of being but belong with the rest of the current, the current that springs from the selfsame source as our capacity for beauty, for transcendence, for experiencing ourselves as “the thing itself”
