E1.5.C.6. FOR THOSE WHO ARE INCONDITIONAL ADVOCATES OF THE "REMEMBRANCE SELF", death carries less weight if you're satisfied with your life.

E1.5.D. HOW MUCH FREE WILL DO WE HAVE?
According to Baruch Spinoza, free will paves a challenging path to joy. Even though we are finite beings and not the cause of ourselves, thus subject to necessity and determinism, the freedom we can achieve is considered a form of participation in nature. Nearly four centuries after Spinoza, what is the scientific perspective on free will? Regardless of whether we possess free will or not, how is our belief in free will connected to happiness?

E1.5.D.1. WHEN WE PARTICIPATE "ONLY IN OURSELVES", WE ARE NEVER FREE. We attain freedom when we participate in nature, that is, in a power greater than ourselves.

E1.5.D.1.1. ONE IS NEVER ENTIRELY FREE. 
The soul is an idea of the body - which is itself infinitely complex, so that we don't know what a body can do - or what the idea of that body can do. This opacity is unsurpassable.
The good faith of the free man in part 4 of the Ethics is not fooled by the fact that consciousness lies to itself. Even the wise man in Part 5 is just as subject to affects as the others.

E1.5.D.1.1_REF1_PH "PROPOSITION 32
Will cannot be called a free cause, but only a necessary cause.

PROOF
Will is only a particular mode of thinking, like intellect ; therefore [...] no volition can exist, nor be conditioned to act, unless it be conditioned by some cause other than itself, which cause is conditioned by a third cause, and so on to infinity. But if will be supposed infinite, it must also be conditioned to exist and act by God, not by virtue of his being substance absolutely infinite, but by virtue of his possessing an attribute which expresses the infinite and eternal essence of thought [...]. Thus, however it be conceived, whether as finite or infinite, it requires a cause by which it should be conditioned to exist and act. Thus [...] it cannot be called a free cause, but only a necessary or constrained cause.

COROLLARY 1
Hence it follows, first, that God does not act according to freedom of the will.

COROLLARY 2
It follows secondly, that will and intellect stand in the same relation to the nature of God as do motion, and rest, and absolutely all natural phenomena, which must be conditioned by God (Prop. 29) to exist and act in a particular manner. For will, like the rest, stands in need of a cause, by which it is conditioned to exist and act in a particular manner. And although, when will or intellect be granted, an infinite number of results may follow, yet God cannot on that account be said to act from freedom of the will, any more than the infinite number of results from motion and rest would justify us in saying that motion and rest act by free will. Wherefore will no more appertains to God than does anything else in nature, but stands in the same relation to him as motion, rest, and the like, which we have shown to follow from the necessity of the divine nature, and to be conditioned by it to exist and act in a particular manner."
(Baruch Spinoza, Ethics, Part One, Proposition 32)

E1.5.D.1.1_REF1.1_PH "PROPOSITION 28
Every individual thing, or everything which is finite and has a conditioned existence, cannot exist or be conditioned to act, unless it be conditioned for existence and action by a cause other than itself, which also is finite, and has a conditioned existence ; and likewise this cause cannot in its turn exist, or be conditioned to act, unless it be conditioned for existence and action by another cause, which also is finite, and has a conditioned existence, and so on to infinity.."
(Baruch Spinoza, Ethics, Part One, Proposition 28)

Read the Ethics online: http://app.ethica-spinoza.net/en/text

E1.5.D.1.1_REF1.2_PH "DEFINITION 7
That thing is called free, which exists solely by the necessity of its own nature, and of which the action is determined by itself alone. On the other hand, that thing is necessary, or rather constrained, which is determined by something external to itself to a fixed and definite method of existence or action.."
(Baruch Spinoza, Ethics, Part One, Définition 7)

Read the Ethics online: http://app.ethica-spinoza.net/en/text

E1.5.D.1.1_REF1.3_PH "PROPOSITION 29
Nothing in the universe is contingent, but all things are conditioned to exist and operate in a particular manner by the necessity of the divine nature."
(Baruch Spinoza, Ethics, Part One, Proposition 29)

Read the Ethics online: http://app.ethica-spinoza.net/en/text

E1.5.D.1.1_REF1.3.a_PH “HAVING FORGOTTEN THE VIOLENCE OF COMMITMENT, WE LIE TO OURSELVES, persuading ourselves that we have freely deliberated, and finally opted for the choice that seemed most reasonable. But, in truth, there's nothing of the sort: we chose because we intimately felt the imperative that commanded us to do so.”
(Jacques Darriulat, Spinoza and the Plenitude of this World, 2- The Critique of Superstitions, 2017)

E1.5.D.1.1_REF1.3.a.i_PH “TO EARN MONEY BUT RISK WASTING YOUR LIFE, OR TO SEEK THE TRUE LIFE BUT RISK LIVING IN RELATIVE POVERTY - this is the highly philosophical dilemma facing everyone who stands on the threshold of the true life. This is what Spinoza himself tells us, in a little treatise he composed later, around 1660, after the leap had been made: Tractatus de intellectus emendatione, Treatise on the reformation (almost restoration) of the intelligence, or faculty of discernment. The opening lines beautifully set out the alternative we've just been talking about: “I decided to investigate whether there wasn't something that was a true good, capable of communicating itself, that the soul, rejecting everything else, could be affected by alone; something whose discovery and acquisition would give me for eternity the enjoyment of a supreme and continuous joy (in aeternum fruerer laetitia). I say 'finally I made up my mind': at first sight, indeed, it seemed unreasonable to want to give up something certain for something uncertain. I saw the advantages that honors and riches give us; but I also saw that I had to give up the pursuit of them if I wanted to apply myself seriously to this other and new search. Now I could see that if ever supreme felicity were contained in honors and riches, I would have to be deprived of it; but if, on the other hand, it were not contained in them and I pursued them exclusively, I would deprive myself of supreme felicity forever.” Spinoza would not have become himself if he had chosen business and dropped philosophy, but neither would he have acted in accordance with himself if he had chosen otherwise than he did. This is how we become only in order to understand what we have always been, and how true freedom lies not in offering choices, but on the contrary in obeying the inner determination that orders us to become what we are: “That thing is said to be free which exists by the mere necessity of its nature and determines by itself alone to act” (Eth. I, def. 7). Later, having forgotten the violence of commitment, we lie to ourselves, persuading ourselves that we have freely deliberated, and finally opted for the choice that seemed most reasonable. But, in truth, there's nothing of the sort: we chose because we intimately felt the imperative that commanded us to do so, and which is truly, according to Spinoza, the voice of God, or of nature. And if we had to do it all over again, we'd do it all over again. There is nothing more necessary than the choices of freedom. ”
(Jacques Darriulat, Spinoza and the Plenitude of this World, 2- The Critique of Superstitions, 2017)

E1.5.D.1.1.4_REF1_PH "APPENDIX
[...] all men are born ignorant of the causes of things, all have the desire to seek for what is useful to them, and they are conscious of such desire. Herefrom it follows first, that men think themselves free, inasmuch as they are conscious of their volitions and desires, and never even dream, in their ignorance, of the causes which have disposed them to wish and desire. Secondly, that men do all things for an end, namely, for that which is useful to them, and which they seek. Thus it comes to pass that they only look for a knowledge of the final causes of events, and when these are learned, they are content, as having no cause for further doubt. If they cannot learn such causes from external sources, they are compelled to turn to considering themselves, and reflecting what end would have induced them personally to bring about the given event, and thus they necessarily judge other natures by their own. [...]"
(Baruch Spinoza, Ethics, Part One, Appendix of Proposition 36)

Read the Ethics online: http://app.ethica-spinoza.net/en/text

E1.5.D.1.1_REF1.5_PH "PROPOSITION 48
In the mind there is no absolute or free will ; but the mind is determined to wish this or that by a cause, which has also been determined by another cause, and this last by another cause, and so on to infinity."
(Baruch Spinoza, Ethics, Part Two)

Read the Ethics online: http://app.ethica-spinoza.net/en/text

E1.5.D.1.1_REF2_PH WE HAVE THE ILLUSION OF FREEDOM
The feeling of freedom when we are subject to affects, as when we feel free to love someone, is an illusion. Only an active joy that is not accompanied by the idea of an external cause lasts.

E1.5.D.1.1_REF2.1_PH "PROPOSITION 43
Stimulation may be excessive and bad ; on the other hand, grief may be good, in so far as stimulation or pleasure is bad.

PROOF
Localized pleasure or stimulation (titillatio) is pleasure, which, in so far as it is referred to the body, consists in one or some of its parts being affected more than the rest [...] ; the power of this emotion may be sufficient to overcome other actions of the body [...], and may remain obstinately fixed therein, thus rendering it incapable of being affected in a variety of other ways : therefore [...] it may, be bad. Again, grief, which is pain, cannot as such be good [...]. But, as its force and increase is defined by the power of an external cause compared with our own [...], we can conceive infinite degrees and modes of strength in this emotion [...] ; we can, therefore, conceive it as capable of restraining stimulation, and preventing its becoming excessive, and hindering the body's capabilities ; thus, to this extent, it will be good.

PROPOSITION 44
Love and desire may be excessive.

PROOF
Love is pleasure, accompanied by the idea of an external cause [...] ; therefore stimulation, accompanied by the idea of an external cause is love [...] ; hence love maybe excessive [...]. Again, the strength of desire varies in proportion to the emotion from which it arises [...]. Now emotion may overcome all the rest of men's actions [...] ; so, therefore, can desire, which arises from the same emotion, overcome all other desires, and become excessive, as we showed in the last Proposition concerning stimulation."
(Baruch Spinoza, Ethics, Part Four)

Read the Ethics online: http://app.ethica-spinoza.net/en/text

E1.5.D.1.1_REF2.1.a_PH EXEMPLE OF AN ORIGINALLY PASSIVE JOY TURNED ACTIVE:
"I wandered
lonely as a cloud
That floats on high o’er vales and hills,

When all at once I saw a crowd,

A host, of golden daffodils;

Beside the lake, beneath the trees,

Fluttering and dancing in the breeze.

 

Continuous as the stars that shine

And twinkle on the milky way,

They stretched in never-ending line

Along the margin of a bay:

Ten thousand saw I at a glance,

Tossing their heads in sprightly dance.

 

The waves beside them danced; but they

Out-did the sparkling waves in glee:

A poet could not but be gay,

In such a jocund company:

I gazed—and gazed—but little thought

What wealth the show to me had brought:

 

For oft, when on my couch I lie

In vacant or in pensive mood,

They flash upon that inward eye

Which is the bliss of solitude;

And then my heart with pleasure fills,

And dances with the daffodils."

(William Wordsworth )

Listen to the poem: https://www.poetryfoundation.org/poems/45521/i-wandered-lonely-as-a-cloud

E1.5.D.1.1_REF2.2_PH A THEORY OF EMOTIONS IN ANIMATED PICTURE
Inside Out: Emotional Theory Comes Alive (how accurate is the emotional theory that underlies the animated picture?) - 0 to 4'50" & 5'40"
Each day corresponds to discrete moments coloured by the 5 emotions: Fear, Sadness, Joy, Anger, Disgust - based on the work of psychologist Paul Eckman (who computes 7 basic emotions - surprise and contempt were cut in the film).
The film seems to pause that the child's behavior will be defined by whichever emotion is at the helm and also that overall personality is determined by core memories as emotional drivers. This theory runs counter to psychoanalysis that postulates that emotions can be driven by the unconscious. Manifestations of emotions are often far removed from their causes; understanding them as entities is troublesome because they are often motivated by something else - being driven, not drivers.
But it is not science, it's a kids movie, a comedy! The film encourages us to feel what needs to be felt - because it is a part of life - even when it hurts. If it helps kids to better expect and accept sadness, it is a good thing (although Joy and Sadness are not discrete things). The film also has the merit of inspiring emotional intelligence, as paradoxically incarnated by Joy herself, who - as a discrete emotion in the film - is subject to all the other emotions.
(NERDWRITER FILM & ART CRITIC: Inside Out: Emotional Theory Comes Alive)

Watch the video on YouTube:
https://youtu.be/xXYhua4IwoE

E1.5.D.1.1_REF3_PH VARIATIONS ON SPINOZA
Although he is a great admirer of Spinozist philosophy, Robert Misrahi believes that Spinoza was mistaken in positing that man is never totally free. He founds a Spinozist philosophy of happiness that blends with Sartrian existentialism.

E1.5.D.1.1_REF3.1_PH "FOR ROBERT MISRAHI, HAPPINESS IS A JOURNEY THROUGH THE FOUR ACTS OF JOY: FOUNDING, LOVING, DREAMING, AND ACTING. It's a philosophy based on the subject as the source of all his actions. The ultimate joy is to create yourself. At some point you reach your greatest desire and you can say to yourself, 'Now I can die.'"
(Robert Misrahi, philosopher of happiness in Les Chemins de la Philosophie, broadcast on France Culture on 12/28/2018)

Listen to the podcast: https://www.franceculture.fr/emissions/les-chemins-de-la-philosophie/profession-philosophe-1742-robert-misrahi-philosophe-du-bonheur

E1.5.D.1.2. SPINOZA'S AMBITION IS FOR US TO UNDERSTAND OURSELVES WITHIN THE NATURAL ORDER IN ORDER TO REFORM OUR LIVES. 
The challenge of the Ethics is to build a behavior based on the mourning of our illusions: I transition from the desires I take for reality to the reality I take for my desire. Finding a way to make ordinary affects make us happy: this is the most direct route to lasting joy.

E1.5.D.1.2.a. "PART THREE: THE ORIGIN AND NATURE OF FEELINGS
Most of those who have written about the feelings and ways of life of men seem to deal, not with natural things that follow the common laws of Nature, but with things that are outside Nature. What's more, they seem to conceive of man in Nature as an empire within an empire. For they believe that man disturbs the order of Nature rather than follows it, that he has absolute power over his actions, and that he determines himself from no other source than himself."
(Baruch Spinoza, Ethics, Part Three).

Read the Ethics online: http://app.ethica-spinoza.net/en/text

E1.5.D.1.2.b. ACCEPTING REALITY AS ONE'S DESIRE DOESN'T MEAN WE NO LONGER SUFFER FROM SADNESS, FEAR OR LOSS.

E1.5.D.1.2.b_REF1_PH THE BOOK OF DELIGHTS "Each day, beginning on his forty-second birthday and ending on his forty-third, Gay composed one miniature essay — “essayettes,” he calls them, in that lovely poet’s way of leavening meaning with makeshift language — about a particular delight encountered that day, swirled around his consciousness to extract its maximum sweetness. (Delight, he tells us, means “out from light,” sharing etymological roots with delicious and delectable.) What emerges is not a ledger of delights passively logged but a radiant lens actively searching for and magnifying them, not just with the mind but with the body as an instrument of wonder-stricken presence "
(Maria Popova, Brain Pickings, review on Ross Gay´s The book of Delights, 2019)

E1.5.D.1.2.b_REF1.1_PH DEVELOPING OF A DELIGHT MUSCLE "It didn’t take me long to learn that the discipline or practice of writing these essays occasioned a kind of delight radar. Or maybe it was more like the development of a delight muscle. Something that implies that the more you study delight, the more delight there is to study… I felt my life to be more full of delight. Not without sorrow or fear or pain or loss. But more full of delight. I also learned this year that my delight grows — much like love and joy — when I share it."
(Ross Gay, The book of Delights, 2019)

E1.5.D.1.2.b_REF2_PO "WILD GEESE

You do not have to be good.
You do not have to walk on your knees
For a hundred miles through the desert, repenting.
You only have to let the soft animal of your body
love what it loves.
Tell me about despair, yours, and I will tell you mine.
Meanwhile the world goes on.
Meanwhile the sun and the clear pebbles of the rain
are moving across the landscapes,
over the prairies and the deep trees,
the mountains and the rivers.
Meanwhile the wild geese, high in the clean blue air,
are heading home again.
Whoever you are, no matter how lonely,
the world offers itself to your imagination,
calls to you like the wild geese, harsh and exciting —
over and over announcing your place
in the family of things."
(Mary Oliver, Dream work, 1986)

E1.5.D.1.2.c. JOHNS COWPER POWYS SHARES A CLOSE LINK TO SPINOZA'S INTUITION OF ETERNITY AND ACTIVE JOY

E1.5.D.1.2.c_REF1_LIT JOHN COWPER POWYS DESCRIBES THE PROFOUND SENSATIONS AND THOUGHTS HE EXPERIENCED DURING SOLITARY WALKS, WHERE HE FELT A MYSTERIOUS CONNECTION WITH NATURE AND OBJECTS AROUND HIM. These walks induced a state where past memories fused with the present, creating a sense of timelessness and contentment, devoid of future desires.
"Did I share at such times the sub-thoughts, or over-thoughts, that the old earth herself has, as she turns upon her axis, or that the vast volume of the ocean has, as his tide gathers along his beaches or draws back hoarsely into his gulfs? 
They were at any rate what might be called sensation-thoughts. [...] What I would like to emphasize just here is that the pleasure I got from these things of my solitary walks did not present itself to me as an aesthetic pleasure, nor did it call up in my mind the idea of beauty. What gave me these sensations seemed to be some mysterious “rapport” between myself and these things. It was like a sudden recognition of some obscure link, some remote identity, between myself and these objects. Posts, palings, hedges, heaps of stones —they were part of my very soul.
But the mere movement of walking, the mere contact with earth and air and the breath of the free wind as I left the town behind, always seemed able to engender in me a mood in which the present took to itself a sort of winnowed essence of similar memories from the past and thus became a continuity of half-forgotten feelings that welled up in my consciousness and reduced the future to complete non-existence. I thus became on these lonely walks like one who has found the clue to the obliteration of the future; and although I did not feel as though I touched the Eternal, I certainly felt as though I ceased to want anything or wish for anything that I did not already possess. What I possessed was in fact—if I may put it so—a sort of half-eternity, made up of a fusion of past and present, with the future, and all its wants and wishes, totally annihilated."
(John Cowper Powys, Autobiography, 1934, Faber and Faber, p. 169-170)

E1.5.D.1.2.d. THIS OBJECT CONSISTS OF A BOOK THAT LOOKS LIKE A SKY ATLAS. However, the representation of stars and galaxies is presented in intersecting plans, suggesting that there are multiple dimensions. On the open atlas is a shiny metal structure that sketches straight lines that one can imagine extending indefinitely in our dimension. The object seems to be a call for us to project ourselves outside of ourselves, beyond local "truths" and determinisms that obstruct our freedom to think.

E1.5.D.1.3. FOR SPINOZA, EVERY REALITY IS SUBJECT TO WONDER, because it is the result of innumerable circumstances. The error consists in taking ourselves to be the center of the world and judging the world. Spinozism is a devaluation of consciousness and a deconstruction of subjectivity, without dissolving our essence: when we participate "only in ourselves", we are never free - we become free when we participate in nature, i.e. in a power greater than ourselves.

E1.5.D.1.3.a. WE MUST LEARN TO SEE THE WORLD SEPARATELY FROM OUR NEED FOR IT.
Friedrich Nietszche said that it's by forgetting ourselves enough that we produce Le Gai Savoir. We must learn to see the world separately from our need for it, by fighting our natural tendency to limit our freedom and joy: "Man is a worshipping animal".

E1.5.D.1.3.a_REF1_CUR THE LIBRARY OF LOVE is a collection of artists' books on the theme of love, featuring the work of 200 artists gathered under the initiative of Sandra Cinto. Victor Leguy presents two books lying flat on a table, separated by a frame placed at right angles between them. Since the cover of one of the books appears to be the mirror image of the other, we get the impression that the frame contains a mirror, but it's actually an empty frame. There are in fact two books, and not one a reflection of the other. Thanks to this device, Leguy makes us think that "Insacro" [the non-sacred] is the opposite or mirror image of "Narciso" [Narcissus]. Is it because Narcissus sacralizes his own image, whereas joy blossoms when we are in love and consider as sacred what is outside ourselves?

E1.5.D.1.3.a_REF2_LIT "I HAD JUST BEGUN TO PONDER OVER THE FACT THAT ALL THE THINGS WHICH I HAD FOUND TO BE SOURCES OF HAPPINESS seemed to depend upon the capacity to relax all straining, to widen my attention beyond the circle of personal interest, and to look detachedly at my own experience. I had just realized that this relaxing and detachment must depend on a fundamental sense of security, and yet that I could apparently never feel safe enough to do it, because there was an urge in me which I had dimly perceived but had never yet been able to face. It was then that the idea occurred to me that until you have, once at least, faced everything you know — the whole universe — with utter giving in, and let all that is “not you” flow over and engulf you, there can be no lasting sense of security."
(Marion Milner,  A Life of One’s Own, 1934)

E1.5.D.1.3.a_REF3_PO "DIARY (SUMMER 2007) 

[...]

The 14th, morning 
as I read on the balcony 
several horses pass in front of the house 
black horses, solid, fast 
I would love to ask them 
what to do with my desire."
(Ana Martins Marques, the other night, Life under water, 2009

E1.5.D.1.3.a_REF4_PH VERMEER'S ART IS CHARACTERIZED BY ITS FOCUS ON OBJECTS RATHER THAN EVENTS, CAPTURING THE ESSENCE OF REALITY WHERE NOTABLE EVENTS ARE SECONDARY. His work, devoid of ego and self-portraits, reflects a joy of existence and indifference to self, making the invisible visible.
"One of the characteristics of Vermeer's art - as perhaps of all art that has reached a certain degree of nobility - is that he paints things, not events. The world that Vermeer perceives is not that of insignificant events, forever mute, but that of matter, eternally rich and alive. The anecdotal, one might say, has driven out the anecdotal: the random occurrence of a moment in the day, in a room where nothing of importance is happening, appears as the essential of a reality of which seemingly notable events are, on the contrary, the accessory part. The ego is absent from the reality Vermeer captures, because the ego is just one event among others, like them mute and like them insignificant. Moreover, there is no self-portrait by Vermeer, and the painter's biography consists of ten anodyne verses. However, Vermeer does seem to have painted himself once. In a game of double mirrors: in this painting without a precise name, now called The Studio. But from behind, as an ordinary painter, who could be any other person busy with his canvas. There is nothing in the painter's costume, stature or attitude that could be considered a distinctive sign, nothing that would indicate any complacency on the part of the painter with regard to his own person. At the same time, this Studio - like all Vermeer's canvases - seems rich in a joy of existence that radiates from all sides and immediately seizes the viewer, testifying to a perpetual jubilation at the sight of things: judging by this moment of happiness, it's easy to convince oneself that whoever did this, if he captured only one moment of his joy on canvas, would have gladly done the same for the moment before and the moment after. He only lacked the time to celebrate all moments and all things. 
It would certainly be an exaggeration to derive this joy solely from the abandonment of one's own specificity, from the discovery that the self, as a singular being, is of interest not only to no one else, but not even to myself, who can only benefit from dispensing with my image. This indifference to oneself is more effect than cause here: it signals bliss rather than provokes it. But the link between the enjoyment of life and indifference to self is no less clear. 
In a way, the painter of the Studio has made the invisible visible: he has painted his absence, better rendered in this way than if he had simply renounced any form of self-portraiture. When nothing is said, it's always possible to imagine some ulterior motive. This is not the case here: nothingness is said here in full, and spread out in full view on the canvas. If not nothingness, at least a very little, a notable something."
(Clément Rosset, Reality and its double, 1976)

E1.5.D.1.3.b. HE WHO INTERROGATES HIMSELF ALWAYS GETS THE WRONG ANSWER. Thought that only contemplates itself is only boredom, or sadness, or anxiety, or impatience. Give it a try.

E1.5.D.1.3.b_REF1_PH ALAIN CRITICIZES EXCESSIVE INTROSPECTION AS A SOURCE OF BOREDOM AND SADNESS, and insists on the importance of the will to transform desire into action. 
"Hegel says that the immediate or natural soul is always shrouded in melancholy and overwhelmed. This seemed very profound to me. When self-reflection doesn't clear things up, it's a nocive game. And those who question themselves always give bad answers. Thought that only contemplates itself is nothing but boredom or sadness or worry or impatience. Try it. Ask yourself: "What could I read to pass the time?" You're already yawning. It's time to start. Desire fades if it doesn't end in will. And these remarks are enough to condemn the psychologists who would have us all curiously study our own thoughts, as if we were studying herbs or shells. But to think is to will. [...]
Why should love be any different? Love is not natural; and desire itself is not natural for long. But real feelings are works of art. You don't play cards and throw them away at the first sign of impatience or boredom; and no one ever had the idea of playing the piano at random. Music, in fact, is the best example of all; for it is maintained, even in song, only by the will, and grace comes afterwards, as theologians have sometimes said, without really knowing what they were talking about.
(Alain, On Happiness, September 10, 1913)

E1.5.D.1.3.b_REF1.1_CUR IN THIS SURREALIST PAINTING, Ismael Nery shows us a silhouette with a sad attitude that seems to embrace itself. The result is eerie. Is it, as Alain tells us, because "those who question themselves always give bad answers. Thought that only looks at itself is nothing but boredom or sadness or worry or impatience. Try it."
(Alain, On Happiness, 1913)

E1.5.D.1.3.c. "NOR IS THERE COMPLETE SATISFACTION WHEN ONE CONCEIVES THE WORLD AS RAW MATERIAL FOR THE GLORIFICATION OF ONE'S OWN SELF."

E1.5.D.1.3.c_REF1_PH “ALEXANDER THE GREAT PSYCHOLOGICALLY BELONGED TO THE SAME TYPE AS THE LUNATIC, although he possessed the talent necessary to realize the lunatic's dream. He was unable, however, to realize his own aspirations, whose scope widened as they were realized. When he saw that he was the most illustrious conqueror in the world, he concluded that he was a god. Was he happy? His drunkenness, his fits of rage, his indifference to women, all suggest otherwise. There is no complete satisfaction in cultivating one element of human nature at the expense of all the others, nor is there complete satisfaction in conceiving the world as raw material for the glorification of one's own self.”
(Bertrand Russell, The Conquest of Happiness, 1962)

E1.5.D.1.3.c_REF2_PH ERICH FROMM ARGUES THAT TRUE HUMAN DEVELOPMENT REQUIRES SHIFTING FROM A POSSESSION-CENTERED TO AN ACTIVITY-CENTERED ORIENTATION, EMPHASIZING SOLIDARITY AND ALTRUISM.
"The full humanization of man requires the breakthrough from the possession-centered to the activity-centered orientation, from selfishness and egotism to solidarity and altruism.[...]
Even if we had no theoretical knowledge about the reasons for the norms that are conducive to man’s optimal growth and functioning, experience tells us just as much as it tells the gardener. Therein lies the reason that all great teachers of man have arrived at essentially the same norms for living, the essence of these norms being that the overcoming of greed, illusions, and hate, and the attainment of love and compassion, are the conditions for attaining optimal being. Drawing conclusions from empirical evidence, even if we cannot explain the evidence theoretically, is a perfectly sound and by no means “unscientific” method, although the scientists’ ideal will remain, to discover the laws behind the empirical evidence."
(Erich Fromm, The Art of Being)

E1.5.D.1.3.c_REF2.1_PH ERICH FROMM ARGUES THAT TRUE LIBERATION INVOLVES FREEING ONESELF FROM BOTH EXTERNAL AND INTERNAL CHAINS. While political and social liberation addresses external forces like feudalism, capitalism, and imperialism, internal chains are more insidious as they are embedded within one's desires and thoughts, often without awareness. 
"Liberation has been exclusively applied to liberation from outside forces; by the middle class from feudalism, by the working class from capitalism, by the peoples in Africa and Asia from imperialism. [...]
This is the case in Western democracy, where political liberation hides the fact of dependency in many disguises… Man can be a slave even without being put in chains… The outer chains have simply been put inside of man. The desires and thoughts that the suggestion apparatus of society fills him with, chain him more thoroughly than outer chains. This is so because man can at least be aware of outer chains but be unaware of inner chains, carrying them with the illusion that he is free. He can try to overthrow the outer chains, but how can he rid himself of chains of whose existence he is unaware?
Any attempt to overcome the possibly fatal crisis of the industrialized part of the world, and perhaps of the human race, must begin with the understanding of the nature of both outer and inner chains; it must be based on the liberation of man in the classic, humanist sense as well as in the modern, political and social sense… The only realistic aim is total liberation, a goal that may well be called radical (or revolutionary) humanism. [...]
If “well-being” — [defined as] functioning well as a person, not as an instrument — is the supreme goal of one’s efforts, two specific ways stand out that lead to the attainment of this goal: Breaking through one’s narcissism and breaking through the property structure of one’s existence."
(Erich Fromm, The Art of Being, 1989)

E1.5.D.1.3.c_REF2.2_PH THE NARCISSISTIC PERSON HAS BUILT AN INVISIBLE WALL AROUND HIMSELF. Narcissism is an orientation in which all one’s interest and passion are directed to one’s own person: one’s body, mind, feelings, interests… For the narcissistic person, only he and what concerns him are fully real; what is outside, what concerns others, is real only in a superficial sense of perception; that is to say, it is real for one’s senses and for one’s intellect. But it is not real in a deeper sense, for our feeling or understanding. He is, in fact, aware only of what is outside, inasmuch as it affects him. Hence, he has no love, no compassion, no rational, objective judgment. The narcissistic person has built an invisible wall around himself. He is everything, the world is nothing. Or rather: He is the world. Fromm writes:
"A person living in this mode is not necessarily very narcissistic. He may have broken through the shell of his narcissism, have an adequate appreciation of reality outside himself, not necessarily be “in love with himself”; he knows who he is and who the others are, and can well distinguish between subjective experience and reality. Nevertheless, he wants everything for himself; has no pleasure in giving, in sharing, in solidarity, in cooperation, in love. He is a closed fortress, suspicious of others, eager to take and most reluctant to give."
(Erich Fromm, The Art of Being reviewed by Maria Popova, Brain Pickings)

E1.5.D.1.3.c_REF2.3_PH "IF A PERSON HAS THE WILL AND THE DETERMINATION TO LOOSEN THE BARS OF HIS PRISON OF NARCISSISM AND SELFISHNESS, when he has the courage to tolerate the intermittent anxiety, he experiences the first glimpses of joy and strength that he sometimes attains. And only then a decisive new factor enters into the dynamics of the process. This new experience becomes the decisive motivation for going ahead and following the path he has charted… [An] experience of well-being — fleeting and small as it may be — … becomes the most powerful motivation for further progress…
Awareness, will, practice, tolerance of fear and of new experience, they are all necessary if transformation of the individual is to succeed. At a certain point the energy and direction of inner forces have changed to the point where an individual’s sense of identity has changed, too. In the property mode of existence the motto is: “I am what I have.” After the breakthrough it is “I am what I do” (in the sense of unalienated activity); or simply, “I am what I am.”"
(Erich Fromm, The Art of Being)

E1.5.D.1.3.c_REF2.4_CUR EVERYTHING THAT IS USUALLY AT THE CENTER IS TURNED AROUND. Jana Euler explains what guided the construction of this painting: "Everything that is usually at the center is turned around; the center begins beyond the borders of my own vision".

E1.5.D.1.3.c_REF3_PH "MAKE YOUR INTERESTS GRADUALLY WIDER AND MORE IMPERSONAL, until bit by bit the walls of the ego recede, and your life becomes increasingly merged in the universal life. An individual human existence should be like a river — small at first, narrowly contained within its banks, and rushing passionately past rocks and over waterfalls. Gradually the river grows wider, the banks recede, the waters flow more quietly, and in the end, without any visible break, they become merged in the sea, and painlessly lose their individual being."
(Bertrand Russell, “How to Grow Old,” Portraits from Memory and Other Essays, 1956)

E1.5.D.1.3.c_REF4_PO "TO OLD AGE
I see in you the estuary that enlarges and spreads itself grandly as it pours in the great sea."
(Walt Whitman, Leaves of grass, 1855)

E1.5.D.1.3.d. YET IT'S SO HARD TO GIVE UP ON THE SELF...

E1.5.D.1.3.d.i. "I KNOW, I GIVE UP EVERYTHING, I DON'T GIVE UP MY “SELF”. IT'S THE CONTRARY OF WHAT I SHOULD BE DOING. [...]
This "I" should renounce, it should abdicate. One should let oneself go. "I" is prevented from letting go; desires arise despite myself, an anxious "me" stands against the serene "me", in which "me" am I? I want to know. I am driven to know. I can't help but desire to know. I am not the master of my desires or my sadness or my anxiety. Or, my anxieties, my nostalgias, my distresses are a self refusing to be serene. Is my anxiety another’s, is my anxiety myself? [...]
I declare that knowledge is useless. I declare that the sciences do not penetrate the essence of being. I don't care about not knowing anything because knowledge is "worldly". The worldly does not interest me: it is what is done, it is what is made, it is what moves. The mechanism of restlessness does not interest me: what is behind, it's Him or It, the Unknown which is alone worthy of our interest. Not science, knowledge. Knowledge is impossible. But I can't resign myself to only knowing the walls of the prison."
(Eugène Ionesco, Journal en miettes [Diary in shreds], 1967)

E1.5.D.1.3.d.ii. PRISONERS OF SUBJECTIVITY
According to Rousseau, the pre-social savage harbored a sense of existence within himself, unaffected by the perceptions of others. In contrast, civilized man only experiences this sense of existence externally, constantly seeking approval from others. He is, therefore, a prisoner of subjectivity.

E1.5.D.1.3.d.iii. THE FAILURE OF THOSE WHO CANNOT FORGET THEMSELVES OUT OF PRIDE

E1.5.D.1.3.d.iii_REF1_PH ACUTELY AWARE OF THE FUTILITY OF HUMAN RESTLESSNESS, OBLOMOV CHOOSES TO LIVE IN STILLNESS IN ORDER TO PRESERVE HIS ESSENCE. Oblomov's failure lies in his inability to distance himself from his "self" and fully embrace the human condition.
"Fatigue - Goncharov's Oblomov, the Lying Man
Seemingly burlesque, as in Beckett, the nonsense of Oblomov's behavior - the man who lives lying down on his sofa - conceals the tragedy of his realization of human nature. The man who sleeps is truly and completely himself. As long as he is only a critical, intensified consciousness, he has not yet become fundamentally human. The mockery he displays is very Russian: it testifies to his determination never to forget what's essential. Grief is at the heart of his character; he can't get rid of it, and that's why he won't marry Olga, with whom he's in love - but who would deprive him of living out his anguished essence. He's not a lazy man, he's someone who's aware that he's been slowly fading since birth, and he's weighed down by nothing, nothing holds him. He suffers from inner annihilation when he realizes that the end of the restlessness of others is only rest. So what's the point? French literature prefers to be clever with Voltaire and Marivaux. But isn't Oblomov right in his awareness that even in a purely rationalist attitude we are constantly under attack, humiliated, tired, exhausted? At the end of his life, Oblomov decides to take refuge in the loveless everyday life of his landlady. He has decided to stop experiencing "flashes of great joy" because he can no longer bear the pain that follows. With extreme awareness, he renounces what he once believed in. He renounces his human condition, that which is superior in man. He lies down in 'the spacious coffin of the rest of his days'. In the novel, however, Olga is joyful-enchanted by the simplicity of things-even as she is overcome by melancholy and the question of whether things will always satisfy her at the end of the novel. She has less pride than Oblomov, and she accuses him of renouncing their love out of pride. Is thiss the key: is the failure to distance himself from his 'self' Oblomov's great failure?"
(Pierre Cahné on Les Chemins de la Philosophie, broadcast on France Culture on 12/19/2017)

Listen to the Podcast: https://www.radiofrance.fr/franceculture/podcasts/les-chemins-de-la-philosophie/oblomov-de-gontcharov-l-homme-couche-8192866

E1.5.D.2. IF WE ARE DETERMINED, HOW CAN WE BRING ABOUT CHANGE?
Instead of thinking in terms of self, consider the whole. This shift in perspective requires a leap that comes with knowledge.

E1.5.D.2.1. THE MORE WE UNDERSTAND OUR DETERMINATIONS, THE FREER WE ARE TO NO LONGER BE BOUND BY THEM.
Spinoza's Ethics is a journey. The wise man, though still subject to alienation, possesses more adequate ideas. We never cease to think autonomously about ourselves, others, and the world. Until we have traversed the path set out in Ethics, we exist not in immanence but in a form of transcendence. Immanence is not an innate state. Transcendence is a common illusion, and immanence must be achieved. We need to rekindle our intuition of nature as a unity.

E1.5.D.2.1.a. FREEDOM IS THE INCREASE IN POWER THAT FREES US FROM DETERMINISM: in the Ethics, Spinoza explains how the joy born of knowledge enables us to gradually reduce the burden of sad affects.

E1.5.D.2.1.a_REF1_PH For BARUCH SPINOZA, it's not about climbing the mountain of self-liberation through willpower, but rather about emotional intelligence. When I understand something that is accompanied by affect - and not just in an intellectual mode - I develop an understanding that increases my power to act; I'm less affected by sadness, and so I'm freer. The joy born of knowledge is stronger than the sad affect - and so, through intelligence, the latter has less and less hold on me. Lu Yang's Power of Will illustrates the limits of voluntary knowledge.

E1.5.D.2.1.a_REF2_PH "PROPOSITION 14
A true knowledge of good and evil cannot check any emotion by virtue of being true, but only in so far as it is considered as an emotion.

PROOF
An emotion is an idea, whereby the mind affirms of its body a greater or less force of existing than before (by the General Definition of the Emotions) ; therefore it has no positive quality, which can be destroyed by the presence of what is true ; consequently the knowledge of good and evil cannot, by virtue of being true, restrain any emotion. But, in so far as such knowledge is an emotion [...] if it have more strength for restraining emotion, it will to that extent be able to restrain the given emotion [...]"
(Baruch Spinoza, The Ethics, Part Four)

Read the Ethics online: http://app.ethica-spinoza.net/en/text

E1.5.D.2.1.a_REF3_PH FREEDOM IS THE GAIN IN POWER THAT LIBERATES US FROM DETERMINISM. SPINOZA EXPLAINS HOW THE JOY ARISING FROM KNOWLEDGE ENABLES US TO GRADUALLY LESSEN THE WEIGHT OF NEGATIVE EMOTIONS.
"PROPOSITION 6
The mind has greater power over the emotions and is less subject thereto, in so far as it understands all things as necessary.

PROOF
The mind understands all things to be necessary [...] and to be determined to existence and operation by an infinite chain of causes [...]; therefore [...], it thus far brings it about, that it is less subject to the emotions arising therefrom, and [...] feels less emotion towards the things themselves."
(Baruch Spinoza, The Ethics, Part Five)

PROPOSITION 7
Emotions which are aroused or spring from reason, if we take account of time, are stronger than those, which are attributable to particular objects that we regard as absent.

PROOF
We do not regard a thing as absent, by reason of the emotion wherewith we conceive it, but by reason of the body, being affected by another emotion excluding the existence of the said thing [...]. Wherefore, the emotion, which is referred to the thing which we regard as absent, is not of a nature to overcome the rest of a man's activities and power [...], but is, on the contrary, of a nature to be in some sort controlled by the emotions, which exclude the existence of its external cause [...]. But an emotion which springs from reason is necessarily referred to the common properties of things [...], which we always regard as present (for there can be nothing to exclude their present existence), and which we always conceive in the same manner [...]. Wherefore an emotion of this kind always remains the same ; and consequently [...] emotions, which are contrary thereto and are not kept going by their external causes, will be obliged to adapt themselves to it more and more, until they are no longer contrary to it ; to this extent the emotion which springs from reason is more powerful."

Read the Ethics online: http://app.ethica-spinoza.net/en/text

E1.5.D.2.1.b. FREEING OURSELVES FROM COLLECTIVE THOUGHT AND FROM MEMORY (PAST THOUGHTS AND PAST FEELINGS)

E1.5.D.2.1.b_REF1_PH DAVID BOHM DISCUSSES THE DISTINCTION BETWEEN "THINKING" (AN ACTIVE PRESENT PROCESS) AND "THOUGHT" (A PAST PARTICIPLE THAT ACTS AUTOMATICALLY FROM MEMORY). He extends this to "feeling" and introduces "felts" as recorded feelings from the past. Bohm emphasizes that thoughts and felts are intertwined processes originating from memory, affecting both intellectual and physical states, and critiques the cultural misconception that they can be separated and controlled independently.
"In our language we have a distinction between « thinking » and « thought ». « Thinking » implies the present tense — some activity going on which may include critical sensitivity to what can go wrong. [...] "Thought" is the past participle of that. We have the idea that after we have been thinking something, it just evaporates. But thinking doesn't disappear. It goes somehow into the brain and leaves something — a trace — which becomes thought. And thought then acts automatically. So thought is the response from memory from the past, from what has been done. Thus we have thinking and thought. We also have the word « feeling." Its present tense suggests the active present, that the feeling is directly in contact with reality. But it might be useful to introduce the word « felt », to say there are « feelings » and « felts ». That is, "felts" are feelings which have been recorded. A traumatic experience in the past can make you feel very uncomfortable when remembered. Nostalgic feelings are also from the past. A lot of the feelings that come up are really from the past, they're "felts." But if they are just a recording being replayed, they don't have as much significance as if they were a response to the present immediate situation.
It's really very important to see that our culture gives us a wrong lead about thoughts and felts. It constantly tends to imply that they could be separated and that one could control the other. But thoughts and felts are one process; they are not two. They both come from the memory; in the memory they are probably all mixed. Memory also affects the physical body. It affects the sensations. You can produce states of stress in the body from memory of states of stress. Therefore, when memory acts you cannot separate the intellectual function, the emotional function, the chemical function, the muscular function — because this tacit knowledge is also a kind of memory — they're all there." 
(David Bohm, On dialogue, 1996)

E1.7.C.3.1.b_REF4_SCI.wE1.5.D.2.1.b_REF1_PH

E1.5.D.2.1.b_REF2_PH "THE NATURE OF COLLECTIVE THOUGHT [...]
The real crisis is not in these events which are confronting us, like wars and crime and drugs and economic chaos and pollution; it's really in the thought which is making it — all the time. [...]
The kind of thought that's going on all around us begins to take over in every one of us, without our even noticing it. It's spreading like a virus and each one of us is nourishing that virus. Do we have a kind of immune system that stops it ? The only way to stop it is to recognize it, to acknowledge it, to see what it is. [...]
We may imagine that the source of the problem is that somebody "over there" is thinking these wrong thoughts or that a lot of people are. But the source of the problem is much deeper. It is that something is going wrong in the whole process of thought, which is collective, which belongs to all of us. [...]

Further, in our language we have a distinction between "thinking" and "thought." "Thinking" implies the present tense — some activity going on which may include critical sensitivity to what can go wrong. Also there may be new ideas, and perhaps occasionally perception of some kind inside. "Thought" is the past participle of that. We have the idea that after we have been thinking something, it just evaporates. But thinking doesn't disappear. It goes somehow into the brain and leaves something — a trace — which becomes thought. And thought then acts automatically. So thought is the response from memory from the past, from what has been done. Thus we have thinking and thought. We also have the word "feeling." Its present tense suggests the active present, that the feeling is directly in contact with reality. But it might be useful to introduce the word "felt," to say there are "feelings" and "felts." That is, "felts" are feelings which have been recorded. A traumatic experience in the past can make you feel very uncomfortable when remembered. Nostalgic feelings are also from the past. A lot of the feelings that come up are really from the past, they're "felts." But if they are just a recording being replayed, they don't have as much significance as if they were a response to the present immediate situation.
It's really very important to see that our culture gives us a wrong lead about thoughts and felts. It constantly tends to imply that they could be separated and that one could control the other. But thoughts and felts are one process; they are not two. They both come from the memory; in the memory they are probably all mixed. Memory also affects the physical body. It affects the sensations. You can produce states of stress in the body from memory of states of stress. Therefore, when memory acts you cannot separate the intellectual function, the emotional function, the chemical function, the muscular function-because this tacit knowledge is also a kind of memory — they're all there." 
(David Bohm, On dialogue, 1996)

E1.5.D.2.1.b_REF2.1_PH "THE NATURE OF COLLECTIVE THOUGHT
The real crisis is not in these events which are confronting us, like wars and crime and drugs and economic chaos and pollution; it's really in the thought which is making it — all the time. [...]
The kind of thought that's going on all around us begins to take over in every one of us, without our even noticing it. It's spreading like a virus and each one of us is nourishing that virus. Do we have a kind of immune system that stops it ? The only way to stop it is to recognize it, to acknowledge it, to see what it is. [...]
We may imagine that the source of the problem is that somebody "over there" is thinking these wrong thoughts or that a lot of people are. But the source of the problem is much deeper. It is that something is going wrong in the whole process of thought, which is collective, which belongs to all of us."
(David Bohm, On dialogue, 1996)

E1.5.D.2.2. EVERYONE MUST INVENT THEIR OWN WAY, as Spinoza provides a guide to understanding one's own emotions, rather than emotions in general. This is not a prescriptive path, even though it's a philosophy that offers little freedom outside its outlined route. This is done explicitly to achieve freedom.

E1.5.D.2.2.a. AN EVER-INCREASING ADHERENCE TO THE LIFE THAT EXPRESSES ITSELF IN OUR INDIVIDUALITY.
"Spinoza signifies that an infinite intensification of our body's consciousness is possible by writing that Desire is the consciousness of Appetite, or of the need expressed by the Body. Despite being only a part of Nature, we can continually elevate our adherence to the life that expresses itself in our individuality."

E1.5.D.2.2.a_REF1_PH "EVENTUALLY THE SADNESS WILL BECOME SECONDARY. If I try to fight it, I'm just adding to it. I'm angry, I don't want to be angry - I'm angry at my anger, and it's kind of a vicious cycle. Instead of focusing on my anger, I'm going to talk to this person I love, I'm going to read these texts that nourish me, and as a result, the sad passion will take up less and less space in my existence. When we achieve bliss, other affects will actually take up less space in our lives without us even having to think about them - and especially if we don't think about them at all. It's not a command to be joyful. If we try to be joyful, we miss out on joy, and we even run the risk of encountering sadness (because we can't make ourselves happy). It's not a matter of willpower, it's a matter of encounters, and joy will come if we do certain things. We begin by proposing to ourselves - and to others - possibilities and rules for living that become habits and bring joy. Spinoza says in Part 3 of the Ethics that in order for something to be possible for oneself, it must already be imagined as possible. What is good is what we know to be useful for ourselves (and not what we believe to be useful for ourselves), that is, what increases our power to act. This is the connection between virtue and bliss."
(Julie Henry on Les Chemins de la Philosophie, France Culture, 06/05/2020)

E1.5.D.2.2.a_REF1.1_PH "BEATITUDE IS NOT THE PRICE OF VIRTUE, BUT VIRTUE ITSELF.
"Beatitude is the keystone of this text. The term 'pedagogy' is something we find in Part 5 of the Ethics. When we talk about eternity, Spinoza tells us that it hasn't begun - by definition it can't have a beginning - and at the same time we're going to pretend that it has a beginning, because that's what will actually enable us to imagine or better understand the path to beatitude. The end of the Ethics helps us to understand the path that Spinoza has been leading us on throughout the first 4 parts of the Ethics. If, to be precise, 'beatitude is not the reward of virtue, but virtue itself,' then the end was not something far away, ahead of us, that we could hope to reach someday, and perhaps not even in this life. It was something that had been with us all along the way, and this is perhaps the first connection between joy and beatitude. If joy is the passage to a higher perfection, then it's that joy that has been with us all along the way, with the idea that beatitude is not a state in which we rest - as if there's some kind of movement and then we settle down and are in eternal rest - but rather something that has guided us and continues to guide us. In fact, I find that the end of Ethics is also like a new beginning, inviting us to continue. 

On the last page of the Ethics, Spinoza distinguishes between the wise and the ignorant. To what extent is this distinction a distinction of nature, or a matter of degree? Can the ignorant that we are ever claim to be wise? I find it very interesting that at the very end of the text Spinoza speaks of a 'path' - what he has shown us is not a model to follow, but rather a kind of path we can follow. He says it is difficult, and it is difficult even for the wise. In part 5, he says that the wise man knows the turmoil of emotions, that he is likely to have moments of doubt, and that he can be overwhelmed by his passions: 'Because they are wise, they are no less men;' this could be the epitaph of ethics... We can all progress toward a certain wisdom, and at the same time the ignorant man is still not as worthy as the wise man. This is not to say that the ignorant are unworthy human beings - it's all part of nature - but in the Treatise on Political Theology he tells us that the ignorant have as much right to live according to their ignorance as the wise have to live according to their wisdom. But it's true that he invites us to walk a certain path with him, to take us by the hand, as it were, especially when he uses the personal pronoun 'we,' which gives us the impression that we are all part of the same community and that we can progress with him. It's not something that's absolutized or substantiated - there's not the wise man on one side and the ignorant man on the other.

We've insisted on Spinoza's willingness to take man as he is, never a god or an animal; he is truly a man, pervaded by all the passions that constitute him. Isn't there a discrepancy between this ultimately realistic concern and the geometric form of ethics? There's something very logical, almost dry, that doesn't seem to fit with who we are - because we're not made of reason and logic alone.

I have the impression that there's a double register: it's true that there's something cold in the form, but at the same time it's quite logical, because Spinoza says that human passions can be studied like other natural phenomena. There's a logic to the way they work, so there's no point in laughing at people or despising them. Just as big fish eat little fish, so men are what they are. At the same time, I find that in the Scolies, with the examples of the everyday that he takes - the chicken in the neighbor's coop, the apple I'm holding, etc. - There's a way to make sense of the mundane. - There's a way to be sensitive to a discourse that may not seem to us to be addressed to us. There's always this attention to people - to what they do, to what they are, to what they can do - and at the same time always a demand; with attention to the human being and his resources - Spinoza tells us: we can do something even if we are determined by nature, thanks to the work of reason. It's through the same movement that we come to know ourselves and nature, so that we can learn to act differently. 

For Spinoza, salvation is immanent. Beatitude, eternity, salvation, these 3 concepts go together, and I find it a little less difficult to understand if we focus on the concept of eternity. It would be paradoxical to say to ourselves: 'I will have access to eternity after my death,' because eternity has no before or after. Spinoza's eternity is the exact opposite of the fear of dying and the hope that it will continue after death. Spinoza suggests that we encounter eternity in this life. Thus, to attain bliss is to enter eternity: bliss is a form of eternal joy. Since joy is always the passage to a higher perfection, and beatitude is perfection, it's as if beatitude were a form of joy anchored in eternity. A joy that would know no variation or increase, for it would be perfection itself. It remains to be seen whether we can encounter bliss in this world... In my opinion, we can, in the same way that we encounter eternity, i.e. not as something that is built up by degrees - a plateau to which we gain access - but rather as something of the order of an encounter. The mistake is to think that adding more and more accumulated time will result in eternity, whereas eternity has nothing to do with time in the sense of something that can have a before and an after.

In the 11th proposition of the third part of the Ethics, Spinoza speaks of tickling, which immediately evokes something physical. Does tickling gradually bring us closer to pleasure? The Latin term titillatio refers to a stimulation that's irritating because it appeals to one part of the body to the detriment of the others. Joy, on the other hand, increases my capacity to act. Joy is the passage to a higher perfection, and for Spinoza, being and perfection are one and the same-not in the sense of being satisfied with what is, which would be paradoxical with the path ethics shows us-but in the sense that our being, our conatus, tends to increase our power to act. It is a power that is not power. Power, potestas in Latin, is something that is 'exercised over' and contains the idea of taking possession of another, a form of authority that is not always benevolent. Potency, potencia in Latin, 'is exercised with'. When I increase my potency of being, I don't decrease yours. I increase my ability to be influenced by you and to influence you, so that together we can have even greater power. While power is usually exercised at the expense of others, potency is really about openness. As potency is expressed, it is shared with others.

This is a very important point, because we might think that the journey Spinoza invites us to take in slow steps is all about the self, whereas he's telling us that understanding relationships with others is indispensable for access to that very beatitude; without it, my potency will be inferior. I'm referring to another text - the Treatise on the Reform of the Understanding - where he says: 'The true and supreme good, that is the end to which I must tend. To acquire this superior human nature, and to make every effort that many others may acquire it with me. [...] It is important for my happiness that many others rise with me to the same thoughts as I do, so that their thoughts and understanding are in harmony with mine.' It's up to each of us to make the journey for ourselves, but it will be easier if we are accompanied by others. 

Eventually the sadness will become secondary. If I try to fight it, I'm just adding to it. I'm angry, I don't want to be angry - I'm angry at my anger, and it's kind of a vicious cycle. Instead of focusing on my anger, I'm going to talk to this person I love, I'm going to read these texts that nourish me, and as a result, the sad passion will take up less and less space in my existence. When we achieve bliss, other affects will actually take up less space in our lives without us even having to think about them - and especially if we don't think about them at all. It's not a command to be joyful. If we try to be joyful, we miss out on joy, and we even run the risk of encountering sadness (because we can't make ourselves happy). It's not a matter of willpower, it's a matter of encounters, and joy will come if we do certain things. We begin by proposing to ourselves - and to others - possibilities and rules for living that become habits and bring joy. Spinoza says in Part 3 of the Ethics that in order for something to be possible for oneself, it must already be imagined as possible. What is good is what we know to be useful for ourselves (and not what we believe to be useful for ourselves), that is, what increases our power to act. This is the connection between virtue and bliss.

Spinoza's presupposition of the love of existence is not self-evident - an unquestioned potency of affirmation. Spinoza does not believe that we can act against ourselves with full knowledge of the facts. There's no room for disgust with existence, or even for neutrality - there's always movement in Spinoza, and little room for chosen rest - to continue to be, you have to keep increasing your potency to act.
(Julie Henry on Les Chemins de la Philosophie, France Culture, 06/05/2020)

Listen to the Podcast :
https://www.franceculture.fr/emissions/les-chemins-de-la-philosophie/quatre-malentendus-spinozistes-34-la-beatitude-nest-pas-le-prix-de-la-vertu-mais-la-vertu-elle-meme

E1.5.D.2.2.a_REF2_PH SPINOZA CRITICIZES THE CARTESIAN CONCEPTION OF IDEAS AS IMAGES, EMPHASIZING THAT EVERY IDEA IS AN AFFIRMATION AND NOT A MERE VISION. Spinoza introduces the concept of 'the idea of the idea' to denote the awareness of an idea by a higher-order idea, implying an infinity of degrees of awareness, always partial and perfectible.
"To take ideas for images, as Descartes does, is to confuse the knowledge of understanding with the knowledge, always mutilated and confused, of imagination. Every idea is an affirmation, an act of definition and determination, in other words, a conception, and not merely a vision of a form, even if it is the pure ideality of thought: 'Those who regard ideas as consisting in the images formed in us by the encounter of bodies [...] regard ideas as dumb pictures on a panel and, with their minds occupied by this prejudice, do not see that an idea, insofar as it is an idea, contains an affirmation or a negation" (Note in the demonstration of the corollary of Proposition 49, Part II).
What then of desire, which is appetite with consciousness of itself? To denote consciousness, Spinoza most often uses a singular expression that, as far as I know, belongs to him alone: the idea that has become conscious of itself is 'the idea of the idea'. By this Spinoza means that the idea, in order to become conscious of itself, must make itself the object of a higher-order idea. In this sense, we could say that consciousness is a metapsychology, i.e. a reflection not on psychology but on psychology itself, in the sense that we still speak of 'meta-linguistics' or 'meta-economics'. In this way, a reflection on the geometric order adopted in the Ethics could be said to constitute a kind of 'meta-ethics' (an outline of which can indeed be found in the Treatise on the Reform of Understanding). By preferring this formula ('idea of the idea') to the word 'consciousness,' which has nevertheless been adopted by the philosophical tradition, Spinoza affirms that intellectual evidence is never a perfect vision, but that, on the contrary, there are an infinite number of degrees of consciousness, depending on whether we rise to the level of the idea of the idea, or to the higher level of the idea of the idea of the idea, and so on ad infinitum. Contrary to Descartes' claims, there is no such thing as a perfect act of consciousness, except in God, insofar as He expresses Himself under the attribute of thought. Our consciousness, which is only a part of the immense nature, is always and necessarily partial, and therefore always perfectible. We could say, in this sense, that the idea of the idea is the idea squared, that is, to the power of two, and that the continuation of this power immediately affirms its infinity, hence the possibility of raising the idea to the power of n, as n increases toward infinity. In this way, we can think of the exaltation of the spirit that takes place in God. When Spinoza writes that desire is the consciousness of the appetite, or the need expressed by the body, he means that an infinite intensification of the consciousness we have of our body is possible, an ever greater elevation of our adherence to the life that is expressed in our individuality, we who are nevertheless only a part of nature.
(Jacques Darriulat, Spinoza and the Fullness of this World, 4-"Desire is the essence of man", 2017)

Read the essay in full: http://www.jdarriulat.net/Auteurs/Spinoza/Introduction/Introd4.html

E1.5.D.2.2.a_REF2.1_PH SPINOZA, EXPLAINS THAT OUR ATTACHMENT TO BEING CAN GROW IN INTENSITY INDEFINITELY. Life becomes more assertive as the conscious animal becomes more aware of its potency.
"Thus, our adherence to Being is likely to grow in intensity without it being possible to set a limit to this elevation to higher potency. To live and to exist, which are the names of the Absolute, since life that is in God or in nature has no other end than itself, is therefore an act that is capable of asserting itself more and more in the animal endowed with consciousness, and thus of exalting itself in the knowledge of its own potency. Of course, the mind is not free to act as it wishes, since all our actions are determined by the dense causality of nature, the full expression of God's creative infinity. That's why ethics does not lay down the maxims to be obeyed by righteous action taken with a view to the good: rather, it leads us to an ever brighter and stronger awareness of the necessity in which our individual existence is immersed. And the more we are aware of the conatus that lifts us up and makes us live, the more we adhere to the life that blossoms in our unique individuality, in our own personality. It is not up to us to change the course of things, but it is up to us to approve it, to participate in its creative impulse, to enjoy more and more this infinite affirmation of the Being that surrounds us and animates us within: 'This teaching is useful in that it teaches us that we act by the sole act of God (ex solo dei nutu) and that we share in the divine nature, and this all the more as we do more perfect acts and know God more and more' (Ethics, II, scolie of prop. 49)."
(Jacques Darriulat, Spinoza and the fullness of this world, 4-“Desire is the very essence of man”, 2017)

Read the essay in full: http://www.jdarriulat.net/Auteurs/Spinoza/Introduction/Introd4.html

E1.5.D.2.2.a_REF2.1.a_PH "THIS ENDLESS COLUMN is the earliest extant version of a motif to which Brâncuşi would return over the course of his career. In previous years Brâncuşi had used a doubled pyramid as a base for his sculptures, but he eventually realized that this abstract block could be multiplied into a pillar form, a fully realized work in its own right. Carved from oak, this succession of pyramids has a rhythmic, undulating geometry that suggests the possibility of infinite expansion."
(Curatorship, MomA, NYC)

E1.5.D.2.2.a_REF3_PH "FREEDOM CANNOT CONSIST IN THE NEGATION OF NECESSITY - ON THE CONTRARY, IT IS PURE AFFIRMATION, and there is nothing negative about it - it consists, on the contrary, in the affirmation of this same necessity, in the joyful and powerful participation in the divine necessity that never ceases to recreate the world, and this with no other end in view than that of expressing its infinite, eternal and gratuitous nature. [...]
I am free insofar as I know the world in the same sense as the swimmer knows the wave, that is, from an intuitive, non-discursive knowledge, from a single point of view and not according to the progression of demonstration."
(Jacques Darriulat, Spinoza and the fullness of this world, 4-“Desire is the very essence of man”, 2017)

E1.5.D.2.2.a_REF3.1_PH ACCORDING TO JACQUES DARRIULAT, SPINOZA'S FREEDOM IS NOT IN OPPOSITION TO NATURE, BUT IN PARTICIPATION IN THE IMMANENT DYNAMISM OF THE UNIVERSE. Freedom is the affirmation of divine necessity, an intuitive and non-discursive experience in which man, as part of nature, expresses his power to act in harmony with the universe.
"Perhaps we are now in a better position to understand in what sense participation in the universal life, which overflows on earth as in the universe - 'All things are animated in varying degrees', writes Spinoza (II, 13, scolie), which must be understood in the sense that all things, from stone to man, are lifted up, This is to be understood in the sense that all things, from stone to man, are animated by the impulse of the conatus, which is the life of God, or rather the activity of the living God, which is existence itself - in this sense, this infinite, living necessity does not contradict the experience of freedom, but, on the contrary, is its very object. For the body, whose radius of action expands in an environment without which it could not live, as well as the mind, which also thinks in an intellectual environment that nourishes and stimulates it, is not limited to itself, but is open to immensity, and expresses its own power to act all the more the greater this openness is. According to Spinoza, freedom does not consist in the act of opposing nature and, through one's work, seeking to domesticate it and subject it to one's own interests; on the contrary, it consists in participating in the immanent dynamism that lifts up the universe, not through the will to transform it, but rather through the will to communicate with the infinite life that runs through and through it. Therefore, freedom cannot consist in the negation of necessity - on the contrary, it is pure affirmation, and there is nothing negative about it - it consists, on the contrary, in the affirmation of this same necessity, in the joyful and powerful participation in the divine necessity that never ceases to recreate the world, and this with no other end in view than that of expressing its infinite, eternal and gratuitous nature. [...]
Man, part of nature, is nature itself, immersed in the immense universe. Freedom is the experience of necessity, an internal necessity expressed by the use of my power to act in the proper degree, and an external necessity resulting from the pressure of events that put me in a position to act. Freedom does not consist in the indifference of choice but, on the contrary, in the extreme determination of the act. I am free insofar as I know the world in the same sense as the swimmer knows the wave, that is, from an intuitive, non-discursive knowledge, from a single point of view and not according to the progression of demonstration. The highest knowledge, which is reason, knows the part through the whole, through the adjustment of each of the singular divisions in the whole of the symphony, and not the whole through the concatenation of the parts, which is knowledge of understanding, the constellation of propositions, but not the intuition of the totality".
(Jacques Darriulat, Spinoza and the fullness of this world, 4-“Desire is the very essence of man”, 2017)

Read the essay in full: http://www.jdarriulat.net/Auteurs/Spinoza/Introduction/Introd4.html

E1.5.D.2.2.b. INDIVIDUAL HAPPINESS CAN ONLY BE HINDERED AND BLOCKED BY EXTERNAL PRECEPTS

E1.5.D.2.2.b_REF1_PH “AS LONG AS HE WANTS HIS HAPPINESS, ONE MUST NOT GIVE THE INDIVIDUAL PRECEPTS CONCERNING THE WAY TO HAPPINESS: for individual happiness springs from laws unknown to all, and can only be hindered and stopped by external precepts. - The precepts we call “moral” are really directed against the individual, and have absolutely no desire for happiness. These precepts have just as little to do with 'the happiness and prosperity of mankind' - for it is absolutely impossible to connect these terms with rigorous concepts, and even less possible to use them as a lantern over the obscure ocean of moral aspirations.” 
(Friedrich Nietzsche, Aurora, 1881)

E1.5.D.2.2.c. "DRAWING ON OUR INMOST VITALITY"

E1.5.D.2.2.c_REF1_LIT “WHAT I MEAN IS THAT OUR MODERN PSEUDO-SCIENCE AND PSEUDO-REALISM TEND TO CORRUPT THE UP-MOUNTING SPRING OF LIFE-ACCEPTANCE IN US, which keeps us going in spite the “urchins” of the First Cause. The old religious faith gave our forefathers the stoical habit of drawing their life-energy not from external conditions but from within. That they called this power, welling up from inside themselves by the name of “God” had an historical justification which is lacking to-day. But that is no reason for deserting the living well-spring of mysterious magic within us. Never mind the name! The point is that we have the power of re-creating the universe from the depths of ourselves. In doing so we share the creative force that started the whole process. It is personality, the out-rushing energy of living organisms, that underlies all the criss-cross currents of the world. Every time a living being gathers itself grimly together, and draws on its inmost vitality to be happy in spite of all, it is doing what our sturdy ancestors called “praying”. “ 
(John Cowper Powys, Autobiography, 1934, Faber and Faber, p. 361)

E1.5.D.2.2.c_REF2_LIT MENTAL INDEPENDENCE "My whole art of self-expression in lecturing was as a matter of fact based upon a certain kind of mental independence, an independence which I was always desperately trying to convey to others, for I regarded it—and do still regard it—as one of the most important elements in human happiness and by no means exclusive of the devotion of a scholar to the Past."
