And boasted that he knew 
The language and the ways of men,
And things both old and new. 
The eagle looked him up and down 
With eyes of burning coal: 
« Fly with me then towards the sun 
And hear the thunders roll! »
« I am afraid, » the parrot said. 
The eagle laughed full high: 
« That is a word I have not read 
In earth, or sea, or sky. 
Back to your perch! These lonely heights 
Were not for parrots made. 
I would not leave my eagle flights 
To learn to be afraid! »"
(John Cowper Powys, Autobiography, 1934)

E1.5.E.2.6. CHOOSING ONE'S "SECOND LIFE"

E1.5.E.2.6_REF1_PO "LIFE WHILE-YOU-WAIT

Life While-You-Wait.
Performance without rehearsal.
Body without alterations.
Head without premeditation.

I know nothing of the role I play.
I only know it’s mine. I can’t exchange it.

I have to guess on the spot
just what this play’s all about.

Ill-prepared for the privilege of living,
I can barely keep up with the pace that the action demands.
I improvise, although I loathe improvisation.
I trip at every step over my own ignorance.
I can’t conceal my hayseed manners.
My instincts are for happy histrionics.
Stage fright makes excuses for me, which humiliate me more.
Extenuating circumstances strike me as cruel.

Words and impulses you can’t take back,
stars you’ll never get counted,
your character like a raincoat you button on the run —
the pitiful results of all this unexpectedness.

If only I could just rehearse one Wednesday in advance,
or repeat a single Thursday that has passed!
But here comes Friday with a script I haven’t seen.
Is it fair, I ask
(my voice a little hoarse,
since I couldn’t even clear my throat offstage).

You’d be wrong to think that it’s just a slapdash quiz
taken in makeshift accommodations. Oh no.
I’m standing on the set and I see how strong it is.
The props are surprisingly precise.
The machine rotating the stage has been around even longer.
The farthest galaxies have been turned on.
Oh no, there’s no question, this must be the premiere.
And whatever I do
will become forever what I’ve done."
(Wisława Szymborska)

E1.5.E.2.6_REF1.1_PO KNOWING THINGS EXIST AND NOT KNOWING ANYTHING ABOUT THEM IS KNOWLEDGE? “I like the poem you sent me very much. I know it and sometimes I read it to remind myself of the non-existent rules of life (Is a rule that doesn't exist still a rule? I think so).
It's very interesting - knowing that things exist and knowing almost nothing or little about them (our life). Is it still wisdom? I think so. It may even be superior.”
(Eliza Piotrowska on Life While-you-wait by Wisława Szymborska)

E1.5.E.2.6_REF1.2_LIT AN INVENTED WORD TO ENCAPSULATE THE SENSE THAT THE FUTURE IS ARRIVING AHEAD OF SCHEDULE
“adomania
n. the sense that the future is arriving ahead of schedule, that all those years with fantastical names like '2013' are bursting from their hypothetical cages into the arena of the present, furiously bucking the grip of your expectations while you lean and slip in your saddle, one hand reaching for reins, the other waving up high like a schoolkid who finally knows the answer to the question.”
(John Koenig, The Dictionary of Obscure Sorrows, 2021)

E1.5.E.2.6_REF2_PH "IF THERE IS NO NEW LIFE, IT IS IN THE TURN-AROUND OF ONE'S OWN LIFE, that by correcting what was perhaps poorly chosen in one's life, but above all by putting oneself in the position of being able to choose what was not, one can begin to 'stand outside' - ex-sistere, as the Latin says - outside what conditioned and contained one's life within limits one did not even know one was undergoing: that we can begin to free ourselves from limits we thought were inherently or fatally imposed, and thus begin - in the literal sense of the word - to 'ex-ist. ' "
(François Jullien, A Second Life, 2017)

E1.5.E.2.6_REF2.1_PH "IF THE QUESTION IS: TO WHAT EXTENT CAN WE REGAIN THE INITIATIVE IN LIFE BY STRIPPING IT OF WHAT IT ONCE WAS? - the question itself must be postponed. Or, if this 'regaining initiative' is problematic in itself, have we ever known a first initiative? For if there had to be such a thing as a beginning (an initium), how much of the subject's 'initiative' did it allow? Not only was such a 'subject' under influence (environment, language, education, etc.: all that Descartes' cogito could free itself from), but when we 'entered life,' as we say, we were unable to choose how to live, and had little experience of a first beginning. Or, when we had to make what were then objectified as 'choices' (the way of life, the profession, the love...), we chose mostly blindly: not only did we not know what we were choosing, but above all, we didn't know that we were choosing. There was never that first - terribly abstract - moment when we really began to choose what we 'wanted' to live. Because those 'first' choices we made were only 'choices' in retrospect, they were rather terminal; and what led us to those choices - and first that there was a choice (beginning) there - eluded us".
(François Jullien, A Second Life, 2017)

E1.5.E.2.6_REF2.2_PH BY REVISITING LIFE WITHOUT THE IMPATIENCE OF WHAT'S TO COME, WE BECOME RECEPTIVE TO DETAILS AND INCONGRUITIES, GIVING NEW DEPTH AND A FINER UNDERSTANDING OF EXISTENCE. "But the same is true of the second life: it's in the resumption of the second life that I actually begin to uncover what I'm experiencing, i.e. to pull it out from under that which has obscured it through hasty projection and normalization designed to reassure me. I'm no longer in a hurry to find out what comes next, since I now know roughly what the end is; I'm no longer interested in the events of life and its twists and turns. Just as rereading is no longer about what comes next, but takes its time in the present of the page, second life is no longer about the happening and the impatience of what comes next. It begins to experience the present moment for itself and to hold on to it ("I retaste it," "I hold on to it," as Montaigne said in his last essay). Not only has it become receptive to details and incongruities, no longer overstepping the line, but it has also begun to explore them. For more than the major events of life, which are always the same story, or the general truths about life, which merely repeat the same old wisdom, it is the infinitesimal and the singular that attract and provoke thought - until they finally seem unheard of. For I thought I knew life as well as I thought I knew this novel. But the more I think about it, the more I invest myself in it, the more I realize that I've only glimpsed it in a hasty flight. In the light of the second life, I realize how much life had eluded me before.
(François Jullien, A Second Life, 2017)

E1.5.E.2.6_REF2.3_PH "I'M GOING TO BE MORE CAREFUL OF THE EXPERIENCE I'M GOING TO TAKE, because I'm relying on what the experience I've already gone through, accumulated, has secretly developed in me. In other words, I'm more aware of the difficulties I'll have to face and the resources I'll have to mobilize - in a way that's more in tune with the reality I've already come into contact with. Instead of accepting the stage I've reached, I pull myself together and raise my standards in the light of what I've done. This verb, it's true, is unpretentious. But it is without remorse or resignation, and it is even imbued with hope - hope that is not easy, but that is justified by what I have already done: because I am no longer in the projected intention, but I have already gained a foothold in the actual experience. [...] "
(François Jullien, Une seconde vie, 2017)

E1.5.E.2.7. BUYING TIME

E1.5.E.2.7_REF1_PSI WHILE BUYING TIME MAY INCREASE HAPPINESS FOR AFFLUENT POPULATIONS, IT MAY NOT HAVE THE SAME EFFECT IN FINANCIALLY CONSTRAINED SETTINGS. "Reducing Unpleasant Time Use
Subtracting unpleasant tasks from one's day may be an effective, and underutilized, strategy for promoting happiness. For example, household chores (such as cleaning or mowing the lawn) tend to rank among the least happy activities of the day in descriptive studies of time use (e.g., Han & Kaiser 2022, Kahneman et al. 2004). One way to reduce the time devoted to such tasks would be to simply give up on doing them (or on doing them well), but a more practical strategy may lie in paying for time-saving services. Whillans et al. (2017) tested the potential happiness benefits of this strategy in a within-subject study conducted on two consecutive weekends with 60 working adults in Canada. On one weekend, participants received $40 to spend on any purchase that would save them time, while on another weekend, participants received $40 to spend on a material purchase. Participants reported more positive affect and less negative affect at the end of the day when they made a time-saving purchase compared to the day when they made a material purchase. Participants also reported feeling less pressed for time on the day when they made a time-saving purchase, and this reduction in time stress mediated the effect of spending on mood. Although mediation analyses should always be interpreted with caution, this pattern of results is consistent with the idea that cutting back on unpleasant activities may promote happiness by reducing feelings of time pressure. These benefits do not seem particularly surprising, but when Whillans et al. (2017) asked 98 working adults in Canada how they would spend a $40 windfall, only 2% said they would choose to make a time-saving purchase. This suggests that reducing unpleasant time use, like other forms of subtraction, may be an underutilized strategy in daily life.

Would the documented benefits of buying time extend beyond relatively affluent populations? To put this question to the test, as part of a registered report, Whillans & West (2022) conducted a field experiment with a large sample of working mothers in an urban slum of Kenya (n’s ≈ 360). One group of women received vouchers for laundry services or prepared meals that they could redeem each week for 3 consecutive weeks, saving recipients an average of 3 to 7 hours per week. Meanwhile, a control group of women received no benefit, aside from being paid to complete surveys (a third group received additional money, as discussed in our section below on cash transfers). Using Bayesian analyses, the researchers found evidence supporting the null hypothesis: Women who received time-saving vouchers did not show greater improvements on SWB the week after the intervention ended, compared to women in the control condition. Interestingly, however, exploratory analyses showed that women in both conditions exhibited substantial increases in SWB over the course of the study, perhaps because the additional money all participants earned for completing surveys was sufficient to enhance their happiness. In any case, this carefully conducted registered report provides some important “evidence of absence”: In a severely disadvantaged population, time-saving services did not raise SWB above and beyond the benefits of receiving cash payments for survey completion.

In summary, these two experiments suggest that eliminating unpleasant daily tasks by buying time may be an effective route to increasing happiness for busy working adults in wealthy countries like Canada, but this insight may not generalize well to more financially constrained populations."
(Dunigan Folk and Elizabeth Dunn, How Can People Become Happier? A Systematic Review of Preregistered Experiments, 2023)

Read the full article: https://www.annualreviews.org/doi/10.1146/annurev-psych-022423-030818#abstractSection

E1.5.E.3. TIME : AN "AUTONOMOUS INSTANCE DOMINATING BEINGS" IN WESTERN CIVILISATIONS

E1.5.E.3.1. TIME : AN "AUTONOMOUS INSTANCE DOMINATING BEINGS" IN WESTERN CIVILISATIONS
Western thought, through the construction of Greco-Latin languages, has made time an "autonomous instance dominating beings", as François Jullien helps us to think. By exploring the "differential" of Chinese thought, which had not developed the concept of time until the first contacts with Europe, he helps us to become aware of this determinism, and to move away from the utilitarian "Western" way of life.

E1.5.E.3.1_REF1_PH WESTERNERS CONSIDER TIME in terms of movement and in contrast to eternity, with a morphological structure of past, present and future. Chinese thought, based on the yin-yang polarity, does not conceive of time as a separate entity, but focuses on the processes and transitions of things without wrapping them in a separate notion of time.
"Greece had to think about time for at least three reasons that China lacked. The first of these stems from the bias of its physics (Aristotle, Physics IV): approaching 'nature', phusis, in terms of bodies in motion, it brings in 'time' to account for the movement of a mobile from point A to point B, points of departure and arrival, as we have seen, as 'numbers of movement according to before and after'. But the Chinese approached what we call 'nature' not in terms of bodies in motion or elements, but in terms of correlating factors (yin and yang) from whose polarity all creation flows. Another Greek entry into the concept of time, which rivals the previous one, lies in its metaphysics, which contrasts time with eternity: no longer as a divisible and continuous greatness, but in the combined aspects of succession and infinite change. While only being is eternal, 'always existing', aei ôn, everything 'becomes' in time, the 'moving image' of eternity, at least if we read the formula in this way, whose time has 'fallen' (Plato - Plotinus). China, however, did not think of the 'eternal' of what would always be identical to itself, but of the 'endless' or the 'inexhaustible' (wu qiong) that allows the invested capacity to renew itself without ever running dry - such is the Chinese 'sky' as the background to the process of things. 
Finally, or rather first and foremost, European languages conjugate. They morphologically separate tenses: past/present/future; and thus conceive of time primarily as the passage from one time to another... [...]. The Chinese language, however, does not conjugate: it has possible markers for the past or the near future, but it does not distinguish between specified tenses by conjugation. Its basic representation in this respect is actually two terms rather than three - always polarity - which I'll translate most literally: 'going away: past/present: coming' (wang gu jin lai). The past never stops going / the present never stops coming. Back to transition. 
Chinese thought has not had to turn time into a 'question' by bypassing these (European) approaches to the question of time. Instead, it has examined the processes of things and behaviors, both in their tendency and in their mode of use (generically called tao in Chinese), from the smallest to the largest scale - the duration of a life or a world - to test their coherence. But it has not, despite all this, posited 'time' as that which envelops them all and, detached from both their duration and their simultaneity, constitutes its own entity to serve as an a priori framework for our perception of change (or a priori form of our sensibility, according to Kant)".
(François Jullien, The Silent Transformations, 2009)

E1.5.E.3.1_REF1.1_PH FRANÇOIS JULLIEN SUGGESTS THAT THE WESTERN CONSTRUCTION OF TIME AS A TOTAL SUBJECT IS DUE TO AN INABILITY TO RECOGNIZE THE SILENT TRANSFORMATIONS OBSERVED IN CHINESE THOUGHT.
"When the logos of philosophy prevails, it can only inherit time, hypostasized as an autonomous instance that dominates beings. And even, more precisely, more sovereignly, the 'being' of beings; and this is true even of the least mythological of Greek thinkers, Aristotle. After having analytically conceived of time in terms of its measuring function, in relation to number, his thinking suddenly shifts to the point of considering that for all things 'to be in time' means to be measured by time, not only in their movement, but also in their 'being'. But why this shift? Why must time, from being the measure of movement, become that 'by which' the 'existence of things' in general is measured, if not because the analysis of physical time, even in Aristotle, is overwhelmed by this cultural representation of a tutelary time that recaptures, in this abstract mode, the omnipotence of religion? [...]
Why, then, has European thought, which throughout its history has tried so hard to contradict and criticize itself, which has never stopped, in other words, to disengage from itself, never thought to get out of this rut of "time", which is buried in language, but which, unlike China, is so singular and culturally marked? "Time", trapped in the metaphysical opposition of being and becoming, and in its globally explanatory role as the omnipotent subject serving as the ultimate avatar of divinity [...]
Is it not the case, then, that we have set up time as a total subject, easily assignable and therefore conveniently invocable, because, failing to give sufficient status to the silent transformations, we had to invoke a great agent responsible for both the emergence of things in the visible and their invisible resorption?
(François Jullien, The Silent Transformations, 2009)

E1.5.E.3.2. THE DOMINATION OF TIME BEGINS WITH PLATO
It was Platonism that froze categories. For Heraclitus, everything was becoming, and even for Parmenides, everything was mixed. 

E1.5.E.3.2_REF1_PH PARMENIDES CRITICIZES HUMANITY FOR FAILING TO SEE THE UNITY IN OPPOSITES. Language isolates opposites, but sensitive experience shows that they are always intermingled.
"Mortals would have erred had they not allowed one form to exist without the other, and thus seen the connection and unity of the two. This unity, on the other hand, is what Parmenides accuses them of failing to see because they "set the signs apart". What Parmenides wants to say, Simplicius observes, is that "those who do not see unity in the opposition of the elements that cause generation are mistaken" [...].
Mortals have named two forms, and they cannot be proved wrong: they are fire and light on the one hand, and night on the other. There is fire and light; there is night. But what mortals have separated, the two Forms they have distinguished 'according to body,' they have failed to unite. And yet, without unification, there can be no generation; mortals, at least non-philosophical mortals, are incapable of understanding the genesis of the world. There is light and there is night, that's all. Now everything that is - is the result of becoming. 
Philosophers have shown this. They first separate the opposites, but they do not leave them in their separateness. They see 'unity in opposition' and thus create realities out of opposites. At the origin of the world, we have Anaximander's opposition of hot and cold, Anaximenes' opposition of the 'dense', which corresponds to 'cold', and the 'rare' or 'relaxed', which is hot, with the Pythagoreans, for whom everything is number, that of the 'finite' and the 'unlimited', elements of number: so that for them, as for the Mimesians, 'opposites are the principles of being'. As Heraclitus explains, nature, the φύσις [fýsis], works by uniting opposites, but not without a rule, but 'always respecting a law of proportion, measure and balance'. In this way, says Philolaus, following Heraclitus, 'harmony is born of the opposition of opposites' - harmony without which there would be no stability for the world. Such harmony, he adds, is defined rather as the 'unification' of a 'mixture of diverse elements.' It is such a unity that ordinary people fail to perceive, confining themselves to seeing opposites juxtaposed, which we would have to see as one in order to grasp the world in its genesis and not simply as a result.
The fundamental opposition out of which the world must be created is that of fire and night. For 'night' means darkness, fire that gives light rather than heat. [...]
For the ordinary man - and the philosopher is first and foremost an ordinary man - there is chiaroscuro between light and darkness, between day and night twilight, between cold and hot lukewarm, between light and heavy many degrees. The 'ethereal fire of the flame' is said to be 'the same everywhere as itself' and night is 'without clarity': light is only light and night is only night. Now, in sensible experience, a flame is more or less luminous, which implies, within clarity, the presence of its opposite; and night is never quite black, this blackness being softened only by the 'obscure clarity that falls from the stars.' How is it, then, that each of these opposites is posited as exclusive of the other, when sensible experience is that of a gradual passage from one to the other? Is such a logicalization the work of the philosopher, who isolates entities before thinking of them together in a controlled union? Not at all. Such an operation is inherent in the mortal naming of forms: for as soon as I say 'light,' I don't say 'darkness,' and as soon as I say 'night,' I don't say 'clarity'-all nuance has disappeared. Sensible night is never without some "obscure clarity," but named night, reduced to its concept, is only 'night' and nothing more. If we are to form and represent the mortal world, we must begin with the words that mortals use. But words separate what does not exist separately in the sensory world. The word "light" distinguishes light from night; similarly, words distinguish "rare" from "dense," "cold" from 'hot,' 'light' from 'heavy'. But in the sensible, light cannot exist without shadow, rarefaction without concentration and therefore rare without dense, heat without warming or cooling and therefore hot without cold, and so on. In short, sensible becoming mixes and combines entities that words had set apart. [...]

FRAGMENT 9 [...].

But since all things have been called light and night,
which have been given names according to their own powers
one or the other, all things are at the same time full of light 
of light and of night without light, both equally, 
for there is nothing that has no part in either. [...]"
(Parmenides, The Poem: Fragments, lecture and commentary by Marcel Conche, 1996)

E1.5.E.4. CONTEMPLATION VS. ACTION: 
A question of temperament or life experience?

E1.5.E.4_REF1_PH "AT NOON. - He who has been granted an active and storm-filled morning of life is overcome at the noontide of life by a strange longing for repose that can last for months or years. It grows still around him, voices recede into the distance; the sun shines down on him from high overhead. Upon a concealed woodland meadow he sees great Pan sleeping; all things of nature have fallen asleep with him, an expression of eternity on their face - that is how it seems to him. He wants nothing, he is troubled by noth- ing, his heart stands still, only his eyes are alive - it is a death with open eyes. Then the man sees many things he never saw before, and for as far as he can see everything is enmeshed in a net of light and as it were buried in it. He feels happy as he gazes, but it is a heavy, heavy happi- ness. - Then at length the wind rises in the trees, noon has gone by, life again draws him to it, life with unseeing eyes, its train of followers sweeping along behind it: desire, deception, forgetfulness, destruction, transience. And thus evening rises up, more active and more storm-filled even than the morning. - To truly active men the more long enduring states of knowledge seem almost uncanny and morbid, but not unpleasant."
(Friedrich Nietzsche, Human, All Too Humain II, Aphorism 308, 1878)

E1.5.E.5. IS BEING CONTEMPLATIVE NECESSARILY BEING SELFISH?
And what's the difference between being selfish and "egotistical"?

E1.5.E.5_REF1_PH "IN THE MIRROR OF NATURE. - Has a man not been fairly exactly described when one hears that he likes to wander between fields of tall yellow corn, that he prefers the colors of the woods and flowers in glowing and golden autumn because they intimate greater beauty than ever nature achieved, that he feels quite at home among great thick-leaved nut-trees as though among his blood relations, that in the mountains his greatest joy is to encounter those remote little lakes out of which solitude itself seems to gaze up at him, that he loves that gray reposefulness of twilight mist that creeps up to the windows on autumn and early winter evenings and shuts out every soulless sound as though with velvet curtains, that he feels the unhewn rocks to be unwillingly mute witnesses to times primeval and has revered them from a child, and finally that the sea, with its rippling snake-skin and beast-of-prey beauty, is and remains alien to him? - Yes, something of this man has therewith been described, to be sure, but the mirror of nature says nothing of the fact that the same man, with all his idyllic sensibility (and not even 'in spite of it'), could be somewhat loveless, niggardly and conceited. Horace, who understood such things as this, placed the tenderest feeling for country life in the mouth and soul of a Roman usurer, in the celebrated 'beatus ille qui procul negotiis'." (1)
(Friedrich Nietzsche, Human, All Too Humain II, Aphorisme 49, 1878)

Note:
(1) Beatus ille qui procul negotiis, quote from the Roman poet Horace, Epodes, 2, 1, meaning “Blessed is he who stays out of trouble."

E1.5.E.5_REF2_LIT EGOISM VS. EGOTISM "Egoism, in the new psychological sense of the word, may be regarded as the deliberate attempt in an individual's life to throw the chief interest and emphasis of his days upon the inward, personal, subjective impressions produced by the world, rather than upon outward action or social progress. Egoism does not necessarily imply the invidious stigma of selfishness. Goethe, the greatest of all egoists, was notoriously free from such a vice. « Who, » cried Wieland, when they first met at Weimar, « who can resist the unselfishness of this man? »
Egoism does not necessarily imply « egotism » [...] Egoism is an intellectual gesture, a spiritual attitude, a temperamental atmosphere. It is a thing which implies a certain definite philosophical mood in regard to the riddle of existence"
(John Cowper Powys, Suspended Judgments, 1916)

E1.5.E.5_REF3_LIT "IN THE FIRST PLACE WHAT IS THE DEEPEST, SECRETEST PURPOSE OF MY LIFE? I can answer categorically: “To enjoy the sensations that I like enjoying, when I am most entirely and shamelessly myself.” Now I know, not only from looking in the looking-glass when I shave, but from certain realistic photographs, that there is an unmistakable ninny-look or zany-look in my face. This look answers to what I feel, and to what I let myself feel, and to what I maliciously encourage myself to feel! And why not? Why should I devote myself to feeling and expressing only noble, monumental and dignified sentiments?"
(John Cowper Powys, Autobiography, 1934)

E1.5.E.5_REF4_LIT "BY SLOW DEGREES IT HAS MADE ITSELF MANIFEST TO ME THAT THE PURPOSE OF MY LIFE was to dodge — when I could get leave of absence from my exacting conscience — all obligations to humanity, and to cultivate certain totally useless, purposeless, unprofitable feelings. These feelings, which to me have come to be precious beyond any power of description, are regarded by many as pathetically colourless and sapless, by others as a kind of wilful insanity, by a few as a damnable affectation! “
(John Cowper Powys, Autobiography, 1934)

E1.5.F. THERAPY AS A PATH FROM UNHAPPINESS
Does therapy aim to transition us from a state of discomfort to a more bearable reality? This process may risk revealing the truth of our desires, which could be overwhelming...

E1.5.F.1. "REMEDY 
[...] As for the health of the soul, it now has its own therapists, who are not philosophers but psychologists or psychiatrists. That's for the better. Health is the ordinary state of life. Wisdom is not. 
Psychoanalysis," said Freud, "is not about making us happy. It's about shifting from neurotic suffering to banal unhappiness. A considerable advance, which makes psychoanalysis, when it succeeds, what it claims to be: a cure for neurosis. In other words, banal or normal unhappiness is not, or no longer, a matter for psychoanalysis. It's a matter for action. It's a matter for philosophy, which begins where psychotherapy ends (because we're more or less cured) or isn't necessary (because we're more or less healthy). There's no need for remedies: reason and willpower are enough, which are not there to cure, but to understand and act."
(André Comte-Sponville, Philosophical Dictionary, 2013)

E1.5.F.1_REF1_SCI WHAT HAPPENS IN THE BRAIN TO CAUSE DEPRESSION?
Drugs that target the neurotransmitter serotonin have long been prescribed to treat depression. Now the spotlight is turning to other aspects of brain chemistry.
(John Krystal speaks with Steven Strogatz, The Joy of Why, Quantamagazine, May 23,2024)

Listen to the Podcast or read the full transcript of the interview:
https://www.quantamagazine.org/what-happens-in-the-brain-to-cause-depression-20240523/?mc_cid=89594a7485&mc_eid=de38e9b771

E1.5.F.1_REF2_SCI LISA FELDMAN BARRETT REDEFINES DEPRESSION NOT AS A DISORDER OF THOUGHT, BUT AS A DISORDER OF PREDICTION WHERE THE BRAIN BECOMES LOCKED IN A SELF-PERPETUATING CYCLE OF MISMANAGING THE BODY BUDGET BASED ON PAST EXPERIENCES AND THEN FAILING TO CORRECT ITSELF WITH INPUT FROM THE PRESENT.
“Depression is  —  you guessed it —  a concept. It is a population of diverse instances, so there are many degenerate paths to depression, many of which begin with an imbalanced body budget. If depression is a disorder of affect, and affect is an integrated summary of how your body budget is doing (answer: pretty poorly), then depression may actually be a disorder of misbudgeting and prediction.
We know that your brain continually predicts your body’s energy needs based on past experience. Under normal circumstances, your brain also corrects its predictions based on actual sensory information from your body. But what if this correction wasn’t working properly? Your momentary experience would be constructed from the past but not corrected by the present. In general terms, that’s what I think is happening in depression. Your brain is continually mispredicting your metabolic needs. Your body and brain therefore act as if you were fighting off an infection or healing from a wound when none exists, as in chronic stress or pain. As a result, your affect is out of whack: you experience debilitating misery, fatigue, or other symp-toms of depression. Simultaneously, your body is quickly metabolizing un-necessary glucose to meet those high yet nonexistent energy needs, leading to weight problems and leaving you at risk for other metabolic-related ill-nesses that co-occur with depression, including diabetes, heart disease, and cancer.
The traditional view of depression is that negative thoughts cause nega-tive feelings. I’m suggesting it’s the other way around. Your feelings right now drive your next thought, as well as your perceptions, as predictions. So a depressed brain relentlessly keeps making withdrawals from the bud-get, basing its predictions on similar withdrawals from the past. This means constantly reliving difficult, unpleasant events. You wind up in a cycle of budgeting imbalances, unbroken by prediction error because it is ignored, gets tuned down, or doesn’t make it to the brain. In effect, you’re locked into a cycle of uncorrected predictions, trapped in an adverse past when your metabolic needs were high.
A depressed brain is effectively locked into misery. It’s like a brain in chronic pain, ignoring prediction error, but on a much larger scale that shuts you down. It puts your budget chronically in debt, so your brain tries to cut spending. What’s the most efficient way to do that? Stop moving and don’t pay attention to the world (prediction error). That is the unrelenting fatigue of depression.”
(Lisa Feldman Barrett, How Emotions Are Made - The Secret Life of the Brain, 2017)

E1.5.F.2. SIGNS OF DEPRESSION

E1.5.F.2_REF1_PH "A MASTER PAINTING WHERE HUMAN DESPAIR INVADES LIFE and a branch of grapes becomes a pistol" (Mineta House)
"Depression is distinguished from unhappiness by its causes, which are psychological or morbid: it is a kind of endogenous and pathological grief. Depression can be treated; unhappiness can be fought. This does not mean that there are not, between the two, all sorts of intermediate degrees and reciprocal actions (depression makes one unhappy, unhappiness depresses one), which may justify prescribing antidepressants, sometimes, to someone who is simply unhappy not because his suffering would be pathological, but to avoid, perhaps, that it becomes pathogenic."
(André Comte-Sponville, Philosophical Dictionary, 2013)

E1.5.F.2_REF2_CUR "THE "BOX" THAT ENCLOSES EGON SCHIELE AND FRANCIS BACON'S CHARACTERS in their paintings touches me deeply and reminds me of how lucky I am to be little subject to the existential doubt that tortured these two great artists - « executioners of themselves » (as Baudelaire would have said) all their lives. Even in the mountains, nested in nature, there is no possible escape by dissolution in the landscape - the stranglehold continues to oppress them (see the imaginary lines around the hut in Schiele's 'Landscape with Crows' or those around the head of the character in Bacon's 'Character in a mountain landscape'). One would have to be as free as the grains of sand in a sand dune (see Bacon's 'Sand Dune') not to be subject to this evil and to succeed in « pulling oneself together » (at Schiele seems trying to do in 'Male nude propping himself up'), when one is born with the genes and has led the life of these two artists."
(Pierre Schindler)

E1.5.F.2_REF3_CUR SELF-EXECUTIONER "Since photography, painting has changed drastically. Now to do what is called realism, you have to distort the images because we are now so stultified by images, by everything we see on posters, movies and television. So you have to make an image that - we can use the word - that shocks you. The purpose is not to shock, but to open the roads through which what I call realism can be expressed." (Francis Bacon)
What fascinates Bacon is this torsion that he imposes on images, he often paints from photos like this one from Eadweard Muybridge that he distorts for our surprise.
But isn't it also a violence that Bacon inflicts on himself? He was often angry with himself. Was he a « self-executioner » as Baudelaire puts it in the poem L'Héautontimorouménos:
« Am I not a false chord
In the divine symphony,
Due to the voracious irony
That shakes and devores me? [...]
I am the wound and the knife!
I am the slap and the cheek,
I am the limbs and the wheel,
The victim and the executioner! [...] »
This violence seems to reside in his gaze rather than in reality -  in his psyche marked by a hard life of rejections (the non-recognition of his sexual preference by his family for example)."
(Pierre Schindler)

1.5.F.2_REF12_LIT YOU CANNOT RUN AWAY FROM YOURSELF

“Hello, Robert,” I said. “Did you come in to cheer me up?”

“Would you like to go to South America, Jake?” he asked.

“No.”

“Why not?”

“I don’t know. I never wanted to go. Too expensive. You can see all the
South Americans you want in Paris anyway.”

“They’re not the real South Americans.”

“They look awfully real to me.”

I had a boat train to catch with a week’s mail stories, and only half of
them written.

“Do you know any dirt?” I asked.

“No.”

“None of your exalted connections getting divorces?”

“No; listen, Jake. If I handled both our expenses, would you go to South
America with me?”

“Why me?”

“You can talk Spanish. And it would be more fun with two of us.”

“No,” I said, “I like this town and I go to Spain in the summer-time.”

“All my life I’ve wanted to go on a trip like that,” Cohn said. He sat
down. “I’ll be too old before I can ever do it.”

“Don’t be a fool,” I said. “You can go anywhere you want. You’ve got
plenty of money.”

“I know. But I can’t get started.”

“Cheer up,” I said. “All countries look just like the moving pictures.”

But I felt sorry for him. He had it badly.

“I can’t stand it to think my life is going so fast and I’m not really
living it.”

“Nobody ever lives their life all the way up except bull-fighters.”

“I’m not interested in bull-fighters. That’s an abnormal life. I want to
go back in the country in South America. We could have a great trip.”

“Did you ever think about going to British East Africa to shoot?”

“No, I wouldn’t like that.”

“I’d go there with you.”

“No; that doesn’t interest me.”

“That’s because you never read a book about it. Go on and read a book
all full of love affairs with the beautiful shiny black princesses.”

“I want to go to South America.”

He had a hard, Jewish, stubborn streak.

“Come on down-stairs and have a drink.”

“Aren’t you working?”

“No,” I said. We went down the stairs to the café on the ground floor. I
had discovered that was the best way to get rid of friends. Once you had
a drink all you had to say was: “Well, I’ve got to get back and get off
some cables,” and it was done. It is very important to discover graceful
exits like that in the newspaper business, where it is such an important
part of the ethics that you should never seem to be working. Anyway, we
went down-stairs to the bar and had a whiskey and soda. Cohn looked at
the bottles in bins around the wall. “This is a good place,” he said.

“There’s a lot of liquor,” I agreed.

“Listen, Jake,” he leaned forward on the bar. “Don’t you ever get the
feeling that all your life is going by and you’re not taking advantage
of it? Do you realize you’ve lived nearly half the time you have to live
already?”

“Yes, every once in a while.”

“Do you know that in about thirty-five years more we’ll be dead?”

“What the hell, Robert,” I said. “What the hell.”

“I’m serious.”

“It’s one thing I don’t worry about,” I said.

“You ought to.”

“I’ve had plenty to worry about one time or other. I’m through
worrying.”

“Well, I want to go to South America.”

“Listen, Robert, going to another country doesn’t make any difference.
I’ve tried all that. You can’t get away from yourself by moving from one
place to another. There’s nothing to that.”

“But you’ve never been to South America.”

“South America hell! If you went there the way you feel now it would be
exactly the same. This is a good town. Why don’t you start living your
life in Paris?”

“I’m sick of Paris, and I’m sick of the Quarter.”

“Stay away from the Quarter. Cruise around by yourself and see what
happens to you.”

“Nothing happens to me. I walked alone all one night and nothing
happened except a bicycle cop stopped me and asked to see my papers.”

“Wasn’t the town nice at night?”

“I don’t care for Paris.”

So there you were. I was sorry for him, but it was not a thing you could
do anything about, because right away you ran up against the two
stubbornnesses: South America could fix it and he did not like Paris. He
got the first idea out of a book, and I suppose the second came out of a
book too.”
(Ernest Hemingway, The Sun Also Rises, 1926)

E1.5.F.3. PSYCHOANALYSIS IS NOT A RECIPE FOR TRANQUILLITY
"Psychoanalysis is not what a vain people think, a recipe for quietude or well-being, but a restless and perilous negotiation, the diplomacy of the balance of terror, which gropingly adjusts the tragic, fragile and ever-threatening balance of the human and the inhuman, the conscious and the unconscious, the Symbolic and the Real."

“Psychotherapy [can] give great gifts to tortured and overwhelmed people and even added dignity to anyone who values and can use self-knowledge. Psychotherapy can allow people to affirm themselves, to smash idols that constrict the self-esteem, to lift the load of neurotic guilt—the extra guilt piled on top of natural existential guilt. It can clear away neurotic despair—the despair that comes from a too-constricted focus for one’s safety and satisfactions. When a person becomes less fragmented, less blocked and bottled up, he does experience real joy: the joy of finding more of himself, of the release from armor and binding reflexes, of throwing off the chains of uncritical and self-defeating dependency, of controlling his own energies, of discovering aspects of the world, intense experience in the present moment that is now freer of prefixed perceptions, new possibilities of choice and action, and so on. Yes, psychotherapy can do all these things, but there are many things it cannot do, and they have not been aired widely enough. Often psychotherapy seems to promise the moon: a more constant joy, delight, celebration of life, perfect love, and perfect freedom. It seems to promise that these things are easy to come by, once self-knowledge is achieved, that they are things that should and could characterize one’s whole waking awareness. As one patient said, who had just undergone a course in “primal scream” therapy: “I feel so fantastic and wonderful, but this is only a beginning—wait till you see me in five years, it’ll be tremendous!” We can only hope that she won’t be too unhappy. Not everyone is as honest as Freud was when he said that he cured the miseries of the neurotic only to open him up to the normal misery of life. Only angels know unrelieved joy—or are able to stand it. Yet we see the books by the mind-healers with their garish titles: “Joy!” “Awakening,” and the like; we see them in person in lecture halls or in groups, beaming their peculiar brand of inward, confident well-being, so that it communicates its unmistakable message: we can do this for you, too, if you will only let us. I have never seen or heard them communicate the dangers of the total liberation that they claim to offer; say, to put up a small sign next to the one advertising joy, carrying some inscription like “Danger: real probability of the awakening of terror and dread, from which there is no turning back.” It would be honest and would also relieve them of some of the guilt of the occasional suicide that takes place in therapy.”
(Ernest Becker, The Denial of Death, 1973)

E1.5.F.3.1. THE UNNAMEABLE AND THE DOUBLE THREATEN OUR KINGDOM
Just as there are two kingdoms at the center of which the subject constructs and affirms itself, so there will be two realities that pose a muted threat at their borders: at the limit of the Symbolic, the Thing (das Ding) is erected, which words fail to name, the unnameable that our discourse always misses; at the limit of the Imaginary, the Double appears, in which the subject loses himself without recognizing himself, like Narcissus drowning in his reflection. The Thing and the Double are the two steles that stand at the border of our two worlds. 

E1.5.F.3.1_REF1_PH ACCORDING TO LACAN, PSYCHOANALYSIS IS A DANGEROUS NEGOTIATION BETWEEN THE CONSCIOUS AND THE UNCONSCIOUS. At the borders of the symbolic and the imaginary, two realities threaten: the unnameable thing and the double in which the subject loses itself.
"Lacan clearly understood that Greek tragedy is based first and foremost on a topology, a rigorous and unchanging structure of space: two "antigonic", even more than antagonistic, realms, between which the balance can only be one of terror: that of the human world, on the one hand, where Logos reigns, that word of which the free and conscious subject is the sole master, and on the other, the sacred realm, where the gods reign, imperiously demanding that mortals pay their dues through the gift of sacrifice. In order for the human world to be protected from the threat of the inhuman or superhuman that always lurks on the sacred side, mortals must ensure that the rites are scrupulously performed. The slightest error, the slightest blunder, would upset the balance and sweep it away in a tragic earthquake. [Lacan is fascinated by the heroism of Antigone, who risks the limit - the threshold of madness - to put an end to tragic destabilization and restore the peace of sharing. Psychoanalysis is not, as the vain think, a recipe for tranquility or well-being, but an anxious and dangerous negotiation, the diplomacy of the balance of terror, which gropingly adjusts the fragile and ever threatening tragic equilibrium between the human and the inhuman, the conscious and the unconscious, the symbolic and the real. [...]
Just as there are two realms at the center of which the subject constructs and affirms itself, so there will be two realms that pose a muted threat at their borders: at the limit of the symbolic, there is the thing that words cannot name, the unnameable that our discourses always miss; at the limit of the imaginary, there is the double, in which the subject loses itself without recognizing itself, like Narcissus drowning in his reflection. The Thing and the Double are the two steles that stand at the border between worlds. Beyond this, every object tends to dissolve into the indeterminate, whether it has been posited in the symbolic by the act of definition, inserted into the network of signifiers, or manifested in the imaginary by the encounter with a stranger who is another myself, from nowhere, forever unique".
(Jacques Darriulat, Lacan, Listen to Madness, III- The Real, 2019)

Read the essay in full: http://www.jdarriulat.net/Auteurs/Lacan/Ecouterlafolie/Ecouterlafolie3.html

E1.5.F.3.1.a. REALITY IS THE SYMBOLIC SYSTEM IN WHICH WE TRY TO IMPRISON THE REAL
"What is Real is not reality, in the sense that what we call reality is a symbolic system made up of values and rituals that structure a community and govern discourse and laws. Reality is ordered; only the Real is pure presence. [...] Reality is thus the symbolic system in which we strive to imprison the Real, which will nevertheless always find the opportunity to show itself through the gaps in our discourse, where words fail us. [...]
The symbolic system represses a world - that of the Real - and defines another - that of reality - in which it is possible for man to live and exercise his control."
(Jacques Darriulat, Lacan, Listen to Madness, III - The Real, 2019)

Read the essay in full: http://www.jdarriulat.net/Auteurs/Lacan/Ecouterlafolie/Ecouterlafolie3.html

E1.5.F.3.1.a_REF1_PH ACCORDING TO LACAN, THE REAL IS A PURE PRESENCE THAT ESCAPES THE SYMBOLIC STRUCTURES OF A REALITY ORDERED BY VALUES AND RITUALS. Language, while essential to the structuring of Reality, cannot fully capture the Real, which manifests itself in the gaps and ambiguities of discourse. The power of language allows us to master the symbolic world, but the real always remains partially out of reach, appearing in dreams, fantasies, and hallucinations.
"Let us begin, then, with the Real that besieges the symbolic world, before moving on to encounter the other Real, at the point where the imaginary comes to a standstill, transfixed by the encounter with the double. As Lacan repeatedly emphasizes, the Real is not reality in the sense that what we call reality is a symbolic system of values and rituals that structure a community and govern discourse and laws. Reality is ordered; only the Real is pure presence. When parents tell their children that they must have a 'sense of reality,' they simply mean that they must obey the laws and customs of their country. But in the presence of the Real, there is no longer any discourse that holds, and the subject is reduced to silence. But there is no language that does not, through its slips of the tongue, its denials, its ambiguities, its silences, like a fabric through its holes or tears, hint at the Real that its web seeks to suppress and master. Reality, then, is the symbolic system in which we try to imprison the Real, which will nevertheless always find the opportunity to show itself through the gaps in our discourse, where words fail us. We can know everything about the objects of experience, except for the act of presence that makes them real and gives them the power of the Real. [...] What escapes our experience of reality is precisely what is obscured by the fact that we have put the Real under the yoke or 'halter' of the signifier. For words do not leave the things they designate untouched, and, as magic has always anticipated, they cast a spell over them, appropriating them symbolically. [...]
'The trap,' says Lacan, 'the hole we must not fall into, is to believe that the signified are objects, things. The signified is something else entirely - it's signification, which I've explained [...] always refers back to another signification. The system of language, no matter how well you understand it, never results in an index directly directed to a point of reality; it is the whole of reality that is covered by the whole network of language'. By giving the creature made in his image the names of the animals, God enables Adam to overcome the fear of the real, to evade its threat, and gives him all power over them. It was the power of language, of thought and its cunning, and not sheer force, that enabled man to cage the lions. It was the power of the signifier, Lacan teaches in his first seminar, that enabled the first hunters to capture the mammoth, the most formidable of the pachyderms. Nothing was more dangerous and nothing less useful, since the meat of the beast, which could not be preserved, was destined to rot on the spot. By killing his prey, the hunter experiments with and verifies the omnipotence of language. [...] The symbolic system suppresses one world - that of the real - and defines another - that of reality - in which it is possible for man to live and exercise his mastery. [...]
Lacan's designation of the real with this substantive adjective is a strange one, closer to what everyday language calls the unreal than to reality, which we also know to be an effect of language. The real is neither the external world, nor the universe, nor the field of experience, all of which are invested by the discipline of language. The Thing, this emissary of the Real, is beyond meaning, it tears at the fragile fabric of words and appears only on the stage of dreams, for example, or through fantasy or hallucination. [...]"
(Jacques Darriulat, Lacan, Listen to Madness, III - The Real, 2019)

Read the essay in full: http://www.jdarriulat.net/Auteurs/Lacan/Ecouterlafolie/Ecouterlafolie3.html

E1.5.F.3.1.a_REF1.1_PH "IF THE CARAVAGO FRUIT BIN IS SO SUPERBLY PRESENT, it's because the fruit is unreal, swollen with juice, standing out fantastically against the luminous background, seeming to detach itself from the wall and fall into our outstretched hand. It's not imitation that gives us a sense of presence, but hallucination, insofar as it expresses the tension of desire. And for the trap to be even more effective, a detail must stand out, like the fly on the coquette's cheek or the wormhole on the shining apple in the Ambrosian basket. Hallucination brings the eye into the painting and places the viewer under a meditative gaze. [...] The real is not descriptive, it is hallucinatory and metonymic. And there is no more powerful affirmation of the Real than the appearance of the thing where words are lacking, in the hollowness of the unnamed".
(Jacques Darriulat, Lacan, Listen to Madness, III - The Real, 2019)

Read the essay in full: http://www.jdarriulat.net/Auteurs/Lacan/Ecouterlafolie/Ecouterlafolie3.html

E1.5.F.3.1.b. THE FASCINATION OF THE DOUBLE: WHEN THE IMAGINARY HYPNOTIZES US
"What now of the Real in the other dimension that gives rise to it, no longer at the limit of the Symbolic, where words are lacking to express it, but at the limit of the Imaginary, where the Double becomes a hypnotist and Narcissus drowns in the water of his reflection?  If the child constructs the schema of his own body through the image that the mirror reveals to him, it could be that the same child loses himself and forgets himself - another form of aphanisis, of the fainting of the subject transfixed by the appearance of a great Other - fascinated by the imaginary creature, buried in the water of the mirror, watching him from the other world. [...]
This specular inversion, which turns the reflection into a real being and the subject into a virtual creature, is based on the original alienation of the little human, whose prematurity renders him incapable of autonomy, and who must find or lose himself in an Other before finding himself. [...]
In disappearing, the object opens a gaping chasm in the subject, its "shadow", as Freud famously put it, "falls on the ego", its "shadow", that is, the outline of an absence, the present trace of a past presence.  [...]
In a world without consistency, where all presence is abolished by the feeling of absence, there is no longer any action that is worthwhile, being or not being are confused, and dying is better than living. [...]
It seems that, for Lacan, once the mirror of fantasy has been shattered, all that remains of being is incomprehensible chaos.
(Jacques Darriulat, Lacan, Listen to Madness, III - The Real, 2019)

Read the essay in full: http://www.jdarriulat.net/Auteurs/Lacan/Ecouterlafolie/Ecouterlafolie3.html

E1.5.F.3.1.b_REF1_PH LACAN EXAMINES THE ALIENATION OF THE SUBJECT, THE MECHANISM OF TRANSFERENCE, AND THE PHANTASM OF THE DOUBLE. He describes a world in which existence dissolves into the infra-real, a zone of indeterminacy in which the real dissolves and the subject is confronted with a profound nihilism and horror of the human condition.
"What then of the Real in the other dimension that produces it, no longer at the limit of the Symbolic, where words fail to express it, but at the limit of the Imaginary, where the Double becomes the hypnotist and Narcissus drowns in the water of his reflection? If the child constructs the schema of its own body through the image that the mirror reveals to it, it could be that the same child loses itself and forgets itself - another form of aphanisis, the fainting of the subject transfixed by the appearance of a great Other - fascinated by the imaginary creature buried in the water of the mirror, watching it from the other world. [...]
This mirroring inversion, which turns the reflection into a real being and the subject into a virtual creature, is based on the original alienation of the infant, whose prematurity renders him incapable of autonomy and who must find or lose himself in an Other before he can find himself. The mechanism of transference, to which we'll return when we question the figure of the master, is decisive in the fantasy of the double. [...]
By disappearing, the object opens a yawning chasm in the subject; its 'shadow,' as Freud famously put it, 'falls on the self,' its 'shadow,' that is, the outline of an absence, the present trace of a past presence. The ideal ego that the neurotic subject hallucinates in the formidable image of the Great Other is followed by a melancholic depression that plunges the subject into the nothingness that lies at the heart of himself and makes him experience the living pain, not of dying, but of living as a dead person. Here begins the land at the end of the world, barren and deserted, shrouded in mist, where form dissolves into formlessness, where existence liquefies into empty time, where being drowns in nothingness. To describe this beyond of the Imaginary and the Symbolic, which is more than real and less than being, we need to invent a new category: the Infra-real. Lacan often ventures into this zone of indeterminacy, but he never names it. Hamlet, prince of melancholy, reigns over this realm of limits, overwhelmed by the double grief of a murdered father and a defiled mother. [...] The story of this dark, orphaned, inconsolable prince is a slow suicide in a setting of ruins that desire has abandoned: Ophelia, the object of his love, is a chimera disintegrating in the waters of the river, the murdered father a groaning avenger not worth the price of revenge, the desecrated mother a slut abandoned to the usurper, the throne a shadow theater where all is pretense. In a world without consistency, where all presence is abolished by the feeling of absence, there is no longer any action worth taking, being and not being are confused, and dying is better than living. In Hamlet, the totem of the Nom-du-Père and the fetish of the phallic mother, which had taken the place of reality, dissolve into hollow dreams and vain phantasmagorias: 'The meaning of what Hamlet learns from this father is there, before us, very clear. It is the irreparable, absolute, unfathomable betrayal of love - of the purest love, the love of this king, who, of course, like all men, may have been a great scoundrel, but who, with this being who was his wife, went so far as to turn the winds of heaven from his face - at least according to what Hamlet says. This is the absolute falsehood of what seemed to Hamlet to be the very testimony of beauty, truth, and essence. Therein lies the answer. Hamlet's truth is a hopeless truth. There is no trace in Hamlet of elevation to something beyond, of salvation or redemption. [...] For Lacan, Hamlet is the brother of the old, blind Oedipus, whom the young Antigone leads to Colone at the end of his exile: "It is better never to be born (mê phunai),' laments the chorus, 'and if we must see the light of day, it is still better to return as quickly as possible to whence we came'. This lament recurs like a leitmotif in Lacan's meditations, not as the death that frightens the living, who live only so as not to die, but as that 'second death' which is the sinister temptation of nothingness, the swampy lands of the Infra-real, where grief and melancholy slowly sink. [...] In this 'nothingness of existence,' this taedium vitae that is as much self-hatred as it is hatred of life, this despair that does not even have the energy to commit suicide, the spectrum that made up the subject's identity is terribly decomposed by the superimposition of successive identifications, like so many masks that fall and, as they fall, reveal the profound misery and radical impoverishment that are the hallmark of the human beast. The image of the body itself dissolves beyond the Real, in the Infra-real, where nothing has any consistency, in front of a mirror that is nothing but darkness, leaving room for that which no longer has a name in any language: a decomposing corpse. [...] Bossuet: 'What good will it do you to have written so much in this book, to have filled all its pages with beautiful characters, when in the end a single erasure must wipe it all out? Even an erasure would at least leave a few traces of itself; instead, this last moment, which will erase your whole life in a single stroke, will be lost, along with everything else, in the great abyss of nothingness. There will be no trace left on earth of what we are: the flesh will change its nature; the body will have another name; even that of the corpse will not last long: it will become, says Tertullian, a je ne sais quoi that has no name in any language'. In the Infra-real, the Real dissolves like a dream or a phantom, and all existence dissolves into the uncertainty of the formless or the vanity of the fickle. There is a profound nihilism in Lacan, a horror of the human condition that is undoubtedly the flip side of the unconditionality of desire that lies at the heart of the ethics of psychoanalysis [...] It seems that for Lacan, once the mirror of fantasy has been shattered, all that remains of being is an incomprehensible chaos.
(Jacques Darriulat, Lacan, Listen to Madness, III - The Real, 2019)

Read the essay in full: http://www.jdarriulat.net/Auteurs/Lacan/Ecouterlafolie/Ecouterlafolie3.html

E1.5.F.3.1.b_REF2_PH JACQUES DARRIULAT DISCUSSES LACAN'S IDEAS ON THE INTERPLAY BETWEEN REALITY, THE SYMBOLIC, AND THE IMAGINARY. He explores how the child's self-concept is influenced by the mirror image, leading to potential self-alienation. This specular inversion creates a dynamic where the real and virtual selves are intertwined, rooted in the child's initial dependency and immaturity. Darriulat highlights Freud's notion of the object's shadow impacting the self, resulting in a pervasive sense of absence. Ultimately, he suggests that once the fantasy mirror is shattered, what remains is an incomprehensible chaos.
"What about Reality in the other dimension where it can be found, no longer at the limit of the Symbolic, where words are lacking to evocate it, but at the limit of the Imaginary, where the Double image becomes a hypnotist and where Narcissus drowns in the water of his own reflection?  If the child constructs his own body's schema based on the image revealed to him by the mirror, it can also be the opposite; that the same child loses himself and forgets himself - another form of aphanisis, of the fainting of the subject meditated by the appearance of a great Other - fascinated by the imaginary creature, buried in the water of the mirror, which looks at him from the other world. [...]
This specular inversion, which makes a real being out of the reflection and a virtual creature out of the subject, is based on the original alienation of the little man incapable of autonomy because of his lack of maturity, so that he must find or lose himself in an Other before finding himself. [...]
The object, by eclipsing itself, opens in the subject a gaping chasm, its « shadow », according to a famous formula of Freud, « falls on the self », its « shadow », that is to say the outline of an absence, the present trace of a past presence.  [...]
In a world without consistency, where all presence is abolished by the feeling of  absence, there is no more action which is worth living, to be or not to be are confused and to die is better than to live. [...]
It seems that, for Lacan, once the mirror of fantasy has been broken, all that remains of a being is an incomprehensible chaos."
(Jacques Darriulat, Lacan, Listening to Madness, III - The Real, 2019)
